Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia
תלמוד ירושלמי בבא מציעא
The Jerusalem Talmud, translation and commentary by Heinrich W. Guggenheimer. Berlin, De Gruyter, 1999-2015
https://www.nli.org.il/he/books/NNL_ALEPH001901012/NLI

Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The main application of the Mishnah is to bankruptcy problems where the amount available for distribution is not sufficient to cover all claims. The principle underlying the distribution has been explained at length in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 10:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.10.4.1"><i>Ketubot</i> 10:4, Note 62</a>.</i> If two persons hold on to one toga and each of them says “I found it” [or] “it wholly belongs to me”<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Each of them has to grab a similar length of cloth, to establish equality of claims.</i>, each of them has to swear that he owns no less than half of it and they shall divide it evenly<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">There are no witnesses nor other kinds of proof; the case has to be settled by judicial oath. It is obvious that if the two claimants do not agree to possess the object in common it has to be split evenly. If both of them are ready to swear that the object entirely belongs to him, at least one of them is swearing falsely. Since the formulation of the oath is in the hand of the judges, they must be careful not to teach the parties to swear to a falsehood; they cannot let them swear about full ownership, for the court is warned (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:1" href="/Exodus.23.1"><i>Ex.</i>23:1</a>) “not to lend a hand to a wicked person to be an extortionary witness”, i. e., it is forbidden to administer an oath to a known felon (Mishnah <i>Šebuot</i> 7:4). But if each party only swear to the amount it actually will receive, it is possible that both swear to the truth. The formulation “no less than” implies that the oath is true even if one of them is the full owner; the language of the oaths does not contradict the claims brought before the court.</i>. If one says “it wholly belongs to me” and one says “half of it belongs to me”, the one who says “it wholly belongs to me” shall swear that he owns no less than three parts<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">3/4. The language of “parts” follows the Egyptian (and Roman) pattern; <i>n parts</i> always means <sup class="footnote-marker">n</sup>/<sub>n</sub><sub>+1</sub>.</i> and the one who says “half of it belongs to me” shall swear that he owns no less than a quarter<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <i>Ketubot,</i> the underlying principle is: “Amounts in doubt are split evenly”. If neither party has proof by document or witnesses and A claims 100% of an amount available but B only claims 50%, 50% are assigned to A as undisputed amount. The remaining 50% are in dispute and have to be split evenly; each of the the parties receives 25%.</i>. The first one takes three parts, the second takes a fourth.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If two people hold on to one stole,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel but different text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.1.4"><i>Ketubot</i> 2:1, Notes 12–15</a>.</i> It was stated: One person said to another, give me the mina<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Greek <i>mina</i> of 100 drachmas (denars). <i>Zuz,</i> “half sheqel” is the talmudic name of the denar.</i> which you owe me. He answered, it never happened. He went and brought witnesses that the other owed him 50 <i>zuz.</i> The elder Rebbi Ḥiyya said, the confession of the witnesses is the same as his own confession; he has to swear about the remainder<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text, formulated as R. Ḥiyya’s statement, is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 3a" href="/Bava_Metzia.3a">Babli, 3a</a>. The oath required here is the biblical “oath imposed by the judges”, Mishnah <i>Ševu‘ot</i> 6:1 based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex.</i> 22:8</a>. The rabbinic interpretation of the biblical expression אֲשֶׁר יֹאמַר כִּי הוּא זֶה is “if he [the defendant] agrees that there be a case.” If the defendant in a civil suit, in which there are no witnesses or documents, denies the entire claim, he does not have to swear a biblical oath (he may have to swear a rabbinical oath). But if he agrees to part of the claim, he has to swear a biblical oath to free himself from the remainder. Only if the claim is advanced as tentative, then any admission by the defendant is a gift to the claimant and by rabbinic rule no oath of any kind is due. (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.10.1"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 5:3, Note 84</a>).</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the confession of the witnesses is not the same as his own confession that he should have to swear<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex.</i> 22:8</a> insists on the defendant’s agreeing to part of the claim, witnesses can never force a judicial oath. This opinion is not mentioned in the Babli. In <i>Ketubot</i>(<i>loc. cit.</i> Note 7), R. Joḥanan denies that the oath is biblical; its rules cannot be determined by biblical arguments.</i>. Rebbi La said, Rebbi Ḥiyya the elder’s statement is implied by “if two people hold on to one stole”. Since he holds on to half of it, is it not as if he brought witnesses that one half belongs to him? Could one not say that he swears and collects<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is refuted. Since each party claims the entire stole, each one is disputing half of a claim. There is no claimant nor defendant; even R. Ḥiyya must agree that the oath imposed by the Mishnah is not biblical.</i>? Is this a similar case<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Ḥiyya, the case of the <i>baraita</i> is biblical; R. La’s argument is impossible. (In <i>Ketubot,</i> R. La quotes R. Joḥanan’s statement in the next paragraph.)</i>?
This topic<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The oath prescribed in the Mishnah.</i> came up between Rebbi Abba bar Mamal and Rav Amram. One said, one does not entrust an oath to a suspect person<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">How is this oath possible? Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.1.1.1">Note 3</a>.</i>. The other said, one does not even formulate an oath for such a one<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation of the oath in the Mishnah still leaves the court open to a charge of administering an oath which helps a person to be dishonest since one could read the text as: “An oath that I have no claim to it, less than half.” Therefore, a positive statement of claim has to be inserted into the formula. The same formula is attributed to Rav Huna in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 5b" href="/Bava_Metzia.5b">Babli, 5b</a>.</i>. Rav Huna said: “An oath that I have property rights to it to the amount of no less than (the value of a <i>peruṭah</i>)<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of the Leiden ms., a scribal error.</i> [half of its value]<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Text of E, confirmed by the Babli.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, if it is about this, it is an institutional oath<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees with R. Ḥiyya and holds that the oath is purely an <i>ad hoc</i> instituted rabbinical ordinance. In the words of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 3a" href="/Bava_Metzia.3a">Babli (3a</a>), “lest it be easy to assert unfounded claims on another person’s property.”</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 1:15" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).1.15">Tosephta 1:15</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 1:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.1.1.9"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 1:1, Notes 68–71</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 7a" href="/Bava_Metzia.7a">Babli <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 7a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 170a" href="/Bava_Batra.170a"><i>Bava batra</i>170a</a>.</i>: “If two persons were holding a document<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">An IOU; the persons involved are the creditor and the debtor.</i> and one said, it is mine but I had lost it; the other said, it is mine since I paid you. The document should be validated by its signatories, the words of Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that a paid IOU either should be torn up or the receipt should be written on the document or be attached to it.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, they should split<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that a paid IOU either should be torn up or the receipt should be written on the document or be attached to it.</i>.” Rabbi Eleazar said, it all depends on who is holding the signatures<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since only the signatures validate the document. He follows the reformulation of the Mishnah in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta (1:1</a>): “If two people hold on to one toga, each one takes what he holds in his hand.” Presumably they split the remainder.</i>. Rav Ḥisda said, if this<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The opinion reported in the name of R. Eleazar the Amora.</i> is true information, it follows Rabban Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Since for Rebbi an IOU without a receipt is valid, irrespective of who holds it.</i>.
One says, half is mine; the other says, a third is mine. The one who says that half is his shall swear that no less than a quarter be his; the one who says that a third is his shall swear that no less than a sixth be his. The principle involved: One only swears on half of what he agreed to<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">On the face of it, the paragraph is unintelligible. R. E. Fulda comments: “I do not feel empowered to emend.” The corresponding <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 1:2" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).1.2">Tosephta (1:2</a>) is clear:<br>“One says, <i>all</i> is mine; the other says, a third is mine. The one who says that all is his shall swear that no less than five parts (5/6) are his; the one who says that a third is his shall swear that no less than a sixth is his. The principle involved: One only swears on half of what he agreed to.”<br>In the case of the Tosephta, the second claimant surrenders 2/3 to the first. Only 1/3 is in dispute; it is split evenly. The first one receives 2/3 + 1/6 = 5/6; the second receives 1/6. Rabbinic practice decrees that one only swears on half of the amount which is in dispute; in this case each would swear that he owns no less that one sixth in addition to what he has by consensus [Maimonides, <i>Ṭo‘en weniṭ‘an</i>9:8 (<i>Maggid Mišneh ad</i> 9:9), <i>Šulḥan ‘Arukh Ḥošen Mišpaṭ</i> 138:2 (Note)].<br>In the Yerushalmi the total of the claims only adds up to 5/6; they can be satisfied in full. There is no reason why anybody should swear. One has to explain the <i>baraita</i> as dealing with a case of bankruptcy in which the claims themselves are not sufficiently documented and can each be considered only by an affirmatory oath.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If two persons were riding on an animal or one was riding and one was walking, each of them says “it wholly belongs to me”<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">It will be explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.1.3.1">Mishnah 3</a> that the finder is not the person who first saw the abandoned property but the one who lifted it to acquire. It would be reasonable to expect that if a walker and a rider simultaneously came upon some lost property the walker had a better chance to grab the object first. But in the absence of proof these arguments are irrelevant to the court; both parties must be given equal standing.</i>, each of them has to swear that he owns no less than half of it and they shall divide it evenly. If they agree<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Even after the court gave its verdict, the parties are free to come to an amicable settlement.</i> or if they have witnesses, they divide without an oath.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If two persons were riding on an animal,” etc. Rebbi Ḥuna said: It was stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">A different, incompatible version is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 65b" href="/Kiddushin.65b">Babli, <i>Qiddušin</i> 65b</a>, in the name of Rav Aḥdevoi bar Ammi who lived a generation before R. Ḥuna and was known for his construction of impossible situations.</i>: “A woman comes<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">From overseas, without identification papers.</i> riding on an animal and two men lead her. She says: these are my slaves; the donkey and the load are mine. Each of them says: this is my wife, the other is my slave, and the donkey and the load are mine<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Since the rules of court proceedings state that “once somebody testified (under cross-examination), he cannot testify a second time”, they cannot come back to court and change their statements (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 10:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.10.3.3"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 10:5 Note 96</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 3:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.3.4.5"><i>Bikkurim</i> 3:4 Note 72</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.4.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 3:3 Note 56</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 18b" href="/Ketubot.18b">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 18b</a>). In line with the principles of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.1.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>, the court has to impose a solution compatible with all statements.</i>. She needs a bill of divorce from both of them<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Since the woman denies being married and there are no witnesses, she certainly has no claim to <i>ketubah</i> from either of the men. From <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.10.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 3:11</a> it would seem that therefore she can marry another person without a bill of divorce, as stated explicitly in the Babli; but the formulation in the Yerushalmi does not seem to support this statement.</i> and has to manumit both of them. They have to manumit one another. In the donkey and the load they are equal co-owners.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Somebody was riding on an animal when he saw a find and said to another person: “give it to me”. If [that one] took it and said: “I acquired it”, he acquired it<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Since ownerless property only can be acquired by action, never by intent or declaration (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.4.3"><i>Ketubot 5:5,</i> Note 100</a>.</i>. If after he had handed it over, he said: “I acquired it first,” he did not say anything<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The rider acquired the object by grabbing it in his hand; he is the presumed owner. The walker is now a claimant and “the burden of proof is on the claimant”. If he did not articulate his claim before handing over the object there can be no witnesses and no proof.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Somebody was riding on an animal,” etc. Rebbi Isaac says, from this rule one cannot infer anything, not from its beginning nor from its end. “Somebody was riding on an animal when he saw a find and said to another person: ‘give it to me’. If [that one] took it and said: ‘I acquired it’, he acquired it”. If he had said, “acquire it for me”, he would have acquired it. That means, from this rule one cannot infer anything, not from its beginning nor from its end<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">As <i>Pene Moshe</i> points out, this refers to the disagreement between R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 4:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.4.6.4"><i>Peah</i> 4:6, Note 112</a>), on whether a person can acquire a find for another person by lifting it. The Mishnah does not help in solving the dispute. The first sentence does not help since it does not exclude that had the rider said, “acquire it for me” instead of “give it to me”, the walker would have acquired it for the rider by lifting and would not have been able to claim that he acquired it. The second sentence does not have to be quoted since it obviously is irrelevant for the disagreement between R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody saw a find and fell upon it<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Without moving the find, it is not acquired. The second person in grabbing the find must have moved it a little; therefore, he acquired it.</i> when another person grabbed it, the one who grabbed it acquired it. If one saw people running after a find<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Lying on an open field after it was harvested, before the field was ploughed.</i>, after an injured deer, after young pigeons unable to fly, and he said: “my field shall acquire it for me”, it acquired it for him<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">A person’s real estate acquires for its owner any property lying there of which he is cognizant; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.2.4"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 6:2, Note 71</a>.</i>. If the deer was running normally, or the pigeons were flying, and he said: “my field shall acquire it for me”, he did not say anything<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Animals and birds on a field are not lying there; real estate cannot acquire moving objects.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody saw a find” etc. Rabbi Simeon ben Laqish said [in the name of] Abba Cohen bar Delaiah<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">“Resh Laqish” for “R. Simeon ben Laqish” and the omission of “in the name of” are typically Babylonian style.</i>: A person acquires a find within four cubits from himself<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 4:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.4.2.3"><i>Peah</i> 4:2, Note 31</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:3:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.3.2-9"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 8:3</a>. If a person stands in the public domain, an ownerless object is within four cubits of him but of no other person, then he has the right to acquire the object and no other person has the right to enter the circle of four cubits around him to take the object. It is clear that taking possession requires intent by the acquirer (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 10" href="/Bava_Metzia.10">Babli <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 10a/b</a>).</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, only if it fell into his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">No acquisition in the public domain can be effected by thought.</i>. A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: <sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The remainder of this paragraph essentially is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.4.2"><i>Peah</i> 4:2, Notes 40–46</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:3:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.3.2-9"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 8:3</a> (49c line 10).</i>“If he took some of the <i>peah</i> and threw it on the rest, he has nothing of it.”<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Taking <i>Peah,</i> the uncut grain at the end of a field, is done from a private domain. But since the Torah grants the poor the right to enter a field after the end of the harvest to collect <i>peah</i> and forgotten stalks and sheaves, the harvested field has the status of public domain for the poor. A poor person acquires <i>peah</i> by taking it. He cannot then take his property and spread it over the remainder of standing produce to claim ownership, neither can he lie down on it for the same purpose. As with any acquisition, an action seems to be needed.</i> He<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Laqish, defending his position.</i> answered: Explain it if the did not say that his four cubits should acquire it for him. But does it not say afterwards: “If he fell on it or spread his talith on it, one removes him from it?” That is the same, if he did not say that his four cubits should acquire it for him. But did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Peah (Lieberman) 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Peah_(Lieberman).2.2">Tosephta <i>Peah</i> 2:2</a>.</i>: “If two were pushing one another because of a sheaf and another poor person came and grabbed it from before them, he is entitled to it<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Without moving the find, it is not acquired. The second person in grabbing the find must have moved it a little; therefore, he acquired it.</i>.” It is the same; he did not say that his four cubits should acquire it for him. But did we not state: “If somebody saw a find and fell upon it when another person grabbed it, the one who grabbed it acquired it.” It is the same, he did not say that his four cubits should acquire it for him. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.2.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 8:2</a>.</i>: “If it landed close to her it is a bill of divorce, close to him it is no bill of divorce, half and half she is divorced and not divorced.” Ḥizqiah said, the Mishnah speaks of the scribe’s fee<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:3:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.3.8"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 8:3, Note 47</a>, the expression is: “The scribe of the second bill of divorce earns money.” Since divorce is a unilateral act by the husband, he has to pay the scribe. But if the wife is “divorced and not divorced”, a second bill of divorce is in the interest of both parties. The husband needs it in order to free himself from the duty to pay for his divorced wife’s upkeep (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.2.3"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 8:2, Note 36</a>), while the ex-wife needs it in order to be able to remarry. Therefore, they share the cost of the second bill.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The text is a reformulation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.4.2"><i>Peah</i> 4:2, Notes 29–39</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 8:3:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.8.3.2-9"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 8:3</a> (49c l. 8).</i> They objected: Is it not written: “I in my poverty did prepare<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Chronicles 22:14" href="/I_Chronicles.22.14"><i>1Chr.</i> 22:14</a>. David in his poverty prepared 100’000 talents of gold and 1’000’000 talents of silver for the future Temple. How can a superrich person be called poor?</i>”? How is that? If it was in his possession, he was rich. If it was not in his possession, how can a person dedicate what is not his? It must be that it was within four cubits from him<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 4:2:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.4.2.7"><i>Peah</i> 4:2, Note 50</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abin said, what means “in my poverty?” That there is no wealth before Him Who commanded and the world came into existence! Another explanation: he fasted<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Reading עני as “to be deprived”.</i> and donated the price of his meal to Heaven.
It was stated: “One who says, my house<sup class="footnote-marker">51a</sup><i class="footnote">Any of his real estate.</i> shall acquire for me any find that fell into it, did not say anything. If the find was known, his words stand<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta 1:4</a>. There, the text reads: “One who says, my house shall acquire for me any find that <i>will fall</i> into it, did not say anything.” This text is intelligible. Real estate acquires for its proprietor by the owner’s will, as expressed in the second sentence. A future find is not foreseeable and cannot be the object of today’s will. In the Halakhah, the past has to be read as future.</i>.” As in the case of Rebbi Abba bar Mina’s<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">It is not impossible that he be R. Abba bar Zamina as conjectured by R. Eliahu Fulda.</i> sharecropper who climbed a date palm<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">On R. Abba’s property.</i>, found pigeon chicks there, and took them. He went to ask the master, who said nothing to him<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">He was not quite sure whether the chicks belonged to himself, as property owner, or to his sharecropper, the finder.</i>. He went and returned them. Rebbi Abba bar Mina said, his returning is more difficult for us<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Since he treated them as ownerless property that nevertheless had an owner, even if it was not clear who the owner was.</i> than his finding.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The find of his small son or daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Even though in general קטן has to be translated as “underage”, the Mishnah might refer to children who do not earn their upkeep, irrespective of age.</i>, or of his Canaanite<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Of non-Jewish origin, whose person is his master’s property. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:3</a>.</i> male or female slave, his wife’s find<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">It is part of the <i>Ketubah</i> contract that the wife’s earnings, including her finds, are the husband’s, since the latter is responsible for his wife’s expenses. This clause can be modified by contract if the wife earns her own money and renounces her claim to her upkeep by the husband (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.1.2"><i>Ketubot</i> 7:1, Note 9</a>).</i>, these are his. The find of his adult son or daughter, or of his Hebrew male or female slave<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">A purely theoretical statement, Hebrew slavery having disappeared with the Babylonian exile; Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.2.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:2</a>.</i>, his wife’s find after he divorced her even though he did not yet pay her <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Then he still is obligated to pay for her upkeep but has no claim to her earnings.</i>, these are their own.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The find of his small son or daughter,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, only if they are not dependent on their father. But if they are dependent on their father, their finds belong to the latter<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This is the only opinion registered in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 12b" href="/Bava_Metzia.12b">Babli, 12b</a>, and in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 6:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.6.1.3"><i>Ketubot</i>6:1, Note 21</a>.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish says, a person cannot acquire a find for another<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, קטן in the Mishnah means “underage”; the finds of aduult children, 12.5 years for girls and 13 years for boys, belong to them even though they still live in their father’s house.</i>. Rebbi [Hila]<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Word missing in L and <i>editio princeps;</i> added from E..</i> said: Not that Rebbi Simeon had formulated thus, but he had a problem with what Rebbi Hoshaiah stated: If a person hires a worker for <i>any</i> work, the latter’s finds belong to the employer. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, should the finds of a person who can resign at any moment belong to his employer<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The last two sentences are formulated similarly in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 4:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.4.6.4"><i>Peah</i> 4:6, Notes 108–110</a>. The statement naturally is not applicable to a worker hired to scout for finds (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 12b" href="/Bava_Metzia.12b">Babli 12b</a>).</i>?
What is the difference between his Hebrew male and female slaves and his Canaanite male and female slaves? Rebbi Joḥanan said, since he is not empowered to change the employment of his male<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 26:39" href="/Leviticus.26.39"><i>Lev.</i> 26:39</a> prohibits to let the male Hebrew slave “work slave’s work”. This is interpreted in <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Neziqin</i> 1, <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Ioḥai</i> p. 160, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Behar, Chapter 7 3" href="/Sifra,_Behar,_Chapter_7.3"><i>Sifra Behar Pereq</i>7(3</a>) that the slave must be employed in the trade he exercised before being sold.</i> or female<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Since she is underage, it is a question whether her find belongs to her father or her master. She probably should not have been mentioned in this connection; the use of the singular in the remainder of the sentence is correct, as compared to the plural used in the next sentence.</i> Hebrew slave, that one’s find belongs to himself. Since he is empowered to change the employment of his male or female Canaanite slave, their find belongs to their master. They objected: He is not empowered to change the employment of his wife, but her find belongs to him. As we have stated there: “He forces her to work with wool,<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.6.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 5:6</a></i>” he cannot force her to work any other material<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:6:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.6.6"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:6, Note 163</a>.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">An expanded version of this discussion is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 6:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.6.1.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 6:1, Notes 17–21</a>.</i> Rebbi Abba bar Mina and Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: there is another reason in the case of the wife. What is another reason in the case of the wife? Rebbi Ḥaggai said, because of quarrel<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">This is the only reason mentioned in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 12b" href="/Bava_Metzia.12b">Babli, 12b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 40b" href="/Ketubot.40b"><i>Ketubot</i>40b</a>. The husband should not be angry that he has to pay for all of his wife’s needs but cannot get his hands on her find.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, lest she smuggle away her husband’s property and say: it is a find. They objected: before witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">72,</sup><i class="footnote">If she finds an object before witnesses, there is no reason for R. Yose’s rule. In <i>Ketubot,</i> several similar instances are invoked.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">E has a longer text, but shorter than the analogous one in <i>Ketubot,</i> to explain the question:<br>מַה מַפְקָה בֵּינֵיהוֹן. מָצָאת בָּעֵדִים. מָאן דָּמַר מִפְּנֵי הַקְּטָטָה. אֵין כָּאן קְטָטָה. וּמָאן דָּמַר שֶׁלֹּא תְהֵא מַבְרַחַת נְכָסִים מִשֶּׁל בַּעֲלָה וְאוֹמֶרֶת מְצִיאָה מָצָאתִי. הָא בָּעֵדִים לֹא אָֽסְרוּ זֶה אֶלָּא מִפְּנֵי זֶה.<br>What is the difference between them? If she found it in the presence of witnesses. For him who says, because of quarrel, would there not be quarrel? For him who says, lest she smuggle away her husband’s property and say: I found a find, it follows that before witnesses they forbade one because of the other.</i>? They forbade<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The wife’s find is handed over to the husband even in cases where it was not necessary.<br>The use of “forbade” seems inadequate here; one would expect “decreed” or “instituted” the rule. In <i>Ketubot,</i> no verb is mentioned in this sentence. R. Yose holds that the worst case has to be presumed in all situations since detailed investigation probably is impossible.</i> one because of the other<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The wife’s find is handed over to the husband even in cases where it was not necessary.<br>The use of “forbade” seems inadequate here; one would expect “decreed” or “instituted” the rule. In <i>Ketubot,</i> no verb is mentioned in this sentence. R. Yose holds that the worst case has to be presumed in all situations since detailed investigation probably is impossible.</i>.
“His wife’s find after he divorced her.” Rebbi Yosana in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: A man who divorced his wife without paying her <i>ketubah</i> is liable for her upkeep until he gave her the last penny<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">A similar rule, in the name of R. Isaac, is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 6:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.6.2.2"><i>Ketubot</i> 6:2</a> (30c line 57) <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 1:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.1.1.4">Note 22</a>. Alfasi (<i>Ketubot</i> Chapter 13, # 390) proves that the Babli disagrees and limits the husband’s liability to the amount of the <i>ketubah.</i></i>. Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah says this, “his wife’s find after he divorced her.<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">If the husband had no financial responsibility for his ex-wife there would be no reason for him to claim her find.</i>” Rebbi Hoshaiah said, the Mishnah is needed lest you say that because he is liable for her upkeep until he gave her the last penny, her find should be his.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who found documents of indebtedness containing an alienation clause should not return them since the court will foreclose on their basis<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The standard document of indebtedness was written as a mortgage covering all the real estate in the debtor’s possession at the moment of the execution of the deed. In contrast to general Egyptian and Roman practice the debtor was allowed to sell his real estate, but the alienation clause stated that the sale did not break the mortgage lien. If the debtor was unable to pay, the creditor could have regress on the buyer, who then would have to sue the debtor for his loss. The finder might not return the deed which he found since the mortgage might have been paid but the receipt somehow became separated from the document. If the deed was returned to the creditor, the latter might use it against an innocent buyer who would not know that it had been liquidated. This standard mortgage might have its root in the Ptolemaic and later Roman-Egyptian <i>obligatio omnium bonorum,</i> but probably it came with its Accadic name <i>šatārum</i> “written document” from Babylonian practice.</i>. If they do not contain an alienation clause, he should return them since the court will not foreclose on their basis<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Since the deed cannot be used against a third party, one assumes that the debtor has the means to prove that the debt was paid. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta, 1:5</a>, the formulation is: should return it if the debtor agrees.</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the Sages say, in no case should he return them since the court will foreclose on their basis<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">They rule that an alienation clause is always implied in a written bill of indebtedness; its absence only shows the incompetence of the scribe formulating the contract.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who found documents of indebtedness,” etc. It was stated in the name of Rebbi Meïr: Both based on a document containing an alienation clause or a document containing no alienation clause, he collects from unincumbered property<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Property which is in the debtor’s possession without being subject to any third party lien. In this version of R. Meïr’s position, a creditor who wants to be secure has to write a contract which forbids the debtor to either sell his property or to mortgage it to another person.</i>. Following this opinion of Rebbi Meïr, to what purpose does he return it? To cover the mouth of his pitcher<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">In this context, the remark is a <i>non sequitur.</i> It seems to refer to another tradition of R. Meïr’s, quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 13a" href="/Bava_Metzia.13a">Babli, 13a</a>, by Samuel: A contract containing no alienation clause cannot be used to foreclose. This means that such a contract is hardly worth the paper it is written on.<br>E has a different text with an additional sentence:<br>תַּנֵּי בְשֵׁם רִבִּי מֵאִיר. בֵּין שְׁטָר שֶׁיֵּשּׁ בּוֹ אַחֵרָיוּת נְכָסִין וּבֵין שְׁטָר שֶׁאֵין בּוֹ אַחֵרָיוּת נְכָסִין גּוֹבֶה מִנְּכָסִין משׁוּעֲבָּדִים. וַחֲכָמִים אומ׳ שְׁטָר שֶׁיֵּשּׁ בּוֹ אַחֵרָיוּת נְכָסִין גּוֹבֶה מִנְּכָסִין משׁוּעֲבָּדִים וְשֶׁאֵין בּוֹ אַחֵרָיוּת נְכָסִין אֵנוֹ גוֹבֶה מִנְּכָסִין משׁוּעֲבָּדִים.<br>It was stated in the name of Rebbi Meïr: Both based on a document containing an alienation clause or a document containing no alienation clause, he collects from incumbered property. But the Sages say, he collects from incumbered property based on a document containing an alienation clause, but based on a document containing no alienation clause, he cannot collect from incumbered property.<br>S. Lieberman suggests to read תַּמָּן אוֹמְרִים “there (in Babylonia), they say” instead of “but the Sages say.” This then properly refers the entire discussion to positions of R. Meïr quoted in the Halakhah. One could also suggest to read אֲחֵרִים אוֹמְרִים “others say,” and refer to the opinion stated as R. Meïr’s in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:9:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.9.2"><i>Ketubot</i> 4:9, Note 217</a>, which is identical with that quoted here in the name of the “Sages”.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The following two sentences are from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 10:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.10.1.3"><i>Bava Qamma</i> 10:1, Notes 12–13</a>. Rav’s statement is a reformulation, Samuel’s a copy.</i> Rav said, an heir is like the holder of encumbered property. Just as a loan executed before witnesses cannot be collected from encumbered property, so it cannot be collected from heirs. Samuel said, what was received cannot be foreclosed on mortgaged property, but on free property it can be foreclosed. Here you say, it can be collected, but there you say, it cannot be collected<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Unencumbered property can be foreclosed on basis of an IOU missing an alienation clause. The property of the recipient of a gift cannot be foreclosed.</i>. There is no comparison between one who agreed to a small lien and one who never agreed to a lien.
Rebbi Eleazar said, for I am saying that he wanted to take a loan but did not<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">He explains the reason of the Sages. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 10:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.10.4.1">Mishnah <i>Bava Batra</i> 10:5</a> permits to write a mortgage document for the debtor and have it signed even though the creditor not be present at that moment. As the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 12b" href="/Bava_Metzia.12b">Babli, 12b</a>, explains, the problem with this is that the mortgage might have been executed in April but the loan was given only in October. If the debtor sold real estate in the meantime, the buyer in effect bought unencumbered property while the mortgage document falsely classified it as encumbered. Therefore, the Babli restricts the permission to mortgage deeds with which the title to the land is immediately deposited with the creditor, the Egyptian ὑπάλλαγμα (cf. R. Taubenschlag, <i>The Law of Graeco-Roman Egypt in the Light of the Papyri,</i> pp. 208 ff.) The Yerushalmi (<i>loc. cit.</i> Halakhah 10:5) gives standing in court only to unilateral promises of payment for future services.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, if the debtor agreed, he should return it. Rebbi Eleazar said, if the document was generally known to be in the creditor’s hand, he should return it. But all three are broken<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">All three statements of R. Eleazar are rejected in practice.</i>, “the Sages say, in no case should he return them.” Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Because of conspiracy<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κοινωνία, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 3:9:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.3.9.4"><i>Yebamot</i>3:9, Note 133</a>. Lender and borrower might conspire to defraud the innocent buyer; see <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 1:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.1.6.3">Note 84</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If its date was that very day, he should return it<sup class="footnote-marker">87,</sup><i class="footnote">The document was dated the day it was found; the witnesses are still available who can testify to its genuineness.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">E here has an addition which is irrelevant:<br>וַאֲפִילוּ אֵין זְמַנוֹ יוֹצֵא לבוֹא בַּיּוֹם יַחֲזִיר. מָהוּ פְליג, ומד רַבִּי אַבָּהוּא בְשֵׁם רַבִּי יוֹחָנָן. מִפְּנֵי קָיְנוֹנִייָה. ומד רַבִּי יוֹחָנָן. אִם הָיָה זְמַנוֹ יוֹצֵא לְבוֹא בַּיוֹם יַחֲזִיר.<br>But could he not return it if the date was not of the same day? Do they disagree? But Rebbi Abbahu said* in the name of Rebbi Johanan: Because of conspiracy. Rebbi Johanan said*: If its date was that very day, he should return it.<br>* Reading ומר for ומד.</i>.
But could he not return it if the date was not of the same day? Do they disagree? But Rebbi Abbahu said* in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Because of conspiracy. Rebbi Joḥanan said*: If its date was that very day, he should return it.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who found women’s bills of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The divorcee would need the bill to collect her <i>ketubah.</i> If the <i>ketubah</i> was paid, the bill should be torn up. Therefore, one could assume that the woman would take care to watch over the document and, in case it was lost, to publicly announce its loss and ask the public to return it to her. In the absence of such a public announcement, the finder has to assume that the bill was not delivered. Similar arguments apply to the other documents enumerated in the Mishnah.</i> or slaves’ bills of manumission, dispositions<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Greek διαθήκη, usually referring to testamentary gifts.</i> of gift or receipts<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Documents which prove the liquidation of a mortgage.</i> should not return them since I say that they were written but he changed his mind not to deliver them.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who found women’s bills of divorce,” etc. It was stated: One whou found women’s bills of divorce with <i>ketubot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">He found a bill of divorce and attached to it a <i>ketubah</i> which either had a receipt written on it or was torn where the witnesses had signed as proof of liquidation of the debt. Then the bill certainly had been delivered to the woman, the <i>ketubah</i> had been paid, and no monetary consequences would follow the return of the bill to the woman. The <i>baraita</i> does not contradict the Mishnah.<br>E has a different formulation of the last sentence:<br>.וּכְתוּבָּה לֹא הוּחְזְקָה בְיַד הָאִשָּׁה<br>Was the <i>ketubah</i> not <i>prima facie</i> evidence in the woman’s hand?</i> should return them.” Is the <i>ketubah</i> not <i>prime facie</i> evidence?
If a person took a documented loan and paid it back, he cannot use the document to take a second loan because it diminishes the power of the buyers. Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Even on the same day<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The same statement is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 17a" href="/Bava_Metzia.17a">Babli, 17a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira asked before Rebbi Yasa: Here you say, even on the same day. But there<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">In the preceding Halakhah.</i>, you say, if its date was that very day, he should return it! He answered, a person is apt to take a loan and repay it the same day, but a person is not apt to repay a loan and take it the same day<sup class="footnote-marker">95,</sup><i class="footnote">It is a matter of <i>prima facie</i> evidence, as in the cases of the Mishnah.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The same statement is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 17a" href="/Bava_Metzia.17a">Babli, 17a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai asked before Rebbi Yasa: It is the same day, the same witnesses, and the same amount. What is the meaning of “it diminishes the power of the buyers”<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation implies that the creditor can unlawfully foreclose property from the innocent buyer in case of nonpayment of the debt.</i>? He answered, if the property had not been mortgaged<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">If the first loan was not given as a mortgage.</i>. But some say, if the witnesses did not sign for <i>this</i> loan<sup class="footnote-marker">98,</sup><i class="footnote">All the statements of R. Ḥaggai are true; nevertheless the document is invalid since the witnesses did not certify the loan actually given but a predecessor loan.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The same statement is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 17a" href="/Bava_Metzia.17a">Babli, 17a</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who found letters of appraisal<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Court documents in which the value of property in execution is appraised for the creditor.</i>, letters of sustenance<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Court documents empowering a widow to sell from her late husband’s property for her sustenance.</i>, or documents of <i>ḥalîṣah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Court documents authorizing a childless widow to remarry outside her late husband’s family.</i> or repudiation<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Court documents confirming that an underage, fatherless girl walked out on a marriage arranged by her mother or her brothers.</i>, or documents of arbitration<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Court documents in which the parties to a civil suit appoint a panel of arbitration judges and promise to abide by their judgment.</i>, or any other court document, should return them. If he found in a basket<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic حِفش “basket, bag”.</i> or in a box<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Greek γλωσσοκομεῖον “chest, box”.</i> a bundle<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Documents rolled and tied together. In joined documents one is joined to the next.</i> or joined documents he should return them. How many are joined documents? Three documents connected one to the other. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, if one persons borrows from three, he should return it to the borrower; if three persons borrow from one, he should return it to the lender. If he found a document among his documents whose nature was unknown to him<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rashi, a third party document; according to Maimonides and Ravia, a valid IOU in a bundle of receipted documents.</i>, it should lie there until Elijah comes<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">The announcer of the Messiah is reputed to know all the answers.</i>. If it has an agreement<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Greek σύμφωνον “contract”, an expression used in Byzantine era papyri for special stipulations within a written contract (cf. Taubenschlag, Note 84, p. 224). The commentators of the Babli and Alfasi follow the Geonim and Rashi in explaining <i>symphon</i> as a receipt; R. Hananel explains “receipt or codicill”.</i> attached to it, he should follow the agreement.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who found letters of appraisal,” etc. Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: If it contained a certification by the court, it should be returned<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Even a private document.</i>. Rebbi Ḥama the father of Rebbi Hoshaia said, the tamarisks among the judges of the diaspora are like chicks among the judges of the Land of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of Rav, the mightiest of the Babylonian authorities, is in error. As the Galilean authorities explain in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 16b" href="/Bava_Metzia.16b">Babli, 16b</a>, the only case in which a court certified mortgage document can be returned, is an instruction by the court that ownership of a well-defined property of the debtor is transferred to the creditor in liquidation of the mortgage. In this case, no third party buyer is endangered. But a document certified for unspecified foreclosure cannot be returned since an innocent third party might be damaged.</i>.
Rav Hamnuna said, if the sale contract was written on the first of Nisan but payment was only on the tenth of Nisan<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Then ownership will only be transferred on the 10<sup>th</sup>; the piece of property will be at the disposal of the creditors of the seller, not of the buyer, between the 1<sup>st</sup> and the 10<sup>th</sup>. In the interpretation of <i>Sefer Ha‘iṭṭur</i> I 30a, the Babli disputes the validity of any sale document predating the transfer of ownership.</i>, they have to write: Even though we wrote the document on the first of Nisan it was agreed that payment be made on the tenth of Nisan.
Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: A stipulation produced by the creditor in the creditor’s handwrtiting is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">The same statement is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 20b" href="/Bava_Metzia.20b">Babli, 20b</a>. <i>Sefer Ha‘iṭṭur</i> I 41a (Notes מט to נא) gives two slightly different versions of parts of this paragraph.</i>, for I am saying that he has trained in writing documents. This implies that in the hand of another it would be valid. Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman in the name of Samuel: A stipulation is only valid if it is in the possession of the creditor but written by the debtor. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šebuot</i> 7:9, quoted <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:7:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.7.5"><i>Ketubot</i> 9:7 (Note 177</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 8:6:2-12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.8.6.2-12"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 8:6</a> (20b l. 62), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 20b" href="/Bava_Metzia.20b">Babli 20b</a>, <i>Šebuot</i> 45. This is the oath required from heirs if they sue for payment of IOU’s they inherited. It is clear that the creditor’s heirs are not the creditor himself.</i>: “That our father did not charge us, that our father did not tell us, that we did not find a document among our father’s documents that this note was paid.” Therefore, it might be found paid. What about this? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, explain it that it was a testamentary gift., since people are not likely to impair their testamentary gifts<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Attachments to documents of testamentary gifts written by the donor are to be accepted at face value. The same statement is repeated in <i>Šebuot</i>7:9, 38a l. 48.</i>.

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Which finds are his and which ones does he have to make public<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.2.5.1">Mishnah 6</a>, there is a biblical requirement that the finder not return a found object unless the person claiming to be the owner clearly identifies the object by some marks or properties not generally known. Therefore, objects without identifying properties cannot be returned and become the finder’s immediately since it may be assumed that the owner immediately gave up hope of recovery, so that an ownerless object was picked up.</i>? The following finds are his: If he found scattered produce, scattered coins<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">If these are common produce or common coins, they have no identifying marks nor can they be identified by the place where they were lost.</i>, bundles of grain in the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Small bundles which are blown away by the wind or moved by passing animals or people; they cannot be expected to be found at the place where they were lost. Since most farmers make such bundles, their shape is no identifying mark.</i>, or fig cakes, baker’s loaves, strings of fish, cuts of meat, local fleece-wool, bundles of flax, or strips of purple wool<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">All these are local products, made uniformly by several producers and, therefore, without individual identifying marks.</i>; these are his<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli and most independent Mishnah mss.: “The words of R. Meïr.” Since an anonymous Mishnah is supposed to be R. Meïr’s, the addition is unnecessary. The individual opinions expressed in the next Mishnah are clarifications, not dissenting opinions.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Which finds are his,” etc. From where may an owner’s hopelessness<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">That a lost article becomes ownerless the moment the owner realizes his loss since he has no hope of recovery. Then appropriating a find which has no distinguishing marks is not theft. This leaves open the question, discussed at length in the Babli, why keeping a find without marks is not theft even before the original owner became conscious of his loss.</i> be deduced from the Torah? Rebbi Joḥanan said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Yehoṣadaq: “So you shall proceed with his donkey,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:3" href="/Deuteronomy.22.3"><i>Deut.</i> 22:3</a>: “So you shall proceed with his donkey, so you shall proceed with his garment, so you shall proceed with anything <i>lost by your neighbor and which you found</i>; you cannot avoid taking notice.” As usual, the argument is about a part of the verse which is not quoted. The clause in Italics seems to be superfluous; if he did not lose and you did not find, there would be no case. It must be that you found something which can be traced back to your neighbor.</i>” etc. What is lost by him but may be found by anybody you have to make public. But what is not lost by him but may be found by anybody you do not have to make public. This excludes the owner’s hopelessness about that which was lost by him or anybody.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).2.5">Tosephta 2:5</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 22b" href="/Bava_Metzia.22b">Babli 22b</a>.</i>: “One who found bundles of grain in the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Small bundles of standard size on the road leading from the fields to town.</i> does not have to make it public; in a private domain<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">On a field or a narrow path, not accessible to persons not engaged in agriculture at that place.</i> he has to make it public<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In this situation, the bundle might not have been moved from the place and the place might count as identifying mark.</i>. Sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Large sheaves for which size, kind, and place might count as identifying marks.</i> he has to make public, whether in the public or the private domain.”
“Fig cakes.” That is, large cakes. But small cakes which come from Bostra<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">They are not locally made and may exhibit characteristics which distinguish them from other imported produce.</i> he has to make public since they are typical for that place. Cuts of meat and among them a loaf<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">E reads כָּבֵד אֶחָד “a liver”. This seems to be a more reasonable text. Meat which is not a standard cut produced by every butcher has to be announced.</i> or a kidney he has to make public. Strings of fish<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Routinely made by every fisherman when he brings his catch to market.</i> strung together with a vegetable or a whitefish<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Routinely made by every fisherman when he brings his catch to market.</i> he has to make public.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Jehudah says, anything out of the ordinary<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The deviation from the norm serves as identifying mark.</i> one has to make public. How is this? If he found a fig cake containing a potsherd, or a loaf containing coins. Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar said, merchandise of any kind<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">S. Lieberman, following B. Mussaphia, identifies as Greek ἐμπορία. If a new vessel is offered for trade and looks like any other new vessel of the same manufacture, no identification is possible. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta, 2:1</a>, R. Simeon ben Eleazar explicitly states that vessels which show signs of use must be advertised.</i> one does not have to make public.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Jehudah says, anything out of the ordinary,” etc. It was stated: If it is embedded in it<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Kneaded into the fig cake or baked into the loaf.</i>. Here you say, if it is embedded in it. But there<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 2:8" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).2.8">Tosefta 2:8</a>: One who found a container and produce in front of it, a wallet and coins in front of it, must make it public.</i> you say, if it lies on top of it. When you say, if it is embedded in it, in the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Where the place is not a distinguishing mark.</i>. When you say, if it lies on top of it, in a private domain. Maybe even in the public domain? If it lies on top of it, I am saying that if fell there.
“Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar said, merchandise of any kind<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">S. Lieberman, following B. Mussaphia, identifies as Greek ἐμπορία. If a new vessel is offered for trade and looks like any other new vessel of the same manufacture, no identification is possible. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta, 2:1</a>, R. Simeon ben Eleazar explicitly states that vessels which show signs of use must be advertised.</i> one does not have to make public.” Rebbi Joḥanan made this public and stated about it<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta 2:1</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 24a" href="/Bava_Metzia.24a">Babli 24a</a>.</i>: “The following are these. For example needle strips<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Intact books of needles; an unused mass-produced article without individual marks.</i>, fire tongs<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Where it is easy to see that they were never used.</i>, and strips of axes<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Since one only uses one axe at a time, these were never used.</i>. The Amora of Rebbi Isaac bar Tevlai found a web rolled in a scarf<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>amictorium</i> “loose garment, throw, scarf, etc.”. <i>Arukh</i> explains as “burnoose”. In Ashkenazic rabbinic Hebrew used for “coat”.</i>. He came before Rebbi Isaac bar Tevlai who instructed him following Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar. The Amora of Rebbi Joḥanan found a web rolled in a scarf. He came before Rebbi Johanan who asked him, where did you find it? In an alley<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>semita.</i> A semi-private place, whose location might be a distinguishing mark and would have to be advertised.</i>, in a public square<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>platea,</i> Greek πλατεῖα. Here R. Simeon ben Eleazar’s rule applies.</i>? For he wanted to free him following Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> But the following must be made public<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Because there are distinguishing marks which the legitimate owner can identify if only the bare fact that such-and-such an item was found was made public.</i>: If one found produce in a container or simply a container, coins in a wallet or simply a wallet, heaps of produce, heaps of coins<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The amount and kind of the coins can be indicated.</i>, three coins one on top of the other<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The particular way in which the coins were arranged is a distinguishing mark.</i>, bundles of grain in a private domain<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Small bundles which are blown away by the wind or moved by passing animals or people; they cannot be expected to be found at the place where they were lost. Since most farmers make such bundles, their shape is no identifying mark.</i>, home-baked loaves<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">They have peculiar shapes which distinguish them from professionally produced loaves.</i>, fleece wool taken from an artisan’s house<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Showing signs of processing.</i>. Pitchers of wine and pitchers of oil he has to make public.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “But the following must be made public,” etc. Rav Jehudah says three coins of three kings<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Three coins from three mints are certainly an identifying mark. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 25a" href="/Bava_Metzia.25a">Babli, 25a</a>, this is a statement by the Galilean R. Ḥanina.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, or even three from one king if they form a tower<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">If the loser can indicate both the kind of coin and the way they were arranged, he is identified as the owner. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 25a" href="/Bava_Metzia.25a">Babli, 25a</a>, the only arrangement counted as identifying mark is that of a tower, the coins one on top of the other.</i>? Of three kings and they are in three towers? Or even three of one king as a tower<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This is the final answer. The claimant must identify either three different mintings or one kind of coin and the way they were arranged. Then it would be improbable that the arrangement was accidental.</i>.
“Pitchers of wine and pitchers of oil he has to make public.”<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">This quote from the Mishnah is out of place since one continues the discussion of single coins found.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, if it was like a tripod<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Three coins forming a regular triangle. This arrangement does not count as identifying mark.<br>A corral is an enclosed space where nobody dwells permanently. If there are three tool sheds or three cow sheds in the corral, it may well be triangular.</i> one does not have to make it public. What means, like a tripod? For example, a corral belonging to three people. Since one does not know to whom to return it, he does not have to make it public. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">This clearly refers to R. Yose the Amora, not the Tanna ben Ḥalafta. The meaning of the mention of “R. Ḥalafta” in his statement is not clear.</i> said, one understands from here that if a person went behind Rebbi Ḥalafta and a denar fell down from them, since one does not know to whom to return it, he does not have to make it public<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">If there are no identifying marks and there is a doubt even between only two people, the find does not have to be returned.</i>. Simeon bar Abba was walking behind Rebbi Eleazar; a denar fell down from him. Simeon bar Abba presented it to him. He answered, I already had given up hope about this. They said, Rebbi Eleazar only intended it for Rebbi Simeon bar Abba because he was poor<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Since he refused to accept alms, R. Eleazar found a way to give him money in a way understood only by scholars</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one found pigeon chicks bound together behind a temporary or permanent fence or on paths between fields<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Not easily accessible to the public.</i>, he should not touch them<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">One may not touch other people’s property which possibly was deposited somewhere, neither lost nor abandoned, since, as Maimonides writes (<i>Gezelah wa‘bedah</i> 15:1), in the worst case the owner would lose his property and in the best he would be obligated to provide a distinguishing mark to regain his property.</i>. If one found a vessel on garbage heaps, if it was covered he should not touch it<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">One may not touch other people’s property which possibly was deposited somewhere, neither lost nor abandoned, since, as Maimonides writes (<i>Gezelah wa‘bedah</i> 15:1), in the worst case the owner would lose his property and in the best he would be obligated to provide a distinguishing mark to regain his property.</i>, if in the open he should take it and make it public<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">An intact vessel usually is not abandoned.</i>. What one found in a heap or an old wall<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">A wall is old if it no longer shields anything.</i> belongs to him. At a new wall<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">A stone wall built with niches.</i>, from the middle to the outside belongs to him, from the middle to the inside belongs to its owner. If he was renting out to others, even what is inside the house belongs to him<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Anything left in a rental apartment after the renter moved out belongs to the landlord.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one found behind a temporary fence”, etc. Rav Jehudah said: Only pigeon chicks tied together at their wings<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">As the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 25b" href="/Bava_Metzia.25b">Babli explains, 25b</a>, it was general usage to tie pigeon chicks together at their wings for transport, sale, etc. If these chicks were taken, no distinguishing marks otherwise were available and the owner could never get them back even if the find was advertised.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Zavda found a ring covered in a box<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">E reads בחספה “with a potsherd”. L is confirmed by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Bava Metzia 25b:8:2" href="/Tosafot_on_Bava_Metzia.25b.8.2">Tosaphot (25b <i>s.v.</i>ואם</a>).</i> and took it. He came and asked Rav, who told him, you did not act correctly. He said, shall I return it? He answered, no, for we might say that his owner came, looked for it, did not find it and gave up hope for it<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 25b" href="/Bava_Metzia.25b">Babli, 25b</a>, does not tell the story but quotes its result: R. Abba bar Zavda says in the name of Rav that “if possibly it was deposited, one should not take it, but if he took it, he should not return it.” In Rashi’s interpretation, one has to advertise the find. Following Maimonides (<i>l</i>. <i>c.</i> <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.2.4.1">Note 42</a>), the finder keeps it. Ravad disagrees and holds that since the finder took it illegally, he cannot acquire but the object must lie untouched until the prophet Elijah comes and determines the owner. The last two opinions can be read into the Yerushalmi text which notes that the owner probably gave up hope of recovery and, therefore, could not regain the object even when the find was advertised.</i>.
It was stated: What one found on a garbage heap he has to make public, for garbage is moved<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Also quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 25b" href="/Bava_Metzia.25b">Babli, 25b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 2:11" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).2.11">Tosephta 2:11</a>. S. Lieberman explains that, in contrast to the garbage heap in the public domain mentioned in the Mishnah, one speaks here of a private garbage heap which periodically is emptied into a landfill. Therefore, everything lying there is either abandoned or lost and must be taken and advertised.</i>. What one found in a heap or an old wall<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">A wall is old if it no longer shields anything.</i> belongs to him, for I am saying that it belonged to the Emorites<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 2:12" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).2.12">Tosephta 2:12</a>. Archeological finds are ownerless and can be taken by the finder.</i>. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">A similar, but not necessarily compatible, text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 2:13" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).2.13">Tosephta 2:13</a>.</i>: If one found between planks<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">A temporary fence made of wooden planks. The planks do not touch but are placed close enough to one another that no goat can pass between them. The problem is the status of the empty spaces between the planks.</i>, from the door and inside is like inside, from the door and outside is like outside. If it was opening to the inside, even from from the middle to the outside belongs to the owner of the property; if it was opening to the outside, even from the middle to the inside belongs to the finders.
“If he was renting out to others, even what is inside the house belongs to him.”<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">This quote is not really connected with the following, which refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).2.2">Tosephta 2:2</a>, in which R. Simeon ben Eleazar states that “what is saved from the mouth of a lion or a wolf, from the surge of the ocean or a river, or found on a public thoroughfare, belongs to the finder since the owners had given up hope.” Since this is stated as an individual opinion, it follows that the anonymous majority require publication in all cases.</i> Rebbi Jeremiah says, they disagree about a hostelry<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πανδοκεῖον, τό.</i>, but in a courtyard everybody agrees that he is required to make it public. Rebbi Yose said, they disagree about a courtyard, but in a hostelry everybody agrees that it belongs to its finder.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> What one found in a store is his<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Because most probably one of the previous customers lost the money which by its nature has no distinguishing marks.</i>. Between the counter and the storekeeper it is the storekeeper’s. Before the moneychanger it is his<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Because most probably one of the previous customers lost the money which by its nature has no distinguishing marks.</i>. Between the chair<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Where the customer sits who has his money exchanged.</i> and the moneychanger it is the moneychanger’s. If one bought produce from another person or another person sent him produce and he found coins in it, they are his<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The coins probably were lost by a person engaged in harvest or transport, not the seller of the produce.</i>. But if they were bundled<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Where the number of coins, their value, and the way they were tied together are distinguishing marks.</i>, he takes them and makes it public.<br>The garment<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:3" href="/Deuteronomy.22.3"><i>Deut.</i> 22:3</a>: “So you shall treat his donkey, so you shall treat <i>his garment,</i> so you shall treat <i>anything</i> lost by your fellow man, which is lost by him but you found it; you are not allowed to disregard it.”</i> was subsumed under “anything”; why was it mentioned separately? To bracket with it and tell you, just as a garment is special in that it has distinguishing marks and claimants, so about everything which has distinguishing marks and claimants you have to give public notice<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifry Deut.</i> 224. This justifies the arguments in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 2:4:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.2.4.2-4">Halakhah 4</a> that even if there are distinguishing marks, one does not have to advertise if there are no claimants.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “What one found in a store is his,” etc. Rebbi Eleazar said, so is the Mishnah: On his chair, on his counter<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 26b" href="/Bava_Metzia.26b">Babli, 26b</a>, in the name of R. Eleazar infers from the Mishnah that forgotten coins on the counter of store or bank are the finder’s. The language of the Yerushalmi rather points to the opposite conclusion, <i>viz.,</i> that they are the storekeeper’s or the banker’s.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">An abbreviated Hebrew version of this story is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Devarim Rabbah 3:5" href="/Devarim_Rabbah.3.5"><i>Deut. rabba</i> 3(5</a>).</i> Simeon ben Shetaḥ<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The head of the Synhedrion in Alexander Yannai’s time.</i> was working with linen. His students said to him, Rabbi, in order to make it easier for you we want to buy a donkey, then you will not have to work so hard. They went and bought a donkey from a Saracen; a pearl was hanging on its neck. They came to him saying, because of this you will not have to work anymore. He said to them, why? They told him, we bought for you a donkey from a Saracen and a pearl is hanging on its neck. He asked them, does its owner know about this? They answered, no. He told them, go and return it. But did not Rav Huna, Bevay bar Gozlan, in the name of Rav say, they objected before Rebbi: Even according to him who says that an object robbed from a Gentile is forbidden, everybody agrees that what he lost is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 113b" href="/Bava_Kamma.113b"><i>Bava qama</i> 113b</a>.</i>. Do you think that Simeon ben Shetaḥ was a barbarian<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Greek βάρβαρος, “barbarous, foreign”.</i>?
