Simeon ben Shetaḥ wanted to hear: Praised be the God of the Jews, more than any gain in this world.
The following implies the same. Rebbi Ḥanina reports the following case: The elder rabbis bought a heap of wheat from the household of the general and found in it a bundle of denars. They returned it to them. They said, praised be the God of the Jews.
Abba Oshaia from Tireh [was a washer. The queen came to bathe in a water pond. She lost her בינדילנין<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Lieberman suggests Greek δεράνιον, Byzantine δεράιον “necklace”.</i>; he found it. When she left, he handed it to her.]<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">From E, missing in L and <i>editio princeps,</i> but already noted in the 17th Cent. by R. Menaḥem di Lonzano in his textual notes on the Yerushalmi.</i> She said, this is for you; it is not important for me and I have more of this. He told her, the Torah decided that we have to return it. She said, praised be the God of the Jews.
Rebbi Samuel bar Sosarṭai went to Rome. The queen had lost her דילניה but he found it. A herold went through the city: One who will return it within 30 days will receive such and such; after 30 days his head will be lifted from him. He did not return it within 30 days. After 30 days he returned it. She asked him, had you not been in the city? He answered, yes. She asked him, had you not heard the voice of the herold? He answered, yes. She asked him, what did he say? He answered, one who will return it within 30 days will receive such and such; after 30 days his head will be lifted from him. She asked him, why did you not return it within 30 days? He answered, one should not say that I acted out of fear of you, but out of fear of the Merciful. She said to him, praised be the God of the Jews.
<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Parallel versions of this story are in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 33:1" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.33.1"><i>Gen. rabba</i> 33(1</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 27:1" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.27.1"><i>Lev. rabba</i>27(1</a>).</i> Alexander the Macedonian went to the king of Cassia<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">In the Midrashim: Behind the Mountains of Darkness.</i>. He saw that he had much gold, much silver. He told him, I do not need your gold nor your silver; I came only so see your practice<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πρᾶξις.</i>, how you are dealing, how you are judging. While he was still discussing with him, there came a man having a dispute with another, for he bought (a plot, entered it) [a garbage heap, dug in it]<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is the L text, the one in brackets is the text of E and the Midrashim. The sequel shows that the latter text is original.</i> and found a treasure trove of denars. The buyer said, I bought a garbage heap, I did not buy a treasure. The seller said, I sold a garbage heap and all it contains. While they still were arguing with one another, the king asked one of them, do you have a male son? He answered, yes. He asked the other one, do you have a female daughter? He answered, yes. He told them, marry them to each other and the treasure should belong to both. He<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Alexander.</i> started to laugh. He<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The savage king.</i> asked him, why are you laughing? Did I not judge correctly? He asked him, if the case came before you, how would you have judged? He<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Alexander.</i> told him, one kills both of them and the treasure falls to the king. He<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The savage king.</i> told him, do you love gold so much? He<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The savage king.</i> made him a meal and brought before him golden meat, golden chicken. He<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Alexander.</i> asked him, can I eat gold? He<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The savage king.</i> told him, the spirit of this person should be wiped out. You cannot eat gold, why do you love gold so much? He<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The savage king.</i> asked him, does the sun shine upon you? He<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Alexander.</i> answered, yes. Does rain fall on you? He<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Alexander.</i> answered, yes. He<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The savage king.</i> asked him, do you have small animals? He<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Alexander.</i> answered, yes. The spirit of this person should be wiped out; you live only by the merit of the small animals, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 36:7" href="/Psalms.36.7"><i>Ps.</i> 36:7</a>.</i>: “Man and animal You are saving, o Eternal!”

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How long does one have to make it public<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">To actively seek out the owner.</i>. Until his neighbors know, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jehudah says, three holidays, and after the last holiday seven days so that one goes home during three days, comes back during three days, and makes it public for one day<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Presuming that every person who made the pilgrimage to Jerusalem publicly declare there his finds (at the “stone of claimants”, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 28b" href="/Bava_Metzia.28b">Babli 28b</a>) so that everybody hear all declarations and can inform his neighbors about the objects found. The person who lost an advertised object then can go to Jerusalem.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “How long does one have to make it public,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 2:17" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).2.17">Tosephta 2:17</a>; a different version in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 28b" href="/Bava_Metzia.28b">Babli 28b</a>.</i>: In earlier times one made it public during three holidays, and after the last holiday seven days so that one goes home during three days, comes back during three days, and makes it public during one day. After the destruction of the Temple they instituted that one has to make it public during three days<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta, 30 days. In the Babli, “one has to give notice in the synagogues and houses of study”, no time limit given. In one Babli ms: “One <i>only</i> has to give notice in the synagogue.” A similar notice is implied by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 2:7:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.2.7.2">Halakhah 8</a>.</i>. Starting from the danger<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Hadrian’s persecutions when all synagogues were closed and religious assemblies were forbidden (S. Lieberman).</i> they instituted that one has to inform his relatives and neighbors<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta and some Babli texts, “his relatives, neighbors, and acquaintances.”</i>; this is sufficient.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one described the lost object but not its identifying marks, he<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The finder may deliver only after satisfying himself that the claimant is the legal owner.</i> should not deliver. To the swindler one should not deliver even if he describes its identifying marks, since it is said: “Until investigating your brother about it,<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:2" href="/Deuteronomy.22.2"><i>Deut.</i> 22:2</a>; <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 223.</i>” until you investigate your brother whether he be a swindler or no swindler.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one described the lost object,” etc. What is a swindler? He shows himself as one who returns finds and uses this for a big coup. How does he make a big coup? He shows himself to people as one who returns finds so they will give him valuables in trust. He takes all of them and disappears. But some say, he goes to one synagogue and hears about distinguishing marks; then he goes to another synagogue, describes the indentifying marks and takes [the finds] away.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Anything which works and eats<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">E. g., cattle or donkeys.</i> shall work and eat<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">While the finder waits for the owner to claim his animal.</i>. What does not work but eats<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Any other kind of animal.</i> should be sold because it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:2" href="/Deuteronomy.22.2"><i>Deut.</i> 22:2</a>; <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 223.</i>, “and return it to him,” plan to return it to him<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The find has to be kept in such a way that it can be returned without presenting to its owner a bill for expenses during the waiting period.</i>. What happens with the money? Rebbi Tarphon says, he may use it; therefore if it is lost he is liable for its alienation. Rebbi Aqiba says, he may not use it; therefore if it is lost he is not liable for its alienation.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Anything which works and eats,” etc. It was stated: There should not be given food in the value of a calf to calves, a foal to foals, or a chicken to chickens<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The Yerushalmi sets no time limit on how long the finder has to care for animals he found. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 2:20" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).2.20">Tosefta (2:20</a>) and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 28b" href="/Bava_Metzia.28b">Babli (28b</a>) give different limits. The statement here in contrast notes that by the time the bill for the food of an animal in a herd approaches the cost of one animal, the finder should sell the herd (under the supervision of the court, in order to escape financial responsibilities.)</i>. It happened that a man found five calves; he was selling and feeding until only one was left. Rav Jehudah in the name of Rav: Practice follows Rebbi Tarphon<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 29b" href="/Bava_Metzia.29b">Babli, 29b</a>, Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel.</i> about objects with distinguishing marks<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Objects without distinguishing marks immediately become the finder’s property.</i>. Rebbi Abba and Rav Jehudah in the name of Rav: It happened that Rebbi instructed following Rebbi Tarphon in a case about objects with distinguishing marks. Rav Jehudah said, a scholar does not have to indicate a distinguishing mark. Rav Huna said, everybody<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Even R. Aqiba.</i> agrees that if it was a bundle of coins he may not touch them<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 29b" href="/Bava_Metzia.29b">Babli 29b</a>.</i>.
The great Jehudah went to the synagogue. He took off his sandals and they disappeared. He said, if I had not gone to the synagogue, my sandals would not have disappeard<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">He followed the oriental usage not to enter a synagogue with his shoes on his feet. The story belongs to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 2:7:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.2.7.2">Halakhah 8</a>; it shows that swindlers (or in this case, thieves) are found even in a synagogue or among scholars.</i>. Rebbi Yose was sitting and teaching when a dead body was brought in. He did not say anything either to those who got up or to those who remained sitting<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">A reformulation of a text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:1:2-27" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.1.2-27"><i>Berakhot</i> 3:1</a>; <i>Nazir</i> 7:1 Note 34. It has no relevance for the topics discussed here.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who found book-scrolls reads in them once every thirty days; if he is illiterate, he rolls them up<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The rules of the Mishnah are based on the finder’s biblical obligation to keep the find in as good condition as possible (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 2:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.2.8.1">Note 88</a>). Leather or parchment scrolls deteriorate if they are not rolled up for a long time.</i>. But he should not study a new subject in them, nor should a second person read with him<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Intensive use is apt to damage the scroll.</i>. One who found a garment shakes it out every thirty days; he spreads it out for its conservation, but not to show off<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Displaying a garment which is too expensive for his means in order to enhance one’s credit standing is illegitimate use of another person’s property.</i>. Silver and brass vessels he uses for their preservation, but not to rub off<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">These vessels get tarnished if they are not used. But they cannot be used in a way which would require heavy rubbing for cleansing since this would diminish their weight.</i>. Gold and glass vessels he should not touch until the prophet Elijah comes<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 2:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.2.4.2">Note 49</a>.</i>. If one found a sack or a chest or anything which he does not take up, he need not take it<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">If his social standing would be impaired if he was seen carrying something that in normal circumstances is carried only by servants, he may disregard the biblical commandment to take up the find.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who found book-scrolls,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 2:21-22" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).2.21-22">Tosephta 2:21–22</a>; in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 29b-30a" href="/Bava_Metzia.29b-30a">Babli 29b–30a</a> a series of <i>baraitot</i>; partial parallel in <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 224.</i>: “One who found book-scrolls reads in them once every thirty days; if he is illiterate he rolls them up. He should not read a paragraph and repeat it, nor should he read a paragraph and translate it<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">If it is a Torah scroll, it may not be used to prepare either for the reading or the Aramaic translation in the synagogue.</i>, nor should he open more than three pages<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">To avoid tearing the scroll inadvertently.</i>, nor should three persons read from the same tome. When has this been said? For new ones<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Whose leather or parchment is stiff and there is a danger lest it break if not moved from time to time.</i>. But for old ones twelve months. Silver vessels he may use cold but not hot because it blackens them<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">They become tarnished.</i>. Brass vessels he may use hot but not on the fire because it diminishes them. Rakes and axes he may use on soft material but not on hard. With a rake he may receive mortar, wash it, and put it back in its place. With an ax he may split wood<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Soft wood, not hardwood.</i>, but not stone-palm or olive wood. What has been said about a find is valid equally for deposits. If one deposits a garment with another person, he has to shake it out once every thirty days. If it is much work, he can collect his expenses<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Such as hiring help to take care of the garment.</i> from it.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> What is a lost animal<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:1" href="/Deuteronomy.22.1"><i>Deut.</i> 22:1</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:4" href="/Exodus.23.4"><i>Ex.</i> 23:4</a> which state the duty to return straying animals to their owners.</i>? If he found a donkey or a cow grazing on the road<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Even if no person was near watching the animal.</i>, this is no lost animal. A donkey whose gear is upside-down or a cow running in a vineyard are lost animals. If he returned it but it ran away, returned it but it ran away even four or five times, he is obligated to return it since it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:1" href="/Deuteronomy.22.1"><i>Deut.</i> 22:1</a>. The infinitive construction is regularly interpreted as implying repetition; cf. <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 222.</i>: “Return it returning.” If he was losing time worth a tetradrachma<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">If the person finding the animal is highly paid and his time is worth much more than that of an agricultural hired hand, the question arises whether he can be forced to miss his lucrative trade in order to return a stray animal.</i>, he cannot say, give me a tetradrachma but [the owner] may pay him at the rate of an unemployed worker. If there is a court<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rashi, this may be an <i>ad hoc</i> court composed of three of the finder’s acquaintances who can empower him to charge full compensation for his time. Since the Yerushalmi does not discuss the Mishnah, its position cannot be ascertained.</i>, he can stipulate with him<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">This word is also in Alfasi’s text but only in one ms. of the Babli. R. Yosef Ḥabiba (<i>Nimmuqe Yosef</i>) in his commentary to Alfasi points out that the word makes no sense since the finder’s obligation already has terminated if the animal’s owner is around.</i> before the court. If there is no court, before whom could he stipulate? His own comes first<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">There is no obligation to incur monetary loss in following <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:1" href="/Deuteronomy.22.1"><i>Deut.</i> 22:1</a>.</i>.<br><sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">This is a continuation of Mishnah 11. On the owner’s property, no animal is staying.</i> If one found it in the cow-shed, he has no obligation; in the public domain he is obligated. But if it was between graves, he<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">If the possible helper was a Cohen.</i> may not defile himself. If his father told him to defile himself, or told him not to return it, he should not listen to him<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Since both son and father are required to follow God’s commandments, if the father commands not to obey God, he must be disobeyed (<i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Neziqin</i> Chapter 20; ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 325.)</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Here begins the rabbinic interpretation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:5" href="/Exodus.23.5"><i>Ex.</i> 23:5</a>.</i> If he unloaded and loaded, unloaded and loaded even four or five times, he is obligated since it is said: “restoring restore”<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:5" href="/Exodus.23.5"><i>Ex.</i> 23:5</a>; in rabbinic interpretation, the root here is עזב II. Ibn Ezra in both his commentaries <i>ad loc.</i> calls this “far fetched”. Onqelos, Pseudo-Jonathan, and Saadia translate as if it were written twice, first עזב I “abandon”, then עזב II “put in good order.” This may be a pun intended in the biblical text.</i>. If he<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of a animal which stumbled.</i> sat down and said, since it is your<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">The person who comes to help. <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Iohai,</i> p. 215.</i> religious obligation, if you want to unload, unload, he<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">The person who comes to help. <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Iohai,</i> p. 215.</i> is free since it is said: “with him”. It is a religious obligation to unload, but not to load; Rebbi Simeon says, also to load<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Implied by the parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:4" href="/Deuteronomy.22.4"><i>Deut.</i>22:4</a>: “uplifting you shall uplift with him.” <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Neziqin</i> 20 (p. 326); <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 225.</i>. Rebbi Yose the Galilean said, if the load was more than the ordinary, he need not do anything since it is said: “under <i>its</i> load,” a load which it can carry<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Neziqin</i> 20 (p. 325).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “What is a lost animal,” etc. “If one found it in the cowshed, he has no obligation; in the public domain he is obligated. Between graves, he may not defile himself. If his father told him to defile himself, or told him not to return it, he should not listen to him.” Everywhere you say that a positive commandment has precedence over a prohibition, but here, you say that a positive commandment does not have precedence over a prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The positive commandment is to honor father and mother, to fear mother and father. The obligation to help with a fallen animal is formulated as a prohibition: one is forbidden to be oblivious of the animal’s pain; the Cohen is forbidden to enter a cemetary. Therefore, why should the father’s command not be obeyed in preference to obeying biblical prohibitions.</i>? There is a difference since both he and his father are required to honor the Omnipresent<sup class="footnote-marker">118,</sup><i class="footnote">Since both son and father are required to follow God’s commandments, if the father commands not to obey God, he must be disobeyed (<i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Neziqin</i> Chapter 20; ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 325.)</i><sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 32a" href="/Bava_Metzia.32a">Babli, 32a</a>, this is explained as direct obligation formulated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:3" href="/Leviticus.19.3"><i>Lev.</i> 19:3</a>.</i>.
It was stated: “Lying down” but not one which habitually lies down<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Neziqin</i> 20 (p. 325); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 33a" href="/Bava_Metzia.33a">Babli 33a</a>.<br>It has been noted by modern Bible commentators that רבץ (Accadic <i>rabaṣu,</i> Arabic ربض) describes an animal resting comfortably on its knees and that, therefore, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:5" href="/Exodus.23.5"><i>Ex.</i> 23:5</a> is an exhortation not to be a busybody offering help when it is not needed, whereas <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:4" href="/Deuteronomy.22.4"><i>Deut</i>. 22:4</a> is the obligation to help where it is needed. The problem with this interpretation is that it does not fit the general context of the Chapter in <i>Ex.</i> Therefore, the rabbinic interpretation is preferable which essentially treats all words in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:5" href="/Exodus.23.5"><i>Ex.</i> 23:5</a> as ambiguous.</i>. But then it was stated: “He unloads with him even a hundred times<sup class="footnote-marker">127*</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 2:24" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).2.24">Tosephta 2:24</a>.</i> in a day.” When you said, “lying down” rather than one which habitually lies down, about one which lies down by himself. When you said, “he unloads with him even a hundred times,” in an accident.
<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 32a" href="/Bava_Metzia.32a">Babli 32a</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Neziqin</i> 20 p. 323, <i>dR. Simeon bar Ioḥai</i> p. 215, <i>Sifry Deut. 222, Midrash Tannaїm</i> (<i>Midrash Haggadol</i>) 22:4.</i>“if you hit upon.<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:5" href="/Exodus.23.5"><i>Ex.</i> 23:5</a>.</i>” I could think, really if you hit upon him; the verse says, “if you see.<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:4" href="/Deuteronomy.22.4"><i>Deut.</i> 22:4</a>.</i>” Concerning “if you see,” I could think even at a distance of a hundred<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">E has מלוא מיל “a full <i>mil</i>”, which for practical purposes is as impossible as 100 <i>mil</i>.</i> <i>mil</i>? The verse says, “if you hit upon.” How is this? The Sages estimated one in 7 1/2 of a <i>mil,</i> i. e., a <i>stadion</i><sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">E has מלוא מיל “a full <i>mil</i>”, which for practical purposes is as impossible as 100 <i>mil</i>.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 32a" href="/Bava_Metzia.32a">Babli 32a</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Neziqin</i> 20 p. 323, <i>dR. Simeon bar Ioḥai</i> p. 215, <i>Sifry Deut. 222, Midrash Tannaїm</i> (<i>Midrash Haggadol</i>) 22:4.</i> Putting in order you shall put in order,<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:5" href="/Exodus.23.5"><i>Ex.</i> 23:5</a>.</i>” this is unloading. “Uplifting you shall uplift,<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:4" href="/Deuteronomy.22.4"><i>Deut.</i> 22:4</a>.</i>” this is loading. Rebbi Simeon ben Ioḥai says, just as his unloading is [an obligation] from the Torah, so his loading is from the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Whereas for the Sages he may ask to be paid for helping in loading; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 2:10:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.2.10.4">Note 134</a>.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 32b" href="/Bava_Metzia.32b">Babli 32b</a>; other sources cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 2:10:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.2.10.4">Note 128</a>.</i> If the donkey was a Jew’s but the load a Gentile’s, everybody says that he unloads and loads. If the donkey was a Gentile’s but the load a Jew’s, according to the Sages one neither unloads nor loads. According to Rebbi Simeon one unloads but does not load.

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah and Halakhah are treated more thoroughly in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:4:2-5:16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.4.2-5.16"><i>Horaiot</i> 3:7</a>, 48b (cf. also end of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 6:9" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.6.9">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i>6:9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Horayot 2:5-6" href="/Tosefta_Horayot.2.5-6">Tosephta <i>Horaiot</i> 2:5</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Horayot 2:6" href="/Tosefta_Horayot.2.6">6</a>).</i> Between his lost property and his father’s lost property, his own has precedence. Between his lost property and his teacher’s lost property, his own has precedence. Between his father’s lost property and his teacher’s lost property, his teacher’s lost property has precedence, for his father brought him to the life of this world, but his teacher who taught him wisdom brought him to the life of the future world. But if his father was his teacher’s equal, his father’s lost property has precedence. If his father and his teacher were carrying loads, he unloads his teacher’s and after that his father’s. If his father and his teacher were jailed, he redeems his teacher and after that redeems his father; but if his father was learned he redeems his father and after that redeems his teacher.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Between his lost property and his father’s lost property,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">A different version in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 33a" href="/Bava_Metzia.33a">Babli 33a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 2:30" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).2.30">Tosephta 2:30</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:5:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.5.2-5"><i>Horaiot</i> 2:5</a>. {The latter text is not in the Yerushalmi appended to the <i>editio princeps</i> of the Babli.}</i>: “Who is his teacher who taught him wisdom? The one who taught him first<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">The elementary school teacher who taught him reading and writing.</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jehudah says, the one from whom he received most of his instruction. Rebbi Yose said, anyone who explained a Mishnah to him.” Rav followed Rebbi Meïr, Rebbi Joḥanan Rebbi Jehudah, and Samuel Rebbi Yose.
Rav followed Rebbi Meïr. A person was Rav’s first teacher. He heard that he had died and tore his garment for him.
Rebbi Joḥanan followed Rebbi Jehudah. Rebbi Joḥanan was climbing from Tiberias to Sepphoris when he saw a man descending from there. He asked him, what is new in the big city? He told him, one of the great rabbis died and everbody was running to occupy themselves with him<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">For the burial.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan understood that it was Rebbi Ḥanina. He sent and brought his good Sabbath clothes and tore them<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Moed Katan 25a" href="/Moed_Katan.25a">Babli, <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 25a</a>, “13 silk stolas.”</i>. But did we not state: Any tear which is not in panic is not a tear<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Since really it is forbidden to destroy usable things, tearing one’s clothes in mourning is acceptable only as an expression of acute pain. How could R. Joḥanan make an elaborate ceremony out of it? In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Moed Katan 25a" href="/Moed_Katan.25a">Babli, <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 25a</a>, the statement is amoraic, attributed to Samuel.</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan wanted to do more and show his appreciation. But we do not know whether it was because he was his teacher or because of bad news. There is the case of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba in Sepphoris<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence seems to be incomplete. In the <i>Horaiot</i> text: “R. Ḥananiah (read: R. Ḥanina) was leaning on R. Ḥiyya bar Abba in Sepphoris.”</i>. He saw that everybody was running. He asked, why is everybody running? They told him, Rebbi Joḥanan is preaching in Rebbi Banaia’s study house and everybody is running to hear him. He said, praised be the Merciful Who showed me people running while I am alive. In <i>Agadah</i> I explained him everything except Proverbs and Ecclesiastes<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">This proves that R. Ḥanina was a teacher of R. Joḥanan, even though in legal matters R. Joḥanan was the student of R. Hoshaia.</i>. This means every single student<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Even if he is a student only in homiletics.</i>.
Samuel followed Rebbi Yose. A person explained to Samuel: “Two keys, for one he has to go down to his arm-pit and one opens straight;<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tamid 3:6" href="/Mishnah_Tamid.3.6">Mishnah <i>Tamid</i> 3:6</a>. The doors of the Temple hall could be opened only from the inside. When the door was locked, a Cohen entered from a small side door which led to a guard room. The lock of this side door was hidden; the Cohen had to take the key, reach with his entire arm, up to his arm-pit, behind the partition wall and then insert the key. The second door, from the guard room into the Temple hall, could then be opened by a regular key inserted straight into the keyhole.</i>” that he lowered his hand under his arm-pit until he could insert the key straight. He heard that this one had died and tore his garment for him<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Horaiot</i>: He removed his phylacteries as s sign of mourning.</i>.

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one deposited an animal or vessels with another<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Without paying for the service. The paid trustee has to pay except for an act of God or armed robbery.</i> and they were stolen or lost: If the other paid and did not want to swear<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Since he avoids all oaths because of religious scruples.</i> although they said that the unpaid trustee swears and is absolved<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 7:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.7.5.1">Mishnah 7:8</a>, based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6-8" href="/Exodus.22.6-8"><i>Ex.</i> 22:6–8</a>. He has to swear (1) that the object deposited is not in his possession and (2) that he was not negligent.</i>, in case the thief was found, he pays double restitution<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex.</i> 22:6</a>.</i>; if he slaughtered or sold it, he pays quadruple or quintuple restitution<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:37" href="/Exodus.21.37"><i>Ex.</i> 21:37</a>.</i>. To whom does he pay? To the person with whom it was deposited<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Who had acquired the right to the deposited object by paying for it.</i>.<br>If the other had sworn and did not pay, in case the thief was found, he pays double restitution; if he slaughtered or sold it, he pays quadruple or quintuple restitution. To whom does he pay? To the owner of the deposit.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one deposited an animal or vessels with another,” etc. From where does he bring this<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">That the fine has to be paid to the trustee if the latter had refused to swear.</i>? “If the stolen object be found in his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:3" href="/Exodus.22.3"><i>Ex.</i> 22:3</a>: “If the stolen object be found in his hand … double he shall pay.”</i>.” Do we not know that if the thief is found that he has to pay double<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is explicit in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex.</i> 22:6</a> (for money or vessels), why does it have to be stated in v. 3 for livestock?</i>? Why does the verse say, “double he shall pay”? If the verse is not needed for the case<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">If the objects were stolen from their owner; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:37-22:3" href="/Exodus.21.37-22.3"><i>Ex.</i> 21:37–22:3</a>.</i>, use it for the following subject<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">If they were stolen from the unpaid trustee, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6-8" href="/Exodus.22.6-8"><i>Ex.</i> 22:6–8</a>.</i>. Rebbi [ ]<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The name of an Amora is missing here. The discussion cannot be between Rebbi and the Tanna R. Jehudah (bar Ilai) since Rebbi was a student of R. Jehudah’s son and never used Aramaic in learned discussions. E reads “Rav Naḥman”. S. Lieberman suggests that he might be R. Naḥman from Jaffa [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 53:4" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.53.4"><i>Gen. r.</i> 53(4</a>)], one of the rabbis of Caesarea.</i> went to the “term”<sup class="footnote-marker">12*</sup><i class="footnote">The “term” is the twice-yearly period of public lectures.</i> of Rebbi Yudan and asked this question before him. He answered, refer to what is stated afterwards: “If the other had sworn and did not pay, in case the thief was found, he pays double restitution; if he had slaughtered or sold it, he pays quadruple or quintuple restitution. To whom does he pay? To the owner of the deposit.” Should he not pay to the person with whom it was deposited<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Since one has to assume that the thief has to pay to the person from whom he stole. Then the trustee would have to pay the restitution to the object’s owner but could retain the fine for himself.</i>? Rebbi Nasa in the name of Rebbi Jonah: “Alive, double he shall pay<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:3" href="/Exodus.22.3"><i>Ex.</i> 22:3</a>.</i>.” To the place where the capital goes, there the double fine goes<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since it says “double”, not “he has to pay the value of the theft and then a fine in an equal amount”, it follows that double restitution is one payment to one recipient.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Eleazar; Rebbi Nasa adds in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: Not only when he paid, but even when he stated his willingness to pay it is as if he had paid<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 34a" href="/Bava_Metzia.34a">Babli 34a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 37a" href="/Bava_Metzia.37a">37a</a>. Since by necessity the transaction between owner and trustee happened before the thief was apprehended, when neither one of the two were in possession, the right to ownership is transferred by agreement, rather than by actual payment.</i>.
If he said, I am paying, one may suspect that possibly he took it for himself<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">One forces him to swear a rabbinic oath that the article is not in his possession.</i>. If he said, I am swearing, but then he saw that other oaths were added<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Since once a person is required to swear by biblical standards, the opposing party can make him swear on any other claims even if those claims alone would not be sufficient to force an oath: <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.5.3"><i>Soṭah</i> 2:6, Notes 166–169</a>.</i> and he said, I am paying, one is suspicious<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The oath required from every non-payor. For rabbinic oaths one cannot force additions; cf. <i>Rosh Šebuot</i>7, end of Section 18 (Alfasi <i>Šebuot</i> 7, # 1186.)</i>. Rebbi Yose said, the Torah did not impose an oath on him to restrict him but to ease for him; for if he wants to pay, he shall pay, and if he wants to swear, he shall swear<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:7-8" href="/Exodus.22.7-8"><i>Ex.</i> 22:7–8</a>. R. Yose denies the possibility of the imposition of a rabbinic oath if there is no case for a biblical oath.</i>. If he had witnesses that it was forcibly stolen<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Then he does not pay without swearing an oath.</i>, to that case refers what Rebbi Eleazar said: If somebody sells his claims of fines to someone else, he did not do anything<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">If the trustee pays even though he is not required to swear in order to be freed from payment, he did not acquire the right to claim double restitution from the thief. If the thief is caught, he has to pay double to the owner of the object who then will return to the trustee what the latter had paid and retain the amount of the fine for himself.</i>. If he had witnesses that it was stolen because of negligence, he comes under the category of “he shall pay”<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex.</i> 22:8</a> explicitly obligates the trustee for damages caused by his negligence. In this case, the Mishnah does not apply and there is no statement that the claims were transmitted to the trustee.</i>. If afterwards the stolen object was found, to whom does [the thief] pay? To the first, or to the second, or to both of them?

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody lease<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 7:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.7.5.1">Mishnah 7:8</a> states that the lessee pays for what is lost or stolen but not for what is robbed, or by natural causes is broken or dies.</i> a cow from another person and then lends<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The borrower pays for anything that happens to the object he rented (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 7:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.7.5.1">Mishnah 7:8</a>).</i> it out to a third person. If it died a natural death, the lessee shall swear that it died a natural death and the borrower has to pay the lessee. Rebbi Yose said, how can this one treat another person’s cow as his merchandise<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Why should the lessee get the value of the cow and its owner get nothing?</i>? But [the value of] the cow shall be returned to its owner.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody lease a cow from another person,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is a fragment from a lengthy discussion in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.5.3"><i>Ketubot</i>9:5, Notes 128–145</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:4:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.4.10"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:4, Note 451</a>.</i> But does he have permission to lend it? Did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state: “The borrower cannot lend, nor the lessee lease, nor the borrower lease, nor the lessee lend, unless he received permission from the owners.” Rebbi La said in the name of Rebbi Yannai: Only if he gave permission to lease. And here, only if he gave permission to let him be a steward<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence does not belong here; it refers to the question in <i>Ketubot</i> whether a wife who has been entrusted by her husband with the care of his properties may delegate her duties to her sons.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu asked: If the owners borrowed it and it died of natural causes, should the lessee swear that it died a natural death and the borrower pay the renter<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">If the owner is the borrower, the rule of the anonymous majority leads to a paradoxical result.</i>? Rebbi Abinna said, if they ate it, they ate their own property. “Rebbi Yose said, how can this one treat another person’s cow as his merchandise? But [the value of] the cow shall be returned to its owner<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, practice has to follow R. Yose.</i>.”

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one says to two people, I robbed a <i>mina</i> from one of you but I do not know who it was or, the father of one of you deposited a <i>mina</i> with me but I do not know who it is, has to pay a <i>mina</i> to each of them since he himself confessed.<br>If two people deposited with a third party, one a <i>mina</i> and one 200 [<i>zuz</i>]. Each of them says, the 200 are mine. He shall give each of them a <i>mina</i> and the remainder shall be deposited until Elijah comes<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli and all independent Mishnah mss., R. Yose’s objection (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.3.3.1">Mishnah 6</a>) is also quoted here.<br>The prophet Elijah, precursor of the Messiah, is supposed to know the answer to all unsolved questions.</i>.<br>Similarly two vessels, one worth a <i>mina,</i> the other worth 1’000 <i>zuz.</i> Each of them says, the valuable one is mine. He shall give the small vessel to one of them, from the value of the large one he shall give the value of the small one to the other and the remainder shall be deposited until Elijah comes. Rebbi Yose said, if that be so, what did the trickster lose? But everything shall be deposited until Elijah comes<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.3.3.1">Mishnaiot 5</a> and 6 are not discussed in the Yerushalmi, it is impossible to know its position relative to all modifications made by the Babli in their interpretation.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one says to two people, I robbed,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This Halakhah is a sketch, or a first draft, of the discussion in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 15:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.15.10"><i>Yebamot</i>15:10, Notes 153–178</a>. The problem discussed is the discrepancy between <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.3.3.1">Mishnah 4</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 15:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.15.9.1">Mishnah <i>Yebamot</i> 15:10</a> which states that if somebody repents after having robbed one of a group of five people but does not know who it was, and each of them claims to be the victim, R. Tarphon says that he puts the amount of the robbery before them and lets them fight about the distribution. R. Aqiba agrees that this is the legal situation but holds that the robber is not free in the eyes of Heaven unless he pay the amount of the robbery to each of them. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.3.3.1">Mishnah 4</a> contains no hint of this controversy; we have to assume that it represents the opinions of both R. Tarphon and R. Aqiba. The question is what is the difference between the two situations? The discussion is explained there in detail.</i> Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Here if they complain, there if they are silent. Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Here, if witnesses know; there, if no witnesses know. Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: Here if he swore; there if he did not swear. There, they say in the name of Rav: Here if he swore; there if he did not swear<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Yebamot</i> 15:10, Note 157.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah wanted to say, he should have kept silent and not confessed. Rebbi Yasa wanted to say, he should have kept silent and not have sworn. Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he swore, he should have appointed an officer of the court. Rebbi Joḥanan seems to say, an officer of the court determined that one was a robber, not a court which determined that one was robbed. Other Tannaїm stated, a court which determined that one was robbed, [as it was stated, Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar said, a court which determined that one was robbed,]<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Addition of E; justified by its parallel in <i>Yebamot.</i></i> not a court which determined that one was a robber. Rebbi Ila said, we also have stated that the rules are identical for robbery, intercourse<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 15:9:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.15.9.2"><i>Yebamot</i> 15:9, Note 152</a>. A man became betrothed by intercourse (violating a rabbinic prohibition) to one of two women. He does not remember who it was; they both claim to be his wife. Following R. Aqiba, he has to pay two <i>ketubot.</i></i>, and loan<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">In the unlikely case that somebody took a loan from one of two people who both claim to be his creditors and he does not remember whose debtor he is.</i>. Everbody agrees about the deposit mentioned in the Mishnah, from the following: “If he said to two people, I robbed a mina from one of you but I do not know which one of you it was.”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody deposits produce with another person, even if it is becoming unusable<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Either being spoiled, or being eaten by animals, or becoming ritually prohibited.</i> he should not touch it<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli explains that this only refers to produce of the depositor’s own farm; for trade produce the anonymous Tanna will agree with Rabban Simeon.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, he shall sell it under the supervision of the court<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">To insure himself against future claims by the depositor.</i> as returning lost property to its owner.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> If somebody deposits produce with another person,” etc. Rebbi Abbahu said, if you would say that he should not touch them even if they are going to spoil, he will invent situations<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">He explains R. Simeon ben Gamliel’s ruling. If the trustee is prohibited from doing anything, he might invent a story that the produce did spoil when in reality he took it. But a sale under the supervision of the court avoids this.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Jacob in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Practice follows Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 38b" href="/Bava_Metzia.38b">Babli 38b</a>, rejected there by the Babylonian authorities.</i>. For example, Rebbi Joḥanan from Ḥaqoq deposited with the Elder Rebbi Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 13a" href="/Pesachim.13a">Babli, <i>Pesaḥim</i> 13a</a>, a person deposited with R. Joḥanan from Ḥaqoq. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 1:5:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.1.5.6"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 1:6</a> (27c l. 74).</i> a saddle bag<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δισάκκιον, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:5:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.5.15"><i>Berakhot</i>3:5, Note 239</a>.</i> full of leavened material. He came to ask Rebbi, who said it should be sold under the surpervision of the court when it would have to be destroyed<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Leavened material in possession of a Jew becomes permanently prohibited for usufruct at noon of the 14th of Nisan. Such material held in deposit should not be touched until 11 o’clock on that day, for the owner might come and dispose of it himself. But at 11 o’clock it should be sold to a Gentile lest it become worthless.</i>. A person deposited with Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Ashi an amphora full of <i>kutaḥ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">A Babylonian kind of soft cheese curdled by mold growing on old bread. This cheese is considered leavened material. [(N. B.) Might the acoustic similarity of the name of <i>cottage</i> cheese (<i>Ricotta</i>), soft cheese comparable to <i>kutaḥ,</i> be more than a coincidence? (E.G.)]</i>. He came to ask Rav, who said it should be sold under the surpervision of the court when otherwise it would have to be destroyed.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody deposits produce with another person<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">It is understood that the recipient stores the depositor’s produce with his own. He has to return a given quantity, not exactly the produce given to him. If it is understood that he has to keep the other farmer’s produce separate, he simply returns the original produce in the original vessels without calculation.</i>, the latter returns it deducting losses<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Grain eaten by mice and other pests.</i>, for wheat and rice 9/2 <i>qab</i> per <i>kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">One <i>kor</i> is 30 <i>se’ah</i>; 1 <i>se’ah</i> is 24 <i>log.</i> The loss ratio is 9/1440 = 1/16 or .625%.</i>, for barley and millet nine <i>qab</i> per <i>kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">1.25%.</i>, for spelt and linseed three <i>se‘ah</i> per <i>kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">10%.</i>; everything proportional to quantity and time elapsed<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The ratios indicated are per year.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri said, do the rats care, do they not eat from much as from little<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">This is incorrect; they breed quickly if there is an abundance of food.</i>? But he only deducts what corresponds to one <i>kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">He accepts proportionality in time but not in quantity. For him the amounts stated in the Mishnah are upper limits of what may be deducted in one year.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, if it was a large quantity, he does not deduct because of the surplus<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">In his place, quantities over 1 <i>kor,</i> 0.384 m<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup>, are measured with an ample supplement which should take care of the losses.</i>.<br>He deducts one sixth of wine; Rebbi Jehudah says a fifth. He deducts three log of oil per hundred, a log and a half lees<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Fragments of olives swimming in the oil, to be filtered out.</i>, a log and a half absorption<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Oil absorbed by the walls of the porous clay vessels in which the oil is stored.</i>. If it was refined oil, he does not deduct lees; if the vessels were old, he does not deduct absorption. Rebbi Jehudah says, also if somebody sells refined oil during the entire year, [the buyer] accepts a log and a half lees per hundred<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">If the oil is sold at the going rate for unrefined oil, the seller may deliver refined oil, delivering only 98.5% of the quantity contracted for.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody deposits produce with another person,” etc. Rebbi Immi said, they stated this at harvest time<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The percentages are computed for a full year, from one harvest to the next; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 40a" href="/Bava_Metzia.40a">Babli 40a</a>.</i>. There, they say that these evil rats, if they see plenty of produce, call their friends and eat with them; Rebbi Joḥanan said, they waste, they squander<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">All give reasons why practice cannot follow R. Joḥanan ben Nuri.</i>.
“He deducts one sixth of wine; Rebbi Jehudah says a fifth.” Rav Oshaia said, if he did not tell him; “I am providing you with refined oil the entire year.” But if he said so, he has to provide him with refined oil<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">If he simply sold “oil” at the going rate, he can deliver a slightly smaller quantity of refined oil. But if he advertised “refined oil”, he has to deliver the full measure of refined oil, irrespective of price.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody deposits an amphora with another person without insisting on a separate place<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Then the trustee may move the amphora using regular care.</i>, when it was moved and broke in his hand, if [the moving] was for his needs, he is liable; for its needs he is not liable. If it broke after it was put down, whether it was for his or its needs, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Unless the trustee was negligent in moving.</i>. If its owner insisted on a separate place<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Then the trustee is not authorized to move the amphora except in an emergency.</i> but it was moved and broke, whether in his hand or after it was put down, for his needs he is liable, for its needs he is not liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody deposits an amphora with another person,” etc. Did not Rebbi Eleazar say, to what can the situation be compared? One who steals an amphora from another person’s wine cellar. Even if the owner does not realize the theft, it is necessary that he know of its return<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 10:33" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).10.33">Tosephta <i>Bava qamma</i> 10:33</a>. Applied to the case in which the trustee has agreed to keep the amphora at a designated place, if later he moves the amphora from that place (except in case of emergency to save it from destruction), he is a thief and even if he returns the amphora to its place he remains fully liable for anything that happens to the amphora until he informs its owner that he did move it and returned it.</i>. He said, there is a difference because [the trustee]<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Addition of E. Even without the addition it is clear that the trustee is referred to.</i> has authority over it<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Since the trustee has emergency powers over the amphora, if he moves it he is not a thief.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, some say because the trustee has authority over it. What means “for its needs”? If the owner insisted on a specified place, at another place, whether it is for his needs or its needs, he is (not)<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Both mss. offer the same scribal error, “not liable” in place of “liable”.</i> liable. There are those who say that the trustee is not authorized; there is no difference whether at this place or at another place; for his needs he is liable, for its needs he is not liable.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody deposits coins with another person who binds them together and carries them on his back<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Where they are easily stolen.</i> or gives them to his underage son or daughter, or locks them insufficiently, he is liable because he did not take care the way people take care. But if he took care the way people take care, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Taking care of money is no different from taking care of any other object. If his care would have been enough for a vessel, it is enough for coins.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody deposits coins with another person,” etc. Rebbi Yose said: One understands from this that in case a man gave <i>plyṭvryn</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">E reads first פליסורין, then כליסורין. The first reading is supported by the text quoted by Menaḥem di Lonzano. S. Lieberman reads this as Greek κλυστήριον, “a clyster”, which fits the context. The explanations proposed by earlier commentators are not reasonable.</i> to another in the market place and that one handed it over to his underage son or daughter, if they were stolen or lost he is not liable since I say that nobody is in the market place because of <i>plyṭvryn</i><sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">If private matters are handed over by the depositor in an inappropriate manner, the trustee is not required to behave appropriately.</i>.
When did they say that the unpaid trustee swears and is absolved? If he took care of it the way caretakers do: He locked as he should, tied as he should, put it into his money-belt<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">E reads instead of באפונדתו (from the Latin <i>funda</i> “money belt”) באפר אפורין which S. Lieberman reads as Greek παράφορον “loose belt”.</i>, tied it into his poncho and kept it in front of him, put it into a cupboard, a chest, or a safety box; if they were stolen or lost he is required to swear but not liable to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">73a</sup><i class="footnote">The unpaid trustee does not swear automatically if the object entrusted to him is lost; the judges have to find that he is entitled to swear.</i>. If there are witnesses that he did so, he even is freed from the oath. He locked carelessly, tied carelessly, put it on his back<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">E has an addition: “He hung it outside his money-belt, tied it into his scarf and put it on his back.”</i>, left it on his roof; if it was stolen he is liable for payment. If he put it at a place where he usually keeps his own things, if it was kept safe as it should have been he is not liable, otherwise he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">A person careless with his own belongings is not absolved from liability if he proves that he treated others’ property no worse than his own.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “If somebody deposits coins with a money changer, if they are tied together, he may not use them, if loose, he may use them. With a householder, whether tied together or loose he may not use them<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah in the Babli and the independent Mishnah mss. adds: Anybody permitted to use the money is liable if it is lost; anybody not permitted to use it is not liable (beyond the usual liability of the unpaid trustee) for coins.</i>. The storekeeper is like a householder, the words of Rebbi Meïr; Rebbi Jehudah says, like a money changer.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody deposits coins with a money changer,” etc. Rav Huna said that Rebbi Jeremiah<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian, known as Rav Jeremiah in the Babli.</i> asked how<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">What is the meaning of “tied together” which prohibits the money changer from using the coins. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 43a" href="/Bava_Metzia.43a">Babli, 43a</a>, a statement attributed by different mss. to different authors states that only money deposited under seal cannot be used.</i>? Tied under seal or tied together without seal. If you say under seal, without seal he may. If you say without seal, so much more under seal.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a person appropriates a deposit<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Illegally.</i>, the House of Shammai say, he shall be hit by less or more<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">He pays the larger of its values at the time of deposit and at the time it should have been delivered to the depositor.</i>, but the House of Hillel say, [at the price] at the time of delivery; Rebbi Aqiba says, at the time of claim. If somebody intends to take the deposit, the House of Shammai hold him liable but the House of Hillel say, he is liable only from the moment he takes it<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah in the <i>editio princeps</i> of the Babli adds: “For it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:7" href="/Exodus.22.7"><i>Ex.</i> 22:7</a>): If he did not stretch out his hand to his neighbor’s possession.” It is not in the mss. of the Babli or the Mishnah. The verse implies that theft occurs only by a thief’s act of acquisition, such as moving the object.</i>. How is this? If he tilted the amphora<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">But the amphora never lost contact with the floor on which it was standing and no part in contact with the floor changed position.</i> and took a <i>quartarius,</i> and then it broke, he pays only for a <i>quartarius</i><sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Because the trustee-thief acquired only the <i>quartarius</i> of wine.</i>. If he lifted it<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Thereby acquiring the [responsability for the] amphora.</i> and took a <i>quartarius,</i> and then it broke, he pays for all.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a person appropriates a deposit,” etc. So is the Mishnah: At the time of claim in court<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The “time of claim” mentioned in the Mishnah is not the time of the claim of the owner on the trustee for return of his property but the moment the case is brought to court. Since the court is supposed to hear each case on the day it was brought, the value of the object can be determined by asking local dealers.</i>. Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: Practice follows Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli agrees in the name of Samuel, 43b.</i>. Even though the House of Hillel disagree with him? Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya: It happened that there was a case and Rebbi instructed following Rebbi Aqiba. Rav Hoshaia said, if there are no witnesses. But if there are witnesses, everybody agrees with Rebbi Aqiba’s position<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 43b" href="/Bava_Metzia.43b">Babli, 43b</a>, even R. Aqiba agrees that if there are witnesses to the theft, the moment of the theft determines the value.</i>. There came a case before Rebbi Nasa and he instructed following Rebbi Aqiba.
And what is the reason of the House of Shammai? “Any talk of criminality.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex.</i> 22:8</a>. The same argument is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 44a" href="/Bava_Metzia.44a">Babli 44a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 42b" href="/Kiddushin.42b"><i>Qiddušin</i> 42b</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Ioḥai</i> p. 202.</i>” How do the House of Hillel explain “any talk of criminality’? I could think only if he acts alone<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The trustee is guilty in civil law even if the taking of the depositor’s property was done by the trustee’s agent; <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Ioḥai</i> p. 202.<br>This is explained at the start of a lengthy text in <i>Šebuot</i> 8:1 (38c l. 37 – 38d l. 1) which really is a commentary on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 3:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.3.9.1">Mishnah <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 3:13</a>. E copies this text here; R. Ḥ. Y. D. Azulai (פתח עינים) published a slightly shorter text from a ms. of R. Menaḥem Lonzano who copied it from a ms. of Yerushalmi <i>Bava meṣia‘.</i> E’s text is an exact copy of <i>Šebuot,</i> and Azulai’s text is identical with these two except for some omissions of repetitions. It seems that these texts are later additions, not original to the text of <i>Neziqin</i> nor in its spirit. The present edition therefore will treat these texts as copies from <i>Šebuot.</i></i>, etc.

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">A general reference to this Mishnah and the first paragraph of the Halakhah is in D. Sperber, <i>Roman Palestine 200–400, Money and Prices,</i> Ramat Gan 1974; Chapters XI,XIV.</i> Silver acquires gold but gold does not acquire silver<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">It is accepted in rabbinic law (in the name of R. Yannai in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:2:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.2.2-7">Halakhah 4:2</a>, in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 46b" href="/Bava_Metzia.46b">Babli 46b</a> in the name of R. Joḥanan) that in Biblical law money acquires both ownership and possession though this is denied in the other Yerushalmi Tractates in the name of R. Joḥanan, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 8:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.8.1.4"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 8:1 Note 15</a>, <i>Erubin</i>7:11 24d l. 3). But it is universally accepted that in rabbinic practice payment only establishes a <i>claim</i> to ownership and possession, not a <i>fact.</i> This is to avoid fraudulent transactions in which the seller sells non-existent goods and then claims that they were lost, e. g., by fire, between sale and delivery when they already were owned by the buyer but not yet in his possession. On the other hand, in a barter transaction taking possession by one party automatically transfers both ownership and possession to the other party (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:6:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.6.2-6"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:6</a>). If a person loses because the other party who took money in a commercial transaction then refuses to go through with the sale and offers to refund the money, he can ask the court to publicly curse the defaulting party but he has no legal way to force completion of the sale (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>). But if the buyer took possession of the merchandise, then the sale is completed; the seller can force payment in court and is not required to take the article back. This means that transactions involving money are considered a kind of barter in which coins always are passive but never active objects of barter.<br>There is no doubt that gold or silver bars are merchandise, objects of trade and barter. The question is about coins. If gold coins are exchanged for silver coins and vice-versa, the question is which of the species are considered as merchandise and which as coins in the transaction. The Babli and all sources depending on it (Mishnah and Tosephta mss.) state that “Gold acquires silver but silver does not acquire gold,” the opposite of the Yerushalmi. The Halakhah states that originally, the Yerushalmi Mishnah was identical with the Babli; the change can be dated to the early Third Century.<br>D. Sperber has argued convincingly that this is not a question of gold or silver standard since both the Roman and the Parthian, later Persian, empires actually were on a gold-and-silver standard. But the Persian empire never experienced the disastrous manipulation of silver coinage which started with Caracalla and ended with the hyper-inflation of the military anarchy, only to be brought under control by Diocletian’s currency reform. By contrast, even the worst Roman emperors did not adulterate gold coinage (if they minted gold at all.) Therefore, in Babylonia silver coin was the common vehicle of trade while gold coin was used only in very large transactions or as a vehicle for hoarding. This means that silver coin was “money” but gold coin was “merchandise” even when gold coin did not trade at an agio over the official ratio. But in the Roman empire in the period of formulation of the Mishnah (roughly 190–230) silver coins changed from dependable vehicle of commerce to objects to be disposed of as quickly as possible; silver became a merchandise relative to gold coin.</i>. Brass acquires silver but silver does not acquire brass<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In good times, brass coins are local currency only which cannot be used at far-away places; they are tokens rather than coins. When tokens are exchanged for coins, the tokens are the merchandise. In bad times, when “silver” coins were brass coins slightly washed in a silver solution, brass coins did not exist.</i>. Bad coins<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Coins taken out of circulation; they are not money in the legal sense.</i> acquire good ones but good ones do not acquire bad ones. A blank<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Metal disks ready to be stamped in the mint. They are not money until stamped.</i> acquires a coin but a coin does not acquire a blank. Movables acquire coins but coins do not acquire movables<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">It is accepted in rabbinic law (in the name of R. Yannai in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:2:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.2.2-7">Halakhah 4:2</a>, in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 46b" href="/Bava_Metzia.46b">Babli 46b</a> in the name of R. Joḥanan) that in Biblical law money acquires both ownership and possession though this is denied in the other Yerushalmi Tractates in the name of R. Joḥanan, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 8:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.8.1.4"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 8:1 Note 15</a>, <i>Erubin</i>7:11 24d l. 3). But it is universally accepted that in rabbinic practice payment only establishes a <i>claim</i> to ownership and possession, not a <i>fact.</i> This is to avoid fraudulent transactions in which the seller sells non-existent goods and then claims that they were lost, e. g., by fire, between sale and delivery when they already were owned by the buyer but not yet in his possession. On the other hand, in a barter transaction taking possession by one party automatically transfers both ownership and possession to the other party (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:6:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.6.2-6"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:6</a>). If a person loses because the other party who took money in a commercial transaction then refuses to go through with the sale and offers to refund the money, he can ask the court to publicly curse the defaulting party but he has no legal way to force completion of the sale (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>). But if the buyer took possession of the merchandise, then the sale is completed; the seller can force payment in court and is not required to take the article back. This means that transactions involving money are considered a kind of barter in which coins always are passive but never active objects of barter.<br>There is no doubt that gold or silver bars are merchandise, objects of trade and barter. The question is about coins. If gold coins are exchanged for silver coins and vice-versa, the question is which of the species are considered as merchandise and which as coins in the transaction. The Babli and all sources depending on it (Mishnah and Tosephta mss.) state that “Gold acquires silver but silver does not acquire gold,” the opposite of the Yerushalmi. The Halakhah states that originally, the Yerushalmi Mishnah was identical with the Babli; the change can be dated to the early Third Century.<br>D. Sperber has argued convincingly that this is not a question of gold or silver standard since both the Roman and the Parthian, later Persian, empires actually were on a gold-and-silver standard. But the Persian empire never experienced the disastrous manipulation of silver coinage which started with Caracalla and ended with the hyper-inflation of the military anarchy, only to be brought under control by Diocletian’s currency reform. By contrast, even the worst Roman emperors did not adulterate gold coinage (if they minted gold at all.) Therefore, in Babylonia silver coin was the common vehicle of trade while gold coin was used only in very large transactions or as a vehicle for hoarding. This means that silver coin was “money” but gold coin was “merchandise” even when gold coin did not trade at an agio over the official ratio. But in the Roman empire in the period of formulation of the Mishnah (roughly 190–230) silver coins changed from dependable vehicle of commerce to objects to be disposed of as quickly as possible; silver became a merchandise relative to gold coin.</i>. This is the rule: all movables acquire one another<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.6.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:6</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Silver acquires gold,” etc. This is a summary of the matter: Anything worth less that the other acquires the other. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ashi said, who stated this? Rebbi Simeon ben Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 44a" href="/Bava_Metzia.44a">Babli 44a</a>.</i>. His father told him, change your opinion and state the following: “Gold acquires silver.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The version accepted in Babylonia sees the world as it should be; not the temporary state at the beginning military anarchy in the Roman empire when the currency was manipulated.</i>” He told him, I do not change my opinion since when your faculties were unimpaired, you instructed me to state: “Silver acquires gold.” The word of Rebbi implies that gold is like produce<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">It has the power of merchandise to transfer possession and finalize a deal.</i>. The Mishnah implies that silver is like produce<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">It has the power of merchandise to transfer possession and finalize a deal.</i>. [Rebbi Ḥiyya’s <i>baraita</i> implies that gold is like produce<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 3:13" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).3.13">Tosephta 3:13</a>.</i>, but his word implies that silver is like produce, as in the following:]<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Addition of E. The characterization of the Tosephta as Galilean might raise doubts as to the genuineness of the addition.</i> The elder Rebbi Ḥiyya’s daughter lent denars<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Gold denars.</i> to Rav. She came and asked her father, who told her: Take from him good and full weight denars<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 44b" href="/Bava_Metzia.44b">Babli 44b</a>.
