The problem here is the prohibition of taking interest, which is defined as “payment for waiting” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 65a" href="/Bava_Metzia.65a">Babli 65a</a>). Therefore, it is forbidden to lend merchandise on condition to receive the same amount of merchandise in return at a specified future time since in the meantime the value of the merchandise might have increased and the lender would be repaid more in monetary value than what he lent out. It is required to determine the monetary value of the articles to be lent before they are transferred. The only exceptions are (a) short term loans and (b) bridge loans where the borrower already owns the borrowed items while they are stored at another locality. If R. Ḥiyya holds that silver is merchandise, how can he allow his daughter to lend gold coin against gold coin? The Babli explicitly states that during the term of the loan, gold rose in price against silver.</i>. Do we learn from Rebbi Ḥiyya’s daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The story is irrelevant for the problem of this Halakhah since Rav states in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:5:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.5.8">Halakhah 5:7 (Note 135</a>) that between close relatives the taking of interest is permitted. Rav was the student of his uncle R. Ḥiyya until he returned to Babylonia in 218; he was R. Ḥiyya’s daughter’s cousin. All we can learn is that Rav’s statement is really R. Ḥiyya’s. [S. Abramson in <i>Sinai</i> 89 (1981) pp. 217–218.] It is not stated whether the lender was Pazi or Martha.</i>? Rebbi Idi said, also Abba, Samuel’s father, asked before Rebbi: May one lend denars against denars<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Gold denars.</i>? He answered him, it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The answer of a young R. Jehudah ben Rabban Simeon.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, also Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish both instruct: It is permitted to lend denars against denars. <i>Qerat</i> against <i>qerat</i> is permitted, <i>lekan</i> against <i>lekan</i> is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Sperber’s analysis (<i>l. c.</i> pp. 92–93) makes it probable that the last sentence is not R. Joḥanan’s and R. Simeon ben Laqish’s, but is the editor’s deduction from the preceding statement. He notes that <i>qeraṭ</i> can either be the Greek κεράτιον, a gold weight, 1/1728 <i>libra aurei,</i> in Dioclation’s system equal in value with the silver <i>siliqua,</i> 1/24 <i>solidus,</i> or Syriac קרטא, <i>quarta</i>(<i>pars sicli</i>), one denar. Since we are dealing with coins, the preferred meaning is that of <i>siliqua,</i> an honest silver coin which did not exist during R. Joḥanan’s lifetime. <i>Lekan</i> was proposed in <i>Ma‘aśer Šeni</i> 4:1, Note 22, as “small measure or weight”, i. e. local small change which in the Mishnah had been defined as merchandise when compared with silver. {The computation of R. Eliahu Fulda in <i>Ma‘aśer Šeni</i> 4:1, who wanted to prove that the <i>lekan</i> was 1/62.5 of a <i>solidus,</i> was already shown to be in error by H. Y. Sheftel [Encyclopedia of biblical and rabbinic coins, measures, weights, areas, and time, Berdiczew 1907 (Hebrew)]}.</i>.
There, we have stated: If anything is given instead of money, if one entered in possession, the other is obligated for its exchange<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.1.1">Note 2</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.6.1"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:6, Note 521</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, they stated this only about an ox for a cow, or a donkey for an ox; but heap against heap one did not acquire. Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: Even heaps among heaps he did acquire<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.6.2"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:6, Note 525</a>. If one of the parties took possession, the other party automatically obtained possession of the exchanged property.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Mina in the name of Rav: One who exchanged אברוקלון against אמברוקלון did acquire<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The reading of R. Ḥananel and <i>‘Arukh</i> is: One who exchanged אמבורקלין against אמבורקלין did acquire. <i>Arukh</i> explains as “bundles of sheets” which in Italian (dialect of Rome) would be called ברוקלי (variants ברוקלון, ברוקלו) or (תרצילו, טורצלי, תרוצולו, תורצלו) .טרצילו. The second word is identified by Krauss in <i>Additamenta ad librum Aruch Completum</i> as <i>turzello.</i> M. Sachs (J. Levy, S. Krauss) identifies אמבורוקלון as Latin <i>involucrum</i> “wrapper; covering; envelope”, from <i>involvo</i> “to wrap up, roll up.”. <i>Turzello,</i> from Latin <i>tortus</i> “a twisting, winding”, from <i>torqueo</i> “to twist, to turn” is an acceptable translation of <i>involucrum.</i> In this interpretation, the statement implies that the laws of barter also apply if something is exchanged against an object of the same kind. It is difficult to understand why this should not be so.<br>H. Y. D. Azulay, in his פתח עיניים, quotes from the ms. of R. Menaḥem di Lonzano בדיקלין באמה בדיקלין. E: אמלוקנין באמבוליקין.<br>However, Maimonides connects the statement with the later Mishnaiot whose subject is the cancellation of a sale because of overcharging by the seller or underpaying by the buyer. He reads the statement as meaning that a barter is concluded the moment one of the parties takes possession of the object coming to him, and no legal recourse exists for the party realizing that he made a bad bargain. He must hold that אברוקלון and אמבורוקלון are two different objects. It is possible that he reads the two words as “needle” and “silk cloth” since he writes (<i>Mekhirah</i> 13:1): “One who barters vessels agains vessels or animals against animals, even a needle for silk cloth or a kid goat for a horse, has no claim of overcharging since he might prefer a needle to silk cloth.” The horse is specifically exempt from the rules of overcharging in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.6.2">Halakhah 4, Note 132</a>. (Ravad objects and thinks that Maimonides misunderstood the Yerushalmi.) The commentary <i>Migdal ‘Oz</i> (R. Šem-Ṭob Gabbai) readsאמבורקלין אמטרקלין, but gives no explanation of the words beyond noting that these clearly denote different objects.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">This text is an outline of the detailed argument in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.6.3"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:6, Notes 532–542</a>.</i> Rebbi Ze‘ira<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Qiddušin,</i> R. Abba. Since R. Ze‘ira objects to the statement in both texts, and the <i>Qiddušin</i> text is reproduced in the next paragraph, the attribution here is incorrect.</i>, Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: One had a cow and one a donkey. They exchanged one for the other. The donkey’s owner took the cow. When the cow’s owner came to take the donkey and found that it had a fracture, the donkey’s owner had to bring proof that his donkey was well at the moment he took the cow. Anybody who does not agree to this does not know anything about civil law. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, I do not agree, I do not know anything about civil law. There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.7.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 7:9</a>. After the definitive wedding, the husband discovers that his bride has a bodily defect and divorces her for that. He does not want to pay her <i>ketubah.</i> A <i>prima facie</i> argument is that the defect happened where it was discovered. Therefore, if the bride was still in her father’s house, the father has to prove that at the time of the preliminary wedding his daughter was without blemish. If the bride now is in her husband’s house, the husband has to prove that she was already blemished at the preliminary wedding when he agreed to be financially responsible for her. Similarly, the rule is that if the donkey was in his first owner’s stable when the buyer found it with a broken leg that the stable’s owner has to prove that it was healthy at the time he took possession of the cow.</i>: “If she had bodily defects, as long as she was in her father’s house,” etc. Rebbi Ḥuna, Rebbi Phineas, and Rebbi Ḥizqiah went to visit Rebbi Yose to sharpen wits. They quoted the statement<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.7.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 7:9</a>. After the definitive wedding, the husband discovers that his bride has a bodily defect and divorces her for that. He does not want to pay her <i>ketubah.</i> A <i>prima facie</i> argument is that the defect happened where it was discovered. Therefore, if the bride was still in her father’s house, the father has to prove that at the time of the preliminary wedding his daughter was without blemish. If the bride now is in her husband’s house, the husband has to prove that she was already blemished at the preliminary wedding when he agreed to be financially responsible for her. Similarly, the rule is that if the donkey was in his first owner’s stable when the buyer found it with a broken leg that the stable’s owner has to prove that it was healthy at the time he took possession of the cow.</i> and he told them, look at the next statement: “Once she entered the husband’s domain, the husband has to prove.” Would not the father have to prove? But you are saying that the husband has to prove; here also, the owner has to prove.
<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is similar to one in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.6.4"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:6, Notes 543–549</a>. The initial sentence is out of place in this paragraph; it should be the start of the preceding one, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.1.4">Note 21</a>. The missing starting sentence would indicate that the parties to a trade determined the monetary value of the animals traded. No money actually changed hands because they agreed that the prices of the items exchanged were equal. The question now arises whether the transaction has to be classified as sell-and-buy, in which case each party has to take possession by an act of acquisition, or as barter, when the taking of possession of one party automatically transfers possession also to the other party.</i>(Rebbi Abba, Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: One had a cow and one a donkey. They exchanged them.) The donkey’s owner took the cow; he did not acquire it<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously, this statement does not fit the preceding sentence; it refers to the statement of R. Abba, Rav Hamnuna, Rav Ada bar Aḥawa in the name of Rav (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:6:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.6.2-6"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:6</a>, 60d l. 75, Note 543): A cow was originally sold for a fixed sum. Then the buyer had second thoughts and offered the seller a donkey instead of money. If now they exchange the animals, it is not barter. Since the first to take the other’s animal takes something for which he did not pay, and the sale was not a credit sale, he cannot have acquired the animal until the other party also takes his. The only problem left open is whether the second party also has to execute a conscious act of acquisition; this is required by R. Yose but waved by R. Abba.</i>. Would the donkey be acquired? Rebbi Abba said, he acquired. Rebby Yasa said, he did not acquire. Rebbi Mana said, there is a situation in which Rebbi Yose would agree: A man said to another, I want to sell my cow. He asked him, for how much? He answered, for eight denars. He went and gave him a draft on his banker<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">For the unexplained word טרפוניטיה, <i>Migdal Oz</i> (<i>loc. cit.</i> Note 19) reads טרפיזטא τραπεζίτης “banker”, the Greek equivalent of Mishnaic שׁוּלְחָנִי. In <i>Qiddušin</i> it simply says, “he gave him a draft for money, פריטייא”; the last term might refer to Greek πρατήριον, τό “place for selling, market” (E. G.)</i>. In the morning he went and found him open for business. He asked him, what are you doing here? He answered, I want to collect the denars in the draft I have on you. He asked him, what do you want to buy with them? He answered, a donkey. He told him, your donkey is with me. If either of them took possession, the other did not acquire but each animal separately has to be taken in possession<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Since three people are involved, the seller of the cow, the buyer of the cow, and the banker as seller of the donkey, this is a sale and a separate buy, not a barter. Two separate acts of acquisition are unquestionably required.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How is this? If he collected from him the produce but did not give him the money<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">He is in possession even though he is not yet the owner. The transaction was concluded. If the buyer does not pay, the seller has a valid case in court for collection of a debt.</i>, he cannot retract. If he gave the money but did not collect the produce, he can retract<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since Mishnah 1 implies that handing over money is not an act of taking possession, both parties can still annul the contract. The seller can return the money and the buyer has no case for breach of contract; the buyer can ask for the money back and refuse to take delivery.</i>, but they said: He Who exacted retribution from the people of the Flood will in future collect from him who does not keep his word<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The injured party can go to court and ask for official moral condemnation of the party who reneged on the contract at the last minute.</i>. Rebbi Simeon said, the one who holds the money is advantaged<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The moment the buyer paid, he lost his right to renege on the contract. Only the seller retains the option of returning the money and not delivering the goods if the contract is still open.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “How is this? If he collected the produce from him,” etc. Rebbi Aḥa said, it is written: “For the earth was filled with oppression because of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 6:13" href="/Genesis.6.13"><i>Gen.</i> 6:13</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 31:5" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.31.5"><i>Gen. rabba</i> 31(5</a>), in the name of R. Ḥanina.</i>” What was their oppression? If a person came carrying a box full of lupines they all asembled and each one was taking less than the worth of a <i>peruṭah,</i> a case which cannot be brought before the judges<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The farmer in the end was left with nothing but so many thieves took only a minute amount that he did not have a case against anybody.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, “much, much”<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 6:5" href="/Genesis.6.5"><i>Gen.</i> 6:5</a> about the generation of the flood; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 18:20" href="/Genesis.18.20"><i>Gen.</i> 18:20</a> about Sodom and Gomorrha, identical expressions. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 28:3" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.28.3"><i>Gen. rabba</i> 28(3</a>); R. Berekhiah in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i>. The acts of these were identical to the acts of those.
Rebbi Ḥanina said, practice follows Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, R. Joḥanan rules following R. Simeon (48b) but Rav Ḥisda explicitly disagrees (49b).</i>, but we do not say this in all cases<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">As explained later in this paragraph, the ruling does not apply to partial payments in all its forms.</i>. Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: A case came before Rebbi and he instructed following Rebbi Simeon. <sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">A completely different version of this story is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 48b" href="/Bava_Metzia.48b">Babli, 48b</a>; it is a contamination of this story with the next one.</i> Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph gave a <i>denar</i> for salt. That one reneged<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The seller refused to fill the contract and gave the money back to him.</i>. He said, does he<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ḥiyya bar Joseph told the seller to exert moral pressure on him even if he could not bring the case to court because of R. Ḥanina’s ruling.</i> not know that already a sickle is put on the thigh of that man, “He Who exacted retribution from the people of the Flood will in the future collect from him who does not keep his word”? <sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">A different version of this story is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:1:19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.1.19"><i>Qiddušin</i> 2:1, Notes 101–107</a>.</i> A person paid for raw silk<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Greek μέταξα.</i>. He reneged<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The seller refused to fill the contract and gave the money back to him.</i>. The case came before Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph and Rebbi Joḥanan. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph said, either he delivers for the amount of the pledge<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since the money was given as surety, not as payment, R. Simeon’s rule cannot be applied to the contract. But he holds that a contract can be enforced in parts. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 49a" href="/Bava_Metzia.49a">Babli, 49a</a>, this is Rav’s position.</i> or one may turn him over to “Him Who exacted retribution.”. Rebbi Joḥanan said, either he delivers the entire contract or one may turn him over to “Him Who exacted retribution.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">A partial payment validates the entire deal; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:1:19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.1.19"><i>Qiddušin</i> 2:1, Note 106</a>.</i>” Rebbi La said, it was a case of a pledge; Rebbi Ze‘ira said, he had given him partial payment<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">There is no difference between the two situations as regards R. Simeon’s rule.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph agrees with Rebbi Joḥanan in a sale which is not usually split, such as of a cow or a stole.
Rebbi Jacob bar Idi, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: A ring does not have the status of a pledge<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since it has to be returned as is, it is only a reminder without legal consequences. Cf. <i>Ševi‘t</i> 10:9, Note 131; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 48b" href="/Bava_Metzia.48b">Babli 48b</a>.</i>. Nobody dealing in words onlymay one deliver to “Him Who exacted retribution”<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sevi‘it</i> 10:9, Notes 129–130; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 49a" href="/Bava_Metzia.49a">Babli 49a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 3:14" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).3.14">Tosephta 3:14</a>. As long as there was no action of acquisition, the person who goes back on his word can be considered untrustworthy but is not subject to judicial censure.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If one promised a gift to another and wanted to renege on it, he may renege.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sevi‘it</i> 10:9, Notes 133–142; <i>Ma‘aśer Šeni</i> 4:7 Note 129 <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 49a" href="/Bava_Metzia.49a">Babli 49a</a>.</i> [Rebbi Jacob bar Zavdi asked before Rebbi Abbahu: Is that “true no, true yes<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:36" href="/Leviticus.19.36"><i>Lev.</i> 19:36</a>.</i>”?]<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Text added from E. Since the answer is given in L, the question must have been in the original text. For the duty to be honest in monetary matters as a biblical command, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 10:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.10.4.6"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 10:9, Note 134</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 6:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.6.1.7"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 6:1, Note 39</a>.</i> He said, at the moment when he said it, he had to speak with full deliberation<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The obligation to be honest does not imply a prohibition to change one’s mind. In the <i>Giṭṭin</i> text, it is R. Jacob bar Zavdi who gives the answer to R. Yose [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 49a" href="/Bava_Metzia.49a">Babli 49a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 13b" href="/Bekhorot.13b"><i>Bekhorot</i> 13b</a>; <i>Sifra Bekhorot Pereq</i> 8(7)].</i>. After that, if he changes his mind, he cannot change it, that is, if [the recipient] is poor<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Giving alms is not a gift to the poor but a gift to God and as such is final upon being promised (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.6.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:6</a>.<br><i>Kaftor waPeraḥ</i> Chapter 44 reads: “If somebody promised to give a gift and wants to change his mind, he may change his mind. But if he said, I am speaking with full deliberation, he cannot change his mind. That is, for a rich recipient. But for a poor recipient it becomes a vow.” This seems to be more of a paraphrase than an exact quote.</i>. But for a rich person, is that a vow? Rav commanded his servant: If I tell you to give a gift to a person, if he is poor, give it to him immediately. But if he is rich, take counsel with me a second time<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This contradicts the statement in <i>Ševi‘t</i> 10:9 that Rav never changed his mind once he had promised a gift.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan gave denars to a relative of his for oil. The oil rose in price<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">And the relative wanted to annul the contract.</i>. He went and asked Rebbi Yannai who told him, by Torah standards money acquires, but why did they say it does not acquire? Lest [the seller] tell him, your wheat was burned in the storage room<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Really ““the upper storey”, usually used as storage area in farmhouses. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 46b" href="/Bava_Metzia.46b">Babli 46b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 47a" href="/Bava_Metzia.47a">47a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 47b" href="/Bava_Metzia.47b">47b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 81b" href="/Eruvin.81b"><i>Erubin</i>81b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Eruvin (Lieberman) 7:1" href="/Tosefta_Eruvin_(Lieberman).7.1">7:1</a> (24d l. 3); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 83a" href="/Chullin.83a"><i>Ḥulin</i> 83a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 26a" href="/Kiddushin.26a"><i>Qiddušin</i>26a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 28b" href="/Kiddushin.28b">28b</a>; <i>Ševi‘it</i> 8:1 (38a l. 2) <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.1.2">Note 15</a>. The opinion attributed here to R. Yannai is that of R. Joḥanan in the Babli, of R. Simeon ben Laqish in the Yerushalmi <i>Erubin</i> and <i>Ševi‘it</i>.</i>. Rebbi Samuel ben Sosartai in the name of Rebbi Abbahu: If [the seller] told him, your wheat was burned in the storage room, he is trustworthy<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The testimony must be accepted by the court unless shown to be perjured by two independent witnesses.</i>. Rebbi Isaac objected: Where do we hold? If his money had acquired for him, his property would have been burned<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Then the statement of R. Samuel ben Sosartai would have made sense. But since rabbinically money cannot acquire, if the seller claims that the wheat was burned after he had received payment, his claim is irrelevant since the wheat was not yet delievered. The seller has to provide the buyer with other grain irrespective of what happened.</i>. But if his money had not acquired for him, the other’s property was burned.
Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: A person who gave ten denars to another and told him, I have to get from you 100 (denars)<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Denars</i> is the reading of the Leiden ms. and the <i>editio princeps</i>; but it is impossible. <i>Barrels</i> is the reading of E, supported by the quote in <i>Sefer Ha‘iṭṭur</i> I, p. 22 (Note 92): גרבין חמרא <i>Wine barrels,</i> copied in <i>Mordekhai Bava meṣi‘a</i> #449 as גריוי 10. gold denars would be an appropriate price for 100 clay barrels of wine.</i> [barrels] from that house, is permitted; from that vineyard, is prohibited<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The “house” is the wine cellar. Since the object of sale already exists in the possession of the seller, the transaction is not a forbidden trade to circumvent the prohibition of interest. But a trade on futures of wine from yet ungrown grapes is impossible, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.5.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:5, Note 113</a>.</i>. What is the difference between house and vineyard? A house is not expected to collapse, a vineyard occasionally collapses<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Occasionally there is no harvest.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, you understand from this that if a person gave ten denars to another and told him, I have to get 100 barrels from you, once he<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of <i>Sefer Ha‘iṭṭur</i> (accepted by <i>Mordekhai, loc. cit</i>.), R. Yose states that for wine, already stored in barrels, the argument of R. Yannai does not apply and the sale was concluded by the seller accepting the money. This argument is justly rejected by R. Joseph Caro (<i>Bet Yosef Ḥošen Mišpaṭ</i> 199,2) as against talmudic principles. In addition, while wine in clay barrels stored in a stone cellar is not likely to be burned, it easily may be lost by breakage of the barrels. Therefore, it seems that this paragraph is a direct continuation of the previous one and R. Ḥanina’s ruling that practice follows R. Simeon is qualified further in that the seller has lost his right of refusal once he used the money in his business.<br>The biblical expression שָׁלַח יָד בְּ (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:7" href="/Exodus.22.7"><i>Ex.</i>22:7</a>) means “illegally to take” (for one’s own use).</i> used them he is required to deliver the merchandise.
Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, “or buying from your neighbor’s hand”<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:14" href="/Leviticus.25.14"><i>Lev.</i> 25:14</a>, containing the prohibition of overcharging or underpaying in commercial transactions.</i>, from you neighbor’s hand you need an act of acquisition; you do not need an act of acquisition from the Gentile’s hand<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The principle that in biblical law payment transfers not only ownership but also possession is restricted to transactions involving Gentiles; for transactions between Jews the transfer needs an actual “taking” from the prior owner’s hand; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 47b" href="/Bava_Metzia.47b">Babli 47b</a>.</i>. In Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish’s opinion, why does one deliver a person to “Him Who exacted retribution”<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Since a deal between Jews is not valid in biblical law without actual transfer, why should the court be involved in a dispute regarding such a deal? E reads here: why does one <i>not</i> deliver a person to “Him Who …”?</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, it follows the Tanna who stated: “Nobody dealing in words only may one deliver to “Him Who exacted retribution”<sup class="footnote-marker">45,</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sevi‘it</i> 10:9, Notes 129–130; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 49a" href="/Bava_Metzia.49a">Babli 49a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 3:14" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).3.14">Tosephta 3:14</a>. As long as there was no action of acquisition, the person who goes back on his word can be considered untrustworthy but is not subject to judicial censure.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">This Tanna holds that taking the money is a “transfer from hand to hand”, which gives the transaction biblical status. The only case which the court will refuse to hear is one where no concrete action has yet taken place.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Cheating<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Overcharging (or underpaying) the current rate, forbidden in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:14" href="/Leviticus.25.14"><i>Lev.</i>25:14</a>. Overcharges of less than 16 2/3% are not recoverable in court.</i> is four obols per tetradrachma of 24 obols<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">A drachma (in the Roman Empire identified with the denar) is 6 obols.</i>, one sixth of the sale price. Until when may one return [the merchandise]? Until one can show it to a trader<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">A market maker in that commodity.</i> or a relative. Rebbi Tarphon instructed in Lydda that cheating is eight obols per tetradrachma, a third of the sale price, and the traders of Lydda were rejoicing. He told them, one may return [the merchandise] the entire day; they said, let Rebbi Tarphon keep our place quiet; they returned to the words of the Sages. Just as a private person can be cheated, so a trader can be cheated. Rebbi Jehudah says, a trader has no claim of being cheated<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Since he has to know what the going rate is for what he sells. This implies that for R. Jehudah a trader is permitted to have a sale with reductions of at least 16 2/3% of the going rate; the Sages would only permit a sale with reductions &lt;16 2/3%.</i>. The person overcharged has the advantage; if he so desires, he says, give me my money back<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The buyer returns the article and gets his money back.</i>, or give me back by what you overcharged me<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">He keeps the article.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Cheating is four obols,” etc. Rav said, this is the rate<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah ties the definition of overcharging to the money changing hands: the sixth has to be computed as one sixth of the sale price both for overcharging or for underpaying. If an article worth 6 was sold for 7 the overcharge was 1/7. If it was sold for 5, the undercharge was 20% and the sale can be annulled on the demand of the seller. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 49b" href="/Bava_Metzia.49b">Babli, 49b</a>, this opinion is attributed to Samuel; Rav holds that the basis of computation always is the current market price.</i>; Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is not the rate<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The larger of sale or market price is the base of computation.</i>. Rav said, if somebody trades on condition that the rules of cheating not apply to him, the rules of cheating do apply to him<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Biblical prohibitions cannot be abolished by private conventions; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.1.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 9:1</a>.</i>. Rebbi Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">R. Levi was the preacher in R. Joḥanan’s academy. His statement is attributed in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 55a" href="/Bava_Metzia.55a">Babli 55a</a> to Levi (bar Sisi), the student of Rebbi. Since here R. Levi is mentioned together with his contemporary R. Joḥanan, and in the Babli Levi bar Sisi together with his contemporary Rav Cahana, there is a genuine discrepancy in the traditions of both Talmudim.</i> stated: Cheating applies to a <i>peruṭah,</i> cheating can be by a <i>peruṭah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The rules of cheating apply even to transactions whose total value does not add up to the smallest silver coin, the obol. (In Hasmonean times, when the obol really was a silver coin, it was 24 <i>peruṭot.</i>) In the Babli, Rav Cahana denies the applicability.<br>E reads וההודאה שוה פרוטה “the confession of a <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth” instead of וההונאה פרוטה. This is an echo of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.5.1">Mishnah 6</a> and <i>Šebuot</i> 6:1 and is out of place here.</i>. But what is the status of cheating<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">If the overcharge was less than 16 2/3%, the buyer has no recourse. If it is more than 16 2/3%, he can annul the sale. What if it is exactly 16 2/3%? The Mishnah gives the injured party the choice of either receiving the amount of the overcharge or returning the object.</i>? It was stated: The item was acquired, he returns the overcharge, the words of Rebbi Jehudah the Prince<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi’s grandson, contemporary of R. Joḥanan.</i>; Rebbi Joḥanan said, the sale is annulled.
Cahana asked before Rav: If the seller is being cheated, he is cheated up to a fifth; if the buyer is being cheated, he is cheated up to a sixth<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rav, if an article worth 6 is sold for 7, the buyer can invoke the statute against cheating because he was overcharged 16 2/3%. But the seller can claim an additional payment if he was only paid 5, when he really was underpaid 20%, which should lead to an outright cancellation of the transaction.</i>! He answered him, it and the amount of cheating add up to a sixth<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The objection really is well taken; for the buyer the percentage is computed from below, for the seller from above.</i>. If he sold him what was worth five for six, can [the seller] not say, you were taken in for a denar, take your denar!<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Since the customer was overcharged by 20%, he can automatically annul the sale. Why can the seller not pay him the amount overcharged and force the customer to keep the merchandise?</i> Rebbi Ze‘ira said, the other can say to him, it is not to my honor that people say, this one was made a laughing stock. Some say, he can say to him, it is not to my honor to wear a garment of five denars. Rebbi Joḥanan, Rebbi Eleazar and Rebbi Hoshaia say, what is that Tanna’s reason<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The one who says that the higher of market or sale price is the basis of computation.</i>? “I know that my merchandise was worth only five denars, but because he needed it, I gave it to him for six. Take yours and return mine.”
If he sold him what was worth five for six; [the buyer] did not have time to ask around until it appreciated to be worth seven. Rebbi Jacob bar Idi, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Since the sale is annulled for one<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The buyer originally was overcharged by 20%, which gives him the right to annul the transaction, even though at the moment he could realize a 16 2/3% gain.</i>, it also is annulled for the other<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The seller can take back the merchandise and sell it again at a higher price.</i>. There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.5.4.1">Mishnah <i>Bava batra</i> 5:8</a>.</i>: “Four situations in sales. If he sold him high quality wheat and it turned out to be low quality, the buyer can annul the transaction. Low quality and it turned out to be high quality, the seller can annul the transaction.” [Therefore, the buyer cannot annul the transaction.]<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Text of E, missing in L; but it is clearly understood that the Mishnah <i>Bava batra</i> was quoted to contradict R. Joḥanan’s statement that the seller can annul the sale because the buyer could.</i> Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, it follows this Tanna: “Nobody dealing in words only may one deliver to “Him Who exacted retribution”<sup class="footnote-marker">45,</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sevi‘it</i> 10:9, Notes 129–130; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 49a" href="/Bava_Metzia.49a">Babli 49a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 3:14" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).3.14">Tosephta 3:14</a>. As long as there was no action of acquisition, the person who goes back on his word can be considered untrustworthy but is not subject to judicial censure.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Even according to R. Joḥanan, the seller can retract his committment to a sale only as long as no money has changed hands; otherwise the buyer could haul him into court publicly to be cursed. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 83b-84a" href="/Bava_Batra.83b-84a">Babli, <i>Bava batra</i> 83b/84a</a> flatly rejects the possibility that a dishonest trader enjoy a privilege from which the honest trader was barred.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> By how much could a tertradrachma be deficient without it being cheating? Rebbi Meïr says four <i>assarii,</i> one <i>assarius</i> per denar. Rebbi Jehudah says four <i>dupondii,</i> a <i>dupondius</i> per denar. Rebbi Simeon says eight <i>dupondii,</i> two <i>dupondii</i> per denar<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">1 <i>denarius argenteus</i> = 12 <i>dupondii</i> = 24 <i>assarii.</i> R. Simeon holds that the standards for coins are the universal standards for everything else. R. Jehudah admits a tolerance of 8.33%, R. Meïr only 4.166%.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “By how much could a tertradrachma be deficient,” etc. It was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Similar texts in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 3:17-19" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).3.17-19">Tosephta 3:17–19</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 52a" href="/Bava_Metzia.52a">Babli 52a</a>.</i>“More than that<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">If the coin is below the tolerances indicated in the Mishnah.</i> he spends it for its worth, a tetradrachma up to a <i>šeqel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">A Mishnaic <i>šeqel</i> is two denars, half a tetradrachma (a Tyrian <i>šeqel).</i> The quarter <i>šeqel</i> then is half a denar.</i>, a denar up to a quarter [<i>šeqel</i>]. If it is less than this even by an <i>assarius,</i> he may not spend it. If it was circulating as a blank for a tetradrachma or as a <i>šeqel</i> coin, as a blank for a <i>šeqel</i> or as a tetradrachma coin, he only has the shape of the coin<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Whether it can be used or not depends on the shape of the coin which it pretends to be.</i>. He should give it neither to a person who condemns property<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">חרם is the receiver of the inheritance tax, (הרג) חרג the receiver of property tax; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 3" href="/Nedarim.3"><i>Nedarim</i> 3:4–5, Note 97</a>.</i> nor to a tax assessor<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">חרם is the receiver of the inheritance tax, (הרג) חרג the receiver of property tax; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 3" href="/Nedarim.3"><i>Nedarim</i> 3:4–5, Note 97</a>.</i> since they defraud people, but he drills a hole into it and hangs it on his son’s neck. When has this been said? About tetradrachmas and denars. But gold denars and silver coins<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Honest silver coin which can be traded for its silver content.</i> one may spend according to their value<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">These coins have to be weighed before being used as currency.</i>. Just as one spends it for profane things one may spend it for Second Tithe provided one does not plan it<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">In Temple times, Second Tithe produce was redeemed for coin which was spent on food in Jerusalem, to be eaten there in purity. Since the redemption is a transaction between a person and himself, occasionally deficient coins may be used since he knows the deficiency. But one may not collect deficient coins during the year and then use the collected coins for redemption (<i>Ma‘aśer Šeni</i> 2:7, Note 96).</i>.”

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How much time does one have to return it<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The defective coin which fails the standards of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i>? In walled cities<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Greek χάραξ, χάρακος “palisade”.</i> until one can show it to a banker, in villages until Sabbath eve. If he recognizes it, he should take it back even after twelve months, but he has only a complaint against him<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">If the person who handed out the defective coin recognizes that it is the coin given out illegally, he has a moral duty to take it back even when the legal period in which he can be forced to take it back has expired. If he refuses, the injured party has the right to complain (i. e., tell about the case to other people) but has no recourse in court.</i>. He may use it for Second Tithe without hesitation since it is only miserly<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">In Temple times, Second Tithe produce was redeemed for coin which was spent on food in Jerusalem, to be eaten there in purity. Since the redemption is a transaction between a person and himself, occasionally deficient coins may be used since he knows the deficiency. But one may not collect deficient coins during the year and then use the collected coins for redemption (<i>Ma‘aśer Šeni</i> 2:7, Note 96).</i>’<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">After the destruction of the Temple and the disappearence of the ashes of the red cow (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.1"><i>Berakhot</i> 1:1, Note 3</a>) when food can no longer be eaten in purity, the coin has to be destroyed. For that purpose it even is preferable to use defective coins.</i>.<br>Cheating is by four oboli<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i>, claim is about two oboli, confession is about one <i>peruṭah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The court will not impose a judicial oath unless it be a case in which the claim is at least 2 oboli and the defendant admits to owing at least 1 <i>peruṭah:,</i> Mishnah <i>Šebuot</i> 6:1.</i>. There are five <i>peruṭot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>peruṭah</i> appears as minimal standard in five legal categories.</i>: Confession is about one <i>peruṭah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The court will not impose a judicial oath unless it be a case in which the claim is at least 2 oboli and the defendant admits to owing at least 1 <i>peruṭah:,</i> Mishnah <i>Šebuot</i> 6:1.</i>; a woman is preliminarily married by one <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:1</a>.</i>; one who used one <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth of Temple property committed larceny<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">He has to pay a 25% fine and bring a sacrifice, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:15-16" href="/Leviticus.5.15-16"><i>Lev.</i> 5:15–16</a>.</i>; one who finds one <i>peruṭah’</i> s worth has to make it public; one who robbed another of one <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth and swore about it, has to return it to him even in Media<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 9:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.9.6.1">Mishnah <i>Bava qamma</i> 9:7</a>.</i>.<br>There are five fifths<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Five cases in which a payment of 125% of the amount is due.</i>: He who eats heave, or heave of the tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">If he eats in error, Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 6:1; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:14" href="/Leviticus.22.14"><i>Lev.</i> 22:14</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 18:26" href="/Numbers.18.26"><i>Num.</i> 18:26</a>.</i>, or heave of the tithe of <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">This only applies to <i>demay</i>(produce of which it is not known whether it was tithed), but not to the heave of its tithe; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Demai 1:2" href="/Mishnah_Demai.1.2">Mishnah <i>Demay</i> 1:2, Note 67</a>.</i>, or <i>ḥallah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:20" href="/Numbers.15.20"><i>Num.</i> 15:20</a>.</i>, or first fruits<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.2.1.1">Mishnah <i>Bikkurim</i> 2:1</a>.</i>, adds a fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">All these cases are considered identical since each of them is called “heave” in a verse.</i>. He who redeems his vineyard in the fourth year<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:24" href="/Leviticus.19.24"><i>Lev.</i>19:24</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:31" href="/Leviticus.27.31">27:31</a>.</i> or his Second Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ma‘aśer Šeni</i> 5:5; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:25" href="/Deuteronomy.14.25"><i>Deut.</i>14:25</a>.</i>, adds a fifth. He who redeems his own gifts to the Temple, adds a fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:19" href="/Leviticus.27.19">Lev. 27:19</a>.</i>. He who used one <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth of Temple property, adds a fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">He has to pay a 25% fine and bring a sacrifice, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:15-16" href="/Leviticus.5.15-16"><i>Lev.</i> 5:15–16</a>.</i>. He who robbed another of one <i>peruṭah</i>’s worth and swore about it, adds a fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:24" href="/Leviticus.5.24"><i>Lev.</i> 5:24</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “How much time does one have to return it,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 52b" href="/Bava_Metzia.52b">Babli 52b</a>. The paragraph is the parallel to <i>Ma‘aśer Šeni</i> 2:7, Notes 96–98. It is clear that an underweight coin in Jerusalem in Temple times, when silver coinage was honest silver, could be spent according to its true value by weight. The Mishnah permits to redeem Second Tithe by an underweight coin as if it were full weight. The question asked is why Mishnah <i>Ma‘aśer Šeni</i> 2:6 requires one to choose the better one for Second Tithe if two similar coins, one of Second Tithe and one profane, were commingled and it is not known which one is the sanctified one. Since one may use an underweight coin for redemption, why does one have to choose the fuller weight coin in case of doubt?</i>Ḥizqiah said, to add it for Jerusalem, he adds it as good coin. If he uses it to redeem in the countryside, he redeems with the underweight coin. But did we not state: “He chooses the better one of the two and exchanges it for them”? Why can he not take the worse [coin] and treat it [as a good one]<sup class="footnote-marker">115a</sup><i class="footnote">Addition of E.</i>. He<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Ma‘aśer Šeni</i>: R. Jonah.</i> said, there is a difference here because it already was shown to be Second Tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">One may use a profane underweight coin for redemption; once it is sanctified it has to be treated with respect.</i>.
“He may use it for Second Tithe without hesitation since it is only miserly.” <sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 53b" href="/Bava_Metzia.53b">Babli 53b</a>. A parallel, different treatment is in <i>Ma‘aśer Šeni</i>4:3, Notes 57–63.</i> Jacob bar Zavdi, Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Simon<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">For obvious chronological reasons this must read: R. Simon (in <i>Ma‘aśer Šeni:</i> R. Yose ben R. Simon) in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i>: One does not add a fifth for any Second Tithe which in itself is not worth a <i>peruṭah.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: One does not add a fifth for any Second Tithe for which the fifth is not worth a <i>peruṭah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli presents this as R. Simeon ben Laqish’s opinion. The Yerushalmi <i>Ma‘aśer Šeni</i> quotes only the second opinion.</i>. A <i>baraita</i> supports the one, a <i>baraita</i> supports the other. As it was stated: “From its tithe,<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:31" href="/Leviticus.27.31"><i>Lev</i>. 27:31</a>.</i>” except what is worth less than a <i>peruṭah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">As always in rabbinic interpretation, the prefix מ is partitive: Not for all tithe has a fifth to be given in redemption. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Bechukotai, Chapter 12 10" href="/Sifra,_Bechukotai,_Chapter_12.10"><i>Sifra Beḥuqqotai Pereq</i>12(10</a>); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 53b" href="/Bava_Metzia.53b">Babli 53b</a>.</i> This means, not unless itself is worth a <i>peruṭah.</i> Another Tanna states: “From its tithe<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:31" href="/Leviticus.27.31"><i>Lev</i>. 27:31</a>.</i>, its fifths<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:23" href="/Leviticus.5.23"><i>Lev.</i> 5:23</a>. The plural “fifths” is interpreted to mean that if somebody is obligated to pay a fifth and then reneges and swears falsely that he does not owe it, the first fifth becomes principal on which another fifth is due. This may continue until the fifth becomes less that a <i>peruṭah. Sifra Wayyiqra Parašah</i> 13(12); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 53b" href="/Bava_Metzia.53b">Babli 53b</a>.</i>” except if it is worth less than a <i>peruṭah.</i> This means, not unless the fifth is worth a <i>peruṭah.</i> Rebbi Abin bar Mamal<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Everywhere else, including <i>Ma‘aśer Šeni,</i> he is called Abba bar Mamal. There, it is shown that quite a number of “<i>perutot”</i> are missing from the Mishnah.</i> said, our Mishnah follows neither one since we have stated “there are five <i>peruṭot</i>”, but we did not state “not unless itself is worth a <i>peruṭah</i>”; we have stated “there are five fifths”, but we did not state “not unless the fifth is worth a <i>peruṭah</i>”.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following are not under the rules of cheating<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">There is no recourse in court for overpaying or underpaying.</i>: Slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:45" href="/Leviticus.25.45"><i>Lev.</i> 25:45</a>, Gentile slaves are put under the rules of real estate.</i>, securities<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Documents of indebtedness. What one buys is not the paper on which the IOU is written but the future claim. What one buys “from the hand of your neighbor” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:14" href="/Leviticus.25.14"><i>Lev.</i> 25:14</a>) is the paper; therefore the IOU is not under the rules of that verse.</i>, real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:14" href="/Leviticus.25.14"><i>Lev.</i> 25:14</a> only refers to movables.</i>, and Temple property<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:14" href="/Leviticus.25.14"><i>Lev.</i> 25:14</a> only refers to “your neighbor,” not to public property.</i>; they are not under the rules of double or quadruple or quintuple restitution<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">If slaves, securities, or Temple property was stolen (e. g., Temple animals).</i>. An unpaid trustee does not have to swear, a paid trustee does not pay. Rebbi Simeon says, sacrifices which he is obligated to warrant<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">If one vows to bring “a sacrifice”, he has not fulfilled his obligation until the animal was sacrificed. Before that moment, it remains the personal property of the offerer and is covered by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:14" href="/Leviticus.25.14"><i>Lev.</i> 25:14</a>. But if he vows to offer “this animal”, he has fulfilled his duty at the moment the animal was delivered to the Temple. After that it is Temple property; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 7:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.7.4.1">Mishnah <i>Bava qamma</i> 7:5</a>.</i> are under the rules of cheating; those for which he is not obligated to warrant are not under the rules of cheating. Rebbi Jehudah says, also he who sells a Torah scroll, an animal, or a pearl is not under the rules of cheating. They told him, they said only these.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The following are not under the rules of cheating,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 58b" href="/Bava_Metzia.58b">Babli 58b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 3:24" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).3.24">Tosephta 3:24</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah says, also a Torah scroll, an animal, and a pearl are not under the rules of cheating. A Torah scroll, for its value is infinite. An animal and a pearl need to be paired. They said to him, does not a person want to pair everything?” It was stated: “Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra says, a sword, a horse, and a shield are not under the rules of cheating at wartime.”

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Just as there is cheating in trade so there is cheating by words. One should not say to another: how much for this object, if he is not interested in buying. If somebody was a repentant sinner, one should not say to him: remember your earlier deeds. If somebody was the son of a proselyte, one may not say to him: remember the deeds of your forefathers, for it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:20" href="/Exodus.22.20"><i>Ex.</i> 22:20</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Neziqin</i> 18, <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Ioḥai</i> p. 210.</i>: “A sojourner you may neither cheat nor oppress, for you were sojourners in the Land of Egypt.”<br>One<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">The farmer.</i> does not mix produce, not even new with new; no need to say new with old. In truth<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">“In truth” characterizes undisputed practice.</i> they permitted to mix strong wine with weak since it improves it. One<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">The winery.</i> does not mix wine lees with wine but one delivers the lees with the wine. If water became mixed with somebody’s wine, he should not sell it in the store unless he declares it, and not to a trader for that one would only use it to trick people; at a place where they usually put water into the wine they may do so.<br>The trader may buy from five threshing floors and put into one chest, wine from five wine presses and put into one barrel<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πίθος.</i>, on condition that he not intend to mix<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">He may only pour wines of the same kind into one barrel.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, a store owner should not distribute roasted kernels and nuts to children because he induces them to come to him, but the Sages permit. He should not reduce the price, but the Sages say, may he be remembered as a benefactor<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The Sages oppose restrictions which hinder competition.</i>. He should not clean the groats following Abba Shaul, but the Sages permit it. However, they agree that he may not clean the upper layer of the chest<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">A store may not dress up the upper layer of merchandise, which is seen by the buyer, and then sell lesser quality from below. This is cheating.</i> since that gives a dishonest impression. One may not put makeup on humans<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Slaves one offers for sale.</i>, animals, or vessels.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Just as there is cheating in trade,” etc. “One may not put on make-up.” Rebbi Eudaimon the salt dealer was mollifying his spread<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">The translation of this sentence is tentative and is based on the meaning of the corresponding words فتر، سرد in Arabic. R. Eliahu Fulda (followed by Levy and Kohut) explains that he washed his sieve in soap to produce better looking salt grains. R. M. Margalit explains without any lexical justification that he washed pieces of meat to make them look better. Jastrow translated: R. Eudaimon the sailor mollified his sails. But sails are not edible and they are not made from loose tissue with lots of holes, سرد.</i>. Jacob bar Aḥa said to him, did we not state: “one may not put on make up”? His word implies that there is make-up on food. Rebbi Ze‘ira was working with flax. He went to Rebbi Abbahu and asked him, may I make my work look good? He told him, go and apply all you know. Rebbi Abbahu was working on those shawls<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Yerushalmi <i>Šabbat</i> 4, 8b l. 69 translates רדדים (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 3:23" href="/Isaiah.3.23"><i>Is.</i> 3:23</a>) by לסוטות.</i>. He came to ask Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina, how am I with those shawls? He told him, go and apply all you know. Rebbi Abba painted a bathtub. Rebbi Jacob from Emmaus stated: What means: “one may not put on make up”? One may not say to him<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Slaves one offers for sale.</i>, paint yourself.

Chapter 5



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> What is biting<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Interest which is prohibited by biblical law (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:36" href="/Leviticus.25.36"><i>Lev.</i> 25:36</a>) must be <i>biting</i>, i. e. cost the debtor money, and <i>increase</i>, result in monetary gain for the lender.</i> and what is increase<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">תַּרְבִּית “increase”, refers to rabbinic interest prohibitions in which the <i>biting</i> feature is missing. Transactions which are perfectly legitimate in general can become forbidden as rabbinic interest if enacted between borrower and lender. There are different shades of rabbinically prohibited interest. Transactions which result in gain for the lender for deferral of repayment of a debt are plain interest payments. Services the debtor renders to the creditor which carry no monetary value are אֲבַק רִבִּית “dust of interest”.</i>? What is biting, lending a tetradrachma for five denars, two <i>se’ah</i> of wheat for three; because this bites. What is increase? Paying more for produce. How is this? One bought from another wheat for a gold denar the <i>kor</i> at the going rate<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">For later delivery.</i>. Wheat appreciated to thirty denars. He told him, deliver my wheat, for I want to sell it and buy wine with the proceeds. The other said, let your wheat be given to me for thirty denars<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">So far, the transaction is perfectly legal.</i> and you have wine with me<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">They fixed the amount of wine which was the monetary equivalent of a <i>kor</i> of wheat at the time of the second transaction.</i>, but he has no wine<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Since at the time of delivery the seller might have to buy wine at a higher price, the quantity of wine delivered would contain a “premium paid to the buyer for waiting to receive delivery”. The latter is the definition of rabbinically forbidden interest. This prohibits all trades in futures (even to a farmer, who might have a bad harvest and be forced to buy grain in the open market to cover the quantities he contracted for at the start of the growing season.)</i>.<br>A creditor may not dwell on the debtor’s premises for free or rent it below the going rate, for this would be interest<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">רִבִּית is the common rabbinic expression for “interest”.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “What is biting,” etc. Rebbi Yannai said, this<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The interest described as “biting” in the Mishnah, where the rate of interest is spelled out beforehand. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 61b" href="/Bava_Metzia.61b">Babli, 61b</a>, this is the opinion of R. Eleazar. Everybody agrees that rabbinic interest, where the effective rate depends on market forces, is not recoverable in court.</i> is interest which is taken away by the judges. They asked before Rebbi Joḥanan, can interest be taken away by the judges? He said, if it were so, nothing would be left for the great people of the Land of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">To return interest taken is a moral but not a legal duty; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 61b" href="/Bava_Metzia.61b">Babli 61b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan agrees that no document obligates another<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">While a court will not force a creditor to return the interest he already received, it cannot accept documents subjecting the debtor to a fixed rate of interest. If several documents are submitted to the court in a dispute between two parties, and one claim, containing a stated interest rate, was already paid off, the interest paid thereon cannot be deducted from the capital in dispute in the document which was not yet liquidated.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan agrees that if the document exists one subtracts [the interest]<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">If a document subjecting the debtor to a fixed rate of interest comes before the court, they have to delete the interest clause.</i>.
“What is increase?” It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 72a" href="/Bava_Metzia.72a">Babli 72a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 30b" href="/Bava_Kamma.30b"><i>Bava qamma</i> 30b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 94b" href="/Bava_Batra.94b"><i>Bava batra</i> 94b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:4:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.4.7">Yerushalmi <i>Giṭṭin</i> 4:4 (Notes 129</a>,130), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:2:2-17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.2.2-17"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 2:2</a> (29a l. 38).</i>: A Jew who lent to another Jew with interest can collect neither principal<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Not as a matter of biblical law but as a fine. For him, the court will refuse to look at a document containing a stated rate of interest which therefore becomes barred from any action needing the sanction of a court.</i> nor interest, the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the Sages say, he may collect the capital but not the interest. <sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 60b" href="/Bava_Metzia.60b">Babli 60b</a>.</i> It is written: “Your money you shall not give him by biting<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The reference includes the second half of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:36" href="/Leviticus.25.36"><i>Lev.</i> 25:36</a>, “and against increase do not give your food.”</i>.” Not only money by biting and food by increase. From where increase in money and biting in food? The verse says, “do not take from him biting and increase,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:35" href="/Leviticus.25.35"><i>Lev.</i> 25:35</a>, “and fear your God, I am the Eternal.” This establishes the prohibition of stated interest as a moral imperative, subject to the jurisdiction of Heaven, not the human court. The argument identifies תַּרְבִּית in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:35" href="/Leviticus.25.35">v. 35</a> and מַרְבִּית in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:36" href="/Leviticus.25.36">v. 36</a>.</i>” it brackets biting with increase and increase with biting. Since biting refers to money, increase also refers to money; since increase refers to food, biting also refers to food<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">In each case every instance of taking stated interest is two sins by one action.</i>.
“One bought wheat from another.” Rebbi Abba bar Cahana said, the rate which is valid for everybody<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The going rate mentioned in the Mishnah is the rate at which the seller is ready to sell to any buyer. It is presumed that market forces are effective at all times.</i>. Less than that, woe to the seller; more than that, woe to the buyer!
“He told him, deliver my wheat, for I want to sell it.” How did he acquire<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah forbids taking money for merchandise which is not yet in the seller’s hand, it must assume that the contract is binding; otherwise it could not be forbidden. E has here an extended quote from the Mishnah.</i>? Rav Naḥman bar Jacob was of the opinion that he had to deliver (to the other person)<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of the Leiden ms. and <i>editio princeps.</i> As stated in the previous note, this is a trivial inference.</i> [by law. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: His money acquired for him by biblical law.]<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E. The Mishnah here follows R. Yannai and R. Joḥanan (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.1.1">Chapter 4, Note 2</a>) that by biblical standards paying money transfers both ownership and possession. The seller obligates himself by taking money or money’s worth. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.5.2"><i>Qiddušin</i> 2:6, Note 138</a>, R. Ḥiyya bar Abba, successor of R. Joḥanan in Tiberias, disagrees in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i>
“A creditor.” A person lent money to another, who let him live in his house, then said, give me the rent for my house. The first one said, give me my money<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The text is quoted by Ran <i>Ketubot</i> 13 (on Alfasi # 391; 63a in the Wilna edition of Alfasi). There, the creditor first asked for his money; the debtor responded by asking for rent.</i>. The case came before Rebbi Abba bar Mina<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">In Ran: Abba bar Zamina.</i>, who told him to give what was a reasonable rent.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 5:3" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).5.3">Tosephta 5:3</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 62b" href="/Bava_Metzia.62b">Babli 62b</a>.</i>: “There are things which are interest but are permitted. How is that? A person may buy another’s promissory notes at a discount, or bills of debt at a discount, and need have no compunctions because of interest<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">As a matter of principle, while interest, a “premium for waiting to receive money due”, is forbidden, discount, a premium for paying early, is unquestionably permitted. The securities are due sometime in the future; the lender needs money now. He sells his securities at a discount.</i>. Other things are not interest but are forbidden because of circumventing interest. How is that? One received from another a field for ten <i>kor</i> of wheat<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is both corrupt and incomplete (in both mss.). Most likely it refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.2.1">Mishnah 3</a>: A farmer rents a field and is given the choice of either paying 10 in advance for the year or each month 1 (of whichever unit it may be.) Since in talmudic tradition rental fees are payable at the end of the rental period, the difference of 2 for the year is discount rather than interest; it is unquestionably permitted.</i>.… A person said to another, lend me a tetradrachma<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The continuation shows that one must read “mina”, 100 denars, for “tetradrachma”.</i>. He answered, I only have a <i>kor</i> of wheat, take it. If he then turned around and bought it back for 24 it is not interest but forbidden because of circumventing interest.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Since a <i>mina</i> equals 25 tetradrachmas, the lender makes 1 tetradrachma on the deal. The question arises, why is this not true interest?</i>” He might have said to him, you do not have to do this and take your denar but then you have to pay for vessels and porters of the containers<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since the borrower would have had expenses of a tetradrachma if he actually had sold the wheat on the market, the lender can save him the expense by taking the wheat back while deducting the putative costs. But since for the lender it actually is a “premium for waiting,” it is prohibited as “circumventing interest”.</i>.
Rebbi La said, he pays him the wages of his foot; Rebbi Ze‘ira said, he rents him the dwelling at an inflated price<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that these statements refer to the following <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.2.1">Mishnah 3</a>, that it is permitted to rent a dwelling at a discount if the rental fee is paid for a year in advance. R. La holds that the discount is not a simple finance operation but represents real savings for the landlord who does not have to go every month to collect his rent. R. Ze‘ira holds that rental fees are not really determined by the market; every landlord has the choice for how much he wants to rent out his real estate. If there exists no market price, one cannot talk about over-or undercharging.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may increase rental fees but not sale price. How is this? He leased a courtyard saying to him<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The landlord to the renter.</i>, if [you pay] now it is yours for ten tetradrachmas a year but if paid monthly it is a tetradrachma per month; this is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.1.7">Note 26</a>.</i>. He sold his field and said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The seller to the buyer.</i>, if [you pay] now it is yours for 1’000 <i>zuz,</i> but if after the threshing floor<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">When the farmer sells his crop; this is the only time of the year when he has ample cash.</i> it is twelve <i>mina</i>; this is forbidden.<br>If one sold his field, [the buyer] made a partial payment and [the seller] said, anytime you wish bring the remainder and take yours; this is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">If the field is sold now but the yield remains the seller’s until it is fully paid for, the yield collected by the seller is a “premium for waiting.”</i>. If one lent money on a field and said, if you do not repay me in three years from now, it will be mine, it is his<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">A mortgage with a forfeiture clause.</i>; Boethos ben Zenon<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 63a" href="/Bava_Metzia.63a">Babli, 63a</a>, <i>‘Arakhin</i> 31a; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 4:2" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).4.2">Tosephta 4:2</a>: On the instruction of R. Eleazar ben Azariah.</i> acted thus following the Sages.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One may increase rental fees,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 4:4" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).4.4">Tosephta 4:4</a>.</i>: “If somebody sells a field to another and stipulates, on condition that I shall work it as a sharecropper, on condition that I shall be part owner, on condition that the tithes shall be mine<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Demay</i> 6:3 (Notes 77,84); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 63a" href="/Bava_Batra.63a">Babli <i>Bava batra</i> 63a</a>. In this case, the seller must be a Cohen or Levite; he reserves for himself the place at which the grain used as tithe grows. The condition is impossible for an Israel since for him it would be a trade in futures which is null and void.</i>, on condition that if you sell it, you will sell it only to me, that anytime I want I may pay you back and take it; this is permitted.” <sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 63a" href="/Bava_Metzia.63a">Babli 63a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 27b" href="/Megillah.27b"><i>Megillah</i> 27b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Arakhin 31a" href="/Arakhin.31a"><i>Arakhin</i> 31a</a>.</i>“If he owed him money and wrote his field over to him as a sale, any time the seller<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The debtor.</i> eats the yield it is permitted; any time the buyer<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The creditor who in the end will return the field to its owner and in the meantime receives the field’s yield as “premium for waiting.”</i> eats the yield, it is forbidden. Rebbi Jehudah says, in any case it is permitted. Rebbi Jehudah said, that is what Boethos ben Zenon did following the Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 63a" href="/Bava_Metzia.63a">Babli, 63a</a>, <i>‘Arakhin</i> 31a; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 4:2" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).4.2">Tosephta 4:2</a>: On the instruction of R. Eleazar ben Azariah.</i>. They said to him, is that a proof? The seller was eating the yield.” Rebbi Joḥanan, Rebbi Eleazar, and Rebbi Hoshaia said, Rebbi Jehudah learned from the houses of walled cities<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:29-30" href="/Leviticus.25.29-30"><i>Lev.</i> 25:29–30</a>. The sale of a house in a walled city implies an automatic right for the seller to buy back his house at the exact sale price during one full year. During that year, the buyer has the house as owner for his unrestricted use.</i>. As it was stated: The year mentioned in the Torah regarding walled cities is like interest but it is not interest<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since no interest rate was spelled out, no biblical prohibition was violated.</i>. Another Tanna stated: This is interest but the Torah permitted it<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that rabbinic interest prohibitions are basically biblical as long as they involve a clear “premium for waiting.” The only rabbinic interest prohibitions which are purely a “fence around the law” are those which only involve a possible, but not a certain, premium (such as “increase” defined in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>.)</i>. He who said, it is like interest but it is not interest, Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one has to switch the positions of R.
