Meïr (i. e., “the rabbis”) and R. Jehudah; cf. Babli <i>‘Arakhin</i> 31a. E here has a lacuna.</i>. He who said, this is interest but the Torah permitted it, Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one has to switch the positions of R. Meïr (i. e., “the rabbis”) and R. Jehudah; cf. Babli <i>‘Arakhin</i> 31a. E here has a lacuna.</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">E here has an addition: “What is R. Jehudah’s reason? Any interest which involves one permitted aspect is permitted.” The permitted aspect in this case is that the debtor decides not to repay his debt. Since the loan was structured as a conditional sale, the buyer retroactively is justified in harvesting the yield from the date of sale. This is Abbaye’s reading of R. Jehudah’s position in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 63a" href="/Bava_Metzia.63a">Babli (63a</a>); the addition seems to be a gloss by a student of the Babli.</i> Rebbi Idi said, when I came from the Diaspora, I found that a case<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">E has an addition: הַדָּר בַּחֲצַר חֲבֵירוֹ שֶׁלֹּא מִדַּעְתּוֹ “about one who dwelt on another’s property without the latter’s knowledge.” If this addition be relevant, it must have been the creditor who lived on property which belonged to the debtor but was held by the latter neither for rental nor for his own use, cf. ברכיהו ליפשיץ <b>מחזי כממון</b> מחקרי תלמוד ג (2005) p. 438–460.</i> was before Rebbi Immi of “it is like interest but it is not interest.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">It is more likely that the problem was a sale which in the end was rescinded but in the meantime the buyer lived in the house.</i>” Rebbi Ḥizqiah said, they only said that “this is interest but the Torah permitted it.” There<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">A house in a walled city, and the case before R. Immi was not in this category.</i>, the Torah permitted it, therefore not in any other case. Nevertheless, Rebbi Immi did not consider it<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">He ruled according to R. Jehudah.</i>, for he said: a house dwells with its inhabitants<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">When the sale was rescinded, the full price was to be returned without any deductions for rental during the period in which the buyer used it. R. Immi did not necessarily follow R. Jehudah’s argument as presented in the Halakhah; he holds that an uninhabited house deteriorates much more than an inhabited one and, therefore, the buyer is due some consideration for the upkeep of the house. Then this consideration might as well be the entire rent.<br>E’s text contradicts the interpretation of J. N. Epstein in <i>Tarbiz</i>8 (1937) 395–397 who considers the case before R. Immi as antichretic lease (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 6:5:1-2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.6.5.1-2">6:7, Notes 77–78</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.6.3"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 4:6, Note 168</a>.) But antichretic loans are endorsed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 6:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.6.5.1">Mishnah 6:7</a> in certain cases.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not hire a shop clerk for half the gain<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., half the gain or loss, because then the material or the money provided to the trader is a loan and the 50% gain, the profit from the loan, is a “premium for waiting”. But if the trader receives a basic wage, he is an employee rather than an independent agent receiving a loan.</i>; one does not give him money to buy produce for half the gain unless one pays him wages as an unemployed<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">This word appears in the Tosephta but is missing in the Babli.</i> worker. One does not give chickens to breed<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">To split the difference in price between chicks and eggs.</i> for half, nor does one hand over calves and foals for care for half the risk unless one pays for his work and food<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Again if the rancher not only will get 50% of the gain but also will be responsible for 50% of any loss. Then the investment is a loan, and the rules of the prohibition of interest are applicable. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.4.1">Note 84</a>.</i>. But one takes in<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">When all the risk is borne by the investor. This is a monetary equivalent of the investor paying the rancher for his work.</i> calves and foals for half and grows them until they are one-third grown<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">It is possible to read this as “three years old”.</i>, and a donkey<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Used in rabbinic Hebrew both for he- and she-asses.</i> until it becomes a beast of burden.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not hire a shop clerk,” etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">E has here an addition: וּבְמָקוֹם שֶׁנָּהֲגוּ לַעֲלוֹת שָׂכָר כַּתָּף לְמָעוֹת מַעֲלִין . "At a place where one is used to pay carrier's wages as money, one pays." This refers to the Mishnah, that one must pay the clerk a minimum wage. It is stated that the minimum cannot be less than what is customary locally. This seems to contradict the next sentence. According to S. Lieberman (<i>Tosefta kiFshutah Bava Mesi'a</i> p. 211) כתף "porter" means "work, exertion"; cf. Halakhah 6, Tosephta 5:6.</i> A person lent another one denars. He said to him, take two denars<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The wages required by the Mishnah can be a token sum. In practice, the Babli agrees in the name of Samuel (69a).</i> as your wages and what they will bring shall be mine and yours.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 4:22" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).4.22">Tosephta 4:22</a></i>: “If somebody gives money to another to buy produce with it for half [the gain]; if that one says, I did not buy<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">After some time he returns the money and claims that he did not trade.</i>, he only has a complaint on him<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The injured party has the right to complain (i. e., tell about the case to other people) but has no recourse in court for the gain lost by leaving his money inactive for some time.</i>. But if it was known<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">If the other's commercial activity can be proven by any means, the injured party can sue in court.</i> that he bought, the other forcibly extracts from him.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 4:18" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).4.18">Tosephta 4:18</a>. There, the second sentence appears in the name of R. Jehudah.</i>“If somebody gives money to another to buy produce with it for half the gain and one of them wants to age it, the other may hinder him<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">In the absence of a prior agreement, if they bought grain at harvest time and one of the parties wants to defer selling until close to the next harvest when prices will be appreciably higher, the other partner can object and prefer a quick penny to a slow dollar.</i>. But if it was the eve of a Sabbatical year, the other may not hinder him because it was understood that it was bought for that purpose<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is no harvest in the Sabbatical year, the price of grain is certain to rise. In this case, the presumption is that the parties bought the grain for storage and later sale.</i>.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 4:17" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).4.17">Tosephta 4:17</a>.</i>“If somebody gives money to another to buy produce with it for half the gain and one says, here you have a <i>mina</i> for I cannot be bothered with the details, that is forbidden. But if he saw that grain was going up in price, it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The person who did the trading wants to pay the person who gave the money a lump sum and not be bothered with providing a detailed accounting. In general, this is (rabbinically) forbidden even though the trader would do it for his own convenience. But if it is obvious that the other’s share in the gain would be more than a <i>mina,</i> only that the other party is ready to take less but receive his money immediately, it would be a permitted discount.</i>.
If somebody gives money to another to buy produce with it for half the gain, the trader is permitted to buy of the same kind. When he sells, he may not sell everything together but sells this first and that later<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The trader is permitted to invest of his own money in the same commodity which he buys together with another in order to get a better price for the larger order. But when he sells, he has to sell the quantity for which he is trustee first and only then may he sell for his own account, when it is to be expected that the large quantity will have lowered the price somewhat.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 4:21" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).4.21">Tosephta 4:21</a>.</i>“If somebody gives money to another to buy produce with<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Without specific instructions.</i> for half the gain, the trader is permitted to buy any kind he wants, but he should not buy a garment or wood<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">This would be illicit use of another person’s money.</i>.” <sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">This remark does not belong here; it is repeated as <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 9:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.9.3.2">Halakhah 9:3</a>. It is a commentary on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 4:22" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).4.22">Tosephta 4:22, Notes 56–58</a>. <i>Or zarua‘</i> (vol. 2. p. 93, #339) quotes it as the Yerushalmi’s commentary to that Tosephta.</i> Rebbi Isaac said, this implies that if one who leaves another’s capital unemployed, he only has a complaint on him<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">It is possible to read this as “three years old”.</i>. If he leaves another’s field unused, he is obligated to serve him<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">A sharecropper is not permitted to leave a field fallow (unless it is good agricultural practice in crop rotation and done with the landlord’s knowledge). He cannot say that he is required only to deliver 50% of the gain, which means 0 if there is no gain. שפת means “to serve food”.</i>. If he leaves another’s ship or store unused<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">When given to him for 50% of the net gain.</i>, what [is the rule]<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Or zarua‘</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:3:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.3.7">Note 69</a>) quotes the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 9:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.9.3.2">Yerushalmi 9:3</a> as leaving the question unanswered, but adds that in Chapter 4 the answer is: He is not liable.</i>?
<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 4" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).4">Tosephta 4</a>:8 (<i>Tosefta ki-Fshutah Bava Meṣ‘ia</i> p. 198), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 72b-73a" href="/Bava_Metzia.72b-73a">Babli 72b/73a</a>.</i>“A person was transporting from a lowprice place to a highprice one. Another said to him, give it to me, I shall pay you what you would charge at place X. If the responsibility is the giver’s, it is forbidden, but the buyer’s is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The other person takes the merchandise, brings it to the high-priced place, sells it there, and pays the owner on his return. In the meantime he has the use of the money he received for the sale. If this second person is responsible for any accidents, the original owner gets a “premium for waiting” in that he does not have to spend time and money for the trip and does not need insurance; this is rabbinically forbidden. But if the risk remains the original owner’s, it is a legitimate business deal and permitted.</i>. A person was transporting a package from place to place. Another said to him, give it to me, I shall pay you what you would charge at place X. If the responsibility is the giver’s, it is forbidden, but the buyer’s is permitted. But donkey drivers who receive from householders<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">They collect produce from farmers at a stated low rate, transport it to a highprice city, sell it there and buy supplies cheaper in the city than in the countryside, sell in the countryside and pay the farmers at the end. They do business without investing their own money.</i> obtain produce for a highprice place at lowprice rates<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">In Tosephta and Babli: “They do not have to worry about interest prohibitions.” This has to be understood here also.</i>.” Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi: At a place where usually they can go and return the same day<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Then no interest would be due and interest restrictions do not apply.</i>. Rav Huna said, he becomes his agent<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The donkey driver is the farmer’s agent for selling the latter’s produce. Interest rules do not apply to agency.</i>. But did one not object to [Rav]<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">From E, missing in L by scribal error.</i> Huna: If an agent fell victim to an accident, is he liable<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Since the donkey driver has to pay the farmer for his grain, irrespective of what happened on the road, he cannot be an agent.</i>? If he was the victim of an accident, he is not liable. Does it not happen that an unpaid trustee accepts to be like a borrower<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">An agent is not liable for accidents only if he strictly acts in the principal’s interest. If he is permitted to use the other’s money for his own trades, be becomes liable as a borrower while remaining an agent (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 94a" href="/Bava_Metzia.94a">Babli 94a</a>).</i>? But did not Rebbi Hoshaia state: Just as he is liable for the entirety, so he is liable for a part<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">This really is the same question again. An agent cannot be responsible for part of the merchandise if he is not responsible for the entirety. How can the donkey-driver be an agent if he collects produce from many farmers? S. Lieberman follows R. Eliahu Fulda in assigning these last sentences to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:5:2-6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.5.2-6.2">Halakhah 5:7</a>.</i>! Why does one quote this? There, he would pay a high price, but here he pays at the low rate<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Usually an agent is told what he is expected to do. If he is a buying agent, he is expected to pay the price which the principal agreed to. But here, he is only required to pay the farmer at the agreed local rate; he can reasonably be expected to be liable for accidents even if he acts as an agent.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One hands a cow or a donkey over for care as well as anything which works and eats<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">This is the text also of most Mishnah and Babli mss.; only the <i>editio princeps</i> of the Babli follows Rashi in reading: “One estimates the value of a cow and a donkey and anything which works and eats <i>for the middle</i>,” i. e., the owner of the animal and the person who cares for it and is the beneficiary of its work split any increase or loss arising from the animal 50–50. Maimonides, whose Mishnah text is identical with the Yerushalmi and the mss., does not comment on it, but in his Code (<i>Šutafim</i> 8:3) formulates that the owner and the tenant farmer share profits on a 50–50 basis; he does not mention that the farmer has to carry 50% of the risk and even 100% of the risk if the animal dies through of his fault (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.4.5">Note 102</a>). In modern terms the Mishnah states that an investment is not a loan, and that the worker and the capitalist who provides the means of production are free to conclude any contractual arrangement.<br>While the dictionaries and the commentators identify this שם with Aramaic שם “to estimate, determine its value”, for the translation it is identified with Arabic سام، سوم “to impose a task” (“to let graze freely” in the <i>af‘ala</i> stem). In the Halakhah, both meanings are used.</i>. At a place where one usually divides the young immediately, one divides; at a place where one usually raises them, they should be raised. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, one hands over a calf with its mother, a foal with its mother<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the calf is not productive, the tenant farmer is obligated to provide food and care for his share in its future value.</i>, and one invests in improving a field and does not worry about interest<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">It is legal for the tenant farmer to ask the landlord to invest money in his field, to be repaid by future increases in yield. That is yield of investment, rather than interest.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One hands a cow or a donkey over for care,” etc. It was stated: “A person may receive from another an animal on condition to deliver to him one lamb and one shearing per year, a chicken for ten eggs a month. <sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 5:5" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).5.5">Tosephta 5:5</a>. For the entire Halakhah, the basic commentary is S. Lieberman, <i>Tosefta ki-Fshutah Bava Meṣi‘a</i> pp. 211–216.</i> The woman receiving a chicken has to care for the chicks as long as those depend on their mother. <sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 5:7" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).5.7">Tosephta 5:7</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 69a" href="/Bava_Metzia.69a">Babli 69a</a>.</i> One cares for small animals 30 days and for large animals 50 days. Rebbi Yose says, for (large) [small]<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">“Large” is L’s, “small” the E text. The latter is supported by Tosephta, Babli, Mishnah <i>Bekhorot</i> 4:1.</i> animals three months because they need special care. After that, he gives the owner his part equally, everything according to local practice.” What means “he gives the owner his part equally”? A person gave to another 100 denars; they produced 20 denars. Each takes half of it; the half of the owner remains with him as capital<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The contract is amended automatically to increase the owner’s share in the herd.</i>. Do people do that<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">In E: People do not do that.</i>? But they trade, and at the end they divide it up. <sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 5:5" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).5.5">Tosephta 5:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 69a" href="/Bava_Metzia.69a">Babli 69a</a>.</i>“A person receiving an animal for care is responsible for it, for an unclean animal<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">A horse or a donkey, which are not kosher. This is the minimum duration of a contract for care of such animals; the contract period can be shorter for edible animals raised for their meat.</i> twelve months, for a human 24 months<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The contract period for a wet-nurse.</i>. At a place where work<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Work exceeding the daily routine.</i> is compensated separately, one pays; for offspring of animals, one pays<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">The terms described at the start of the paragraph are not prescriptive; without special written stipulation local custom prevails.</i>, everything following local custom.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 5:5" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).5.5">Tosephta 5:5</a>.</i>“Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, one hands over, etc., even at a place where one pays additional money for exertion, and one is not worried because of interest.<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the calf is not productive, the tenant farmer is obligated to provide food and care for his share in its future value.</i>”
<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 5:8" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).5.8">Tosephta 5:8</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 69a" href="/Bava_Metzia.69a">Babli 69a</a>.</i>“If one accepts an animal for care from another person, how long does he have to care for it? Symmachos says, small asses twelve months, adult donkeys 24 months<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">In E and both other sources, the minimum time is 18 months for adult she-asses, 24 months for the young. This seems reasonable.<br>In E, the name Symmachos is correctly spelled סימכוס.<br>The translation follows S. Lieberman (<i>l. c.</i> <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.4.2">Note 87</a> p. 213–4) in identifying גודרות (in E: העדרות) as γαϊδάρια, plural of γαϊδάριον, “small donkey”, a word frequently found in Egyptian papyri (it seems originally Persian <i>gaudar,</i> “calf”). He also notes that since in all of Talmudic literature חֲמוֹר denotes both male and female donkeys, one may assume that the word אֲתוֹן, “she-ass” in Biblical texts, also denotes both male and female donkeys.</i>. If he terminates the care in the middle of the term, one estimates for him; after the term one does not estimate for him, but care for one year is not comparable to two years<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">If one of the parties wants to terminate the contract (for cause, as shown later in the Halakhah), the determination of the difference in value of the animals at the start and at the end of the time of care is a matter for the courts. Otherwise, this is not the case since the sale price will determine the final value. It is noted that the increase in value of calves is not constant in time; the increase in the first year of a two-year contract is less than half of the final value.</i>.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 5:11-12" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).5.11-12">Tosephta 5:11–12</a>.</i>“If it dies because of gross negligence, he gives everything; not because of gross negligence, he gives half<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Since the tenant farmer is given a 50% interest in the gain, he also must bear a 50% risk of loss. The element of risk is what distinguishes investment from loan and exempts the transaction from the laws of interest. If the death is due to gross negligence of the caregiver, the owner may sue him for his loss.</i>. How is this? It was estimated as one <i>mina</i><sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">At the start of the contract period.</i> and improved and now is worth 200. If it dies because of gross negligence, he gives six gold denars<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">150 silver denars. He pays 100 for the animal and 50 for the owner’s part in the improvement which was lost through the caregiver’s fault.</i>; if not because of gross negligence, he gives 50 <i>zuz</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Half the original value.</i>”
<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 5:9" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).5.9">Tosephta 5:9</a>.</i>“If one accepts an animal for care from another person, it is for no less than twelve months. If he occupied himself with it all through the summer and he says to sell it, one forces him to feed it all through the winter. If he fed it all through the winter and he wants to sell it in the summer, one forces him to occupy himself with it all through the summer.<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">If either of the parties wants to terminate the contract before its expiration date, the other party can go to court and force the continuation until the end of the contract period.</i>”
<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 5:13" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).5.13">Tosephta 5:13</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 69b" href="/Bava_Metzia.69b">Babli 69b</a>.</i> It was stated: “Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, one invests in improving a field and does not worry about interest. How is this? One leased a field for ten <i>kor</i> of wheat and said to him, give me a tetradrachma<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel sources: 200 denars, 2 <i>minas.</i> This seems to be the correct text since it is unlikely that much improvement can be bought for 4 silver denars, and it is incomprehensible that the rent should increase by 50 silver denars a year for a loan of 4. But in E the text is: תֵּן לִי סֶלַע אַחַת וַאֲנִי נוֹתֵן לָךְ כּוֹר אֶחָד. “Give me one tetradrachma and I shall give you one <i>kor</i>.”</i> and I shall give you twelve <i>kor</i> at threshing time, it is permitted.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, because the field may bring an extraordinary yield<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The improvement of the object justifies the increase in rent.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, it is as if he increased the rent for the field. What is the difference between them? A store or a ship<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">As will be noted at the end, this refers to an additional investment by the owner in merchandise to be sold in the store or transported on the ship.</i>. According to him who said, because the field may bring an extraordinary yield, one invests in a store or a ship<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">A larger inventory may yield larger earnings.</i>. According to him who said, it is as if he increased the rent for the field, one may not invest in a store or a ship<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">A perpetual rent for an investment in merchandise would be interest.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, they disagree explicitly: Rebbi Joḥanan said, one invests in a store or a ship. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, one may not invest in a store or a ship, nor in anything which is not invested in the object itself<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Laqish agrees that an investment in a renovation of the store, or to provide the ship with better sails or steering gear, is legitimate (opinion of Rav Naḥman in the Babli).</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may not receive mortmain property<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Property given in perpetuity, for which rental is due in perpetuity irrespective of whether the original objects are still in existence. Risk-free income is interest; this also is the definition of interest in modern Mathematics of Finance.</i> from a Jew because that is interest. But one may receive mortmain property from Gentiles, one may borrow from them and lend them on interest, and similarly from a sojourner<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">A Gentile who follows the seven Noaḥide commandments, in particular the prohibition of idolatry.</i>. A Jew can lend a Gentile’s money on interest by the Gentile’s instruction but not by a Jew’s<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">In particular, not his own initiative.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One may not receive mortmain property form a Jew,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 5:13" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).5.13">Tosephta 5:13</a>.</i>“What is mortmain property? He had 100 sheep and told another: They are valued for you at 100 gold [denars]; their young, their milk, and their shearings are yours, but if they die you are liable for them and you pay me a tetradrachma for every one of them at the end of the year<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">This example has an interest rate of 16%.</i>. This is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">119a</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: permitted.</i>.”
Rebbi Jeremiah asked: There<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.3.1">Mishnah 5</a>, which forbids handing over calves to be raised unless the rancher is paid for his work.</i> you say that a paid trustee who would be responsible for accidents is forbidden. But here<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.4.1">Mishnah 6</a>, which allows such contracts for adult animals without restrictions.</i> you say that a paid trustee who is responsible for accidents is permitted. Does it not happen that an unpaid trustee agrees to be like a borrower<sup class="footnote-marker">81,</sup><i class="footnote">An agent is not liable for accidents only if he strictly acts in the principal’s interest. If he is permitted to use the other’s money for his own trades, be becomes liable as a borrower while remaining an agent (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 94a" href="/Bava_Metzia.94a">Babli 94a</a>).</i><sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">The quote from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:3:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.3.2-8">Halakhah 5</a> is slightly out of place here. Since the rancher is supposed to use the animal for his purposes, he cannot be under the rules of the unpaid trustee; he is a paid trustee. But this is really irrelevant for the question; the main point is that the transaction involves an element of risk which shields it from the laws of interest, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.5.1">Note 115</a>.</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah asked: There<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 2:4" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.2.4">Mishnah <i>Bekhorot</i> 2:4</a>, quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 70b" href="/Bava_Metzia.70b">Babli 70b</a>. The offspring of a Gentile’s mortmain animals in the hands of a Jewish tenant farmer are not subject to the laws of the firstling since the mothers are considered the Gentile’s property.</i> you say that mortmain belongs to the first, but here you say to the second<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">In the Halakhah here, the offspring is defined as the tenant’s property.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, there since the essence belongs to the first, the offspring are counted for the first<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">Since the owner can repossess the mother if the tenant is in arrears with his payment, the Gentile retains a monetary interest in the mother. This is enough to free the offspring from the rules of firstlings.</i>. But here the essence belongs to the second since the offspring belong to the second<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Halakhah. For the majority opinion in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Bekhorot 2:4" href="/Mishnah_Bekhorot.2.4">Mishnah <i>Bekhorot</i> 2:4</a>, the offspring’s offspring is subject to the rules of firstlings for the same reason.</i>.
“One may borrow from them and lend them on interest, and similarly from a sojourner.” Rebbi said, I do not know the definition of “sojourner” quoted in reference to the Hebrew slave. I do not know the definition of “proselyte” quoted in reference to interest<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 91a" href="/Bava_Metzia.91a">Babli, 91a</a>, “sojourner” is quoted in connection with interest, “proselyte” in connection with the Hebrew slave. There is no need to adapt the Babli’s reading.<br>The first reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:47" href="/Leviticus.25.47"><i>Lev.</i> 25:47</a>: “If the hand of a stranger <i>and</i> sojourner with you be opulent but your brother wax poor next to him and be sold to the stranger sojourner with you or to the essence of the stranger’s family.” Rebbi notes that the verse implies that “stranger <i>and</i> sojourner” is the same as “stranger sojourner”. Therefore, he objects to the usual explanation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 20b" href="/Kiddushin.20b"><i>Qiddušin</i> 20b</a>; <i>Sifra ad</i> 25:47): “Stranger” is the proselyte; “sojourner” is the Noaḥide stranger, “stranger’s family” is the idolater, “essence” is an idolatrous temple. But he has no better interpretation to offer.<br>The second question is about <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:35" href="/Leviticus.25.35"><i>Lev.</i>25:35</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:36" href="/Leviticus.25.36">36</a>: “If your brother wax poor and his hand totter with you; you have to support him, stranger <i>and</i> sojourner and living with you. Do not take from him interest and increase, …” Here again, <i>Sifra</i> explains that “stranger” is the proselyte; “sojourner” is the Noaḥide stranger, but the second statement contradicts our Mishnah.</i>.
“A Jew can lend a Gentile’s money on interest by the Gentile’s instruction but not by a Jew’s.” <sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 5:16" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).5.16">Tosephta 5:16</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 71b" href="/Bava_Metzia.71b">Babli 71b</a>. For the remainder of the Halakhah, cf. <i>Tosefta kiFshutah Bava meṣi‘a</i> pp. 220–229.</i>“A Jew had loaned money from a Gentile and wanted to return it when another Jew said to him, give it to me and I shall pay him the same rate as you are paying; this is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">If the Jew receives the money from the Jew not on the Gentile’s command; this is forbidden by the Mishnah.</i>. But if he introduced him to the Gentile, it is permitted.” [Rebbi Yose said, only if he let him stand with the Gentile.]<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Added from E supported by Nachmanides’s quote from the Yerushalmi in his <i>Novellae ad</i> 71b.<br>The question is, what is the role of the Gentile in this matter. Even though R. Yasa holds in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 6:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.6.1.3"><i>Demay</i> 6:1 (Note 10</a>) and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:1:16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.1.16"><i>Terumot</i> 1:1 (Note 76</a>) that a Gentile can appoint a Jew as his agent, and the Tosephta can be interpreted in this sense that the borrower has to get the lender’s agreement to transfer the loan to another borrower, R. Yose denies this and requires that the loan actually be transferred by the Gentile. The Amora R. Yose was the student of R. Jeremiah, student of R. Ze‘ira, student of R. Yasa.</i>
<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 5:17" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).5.17">Tosephta 5:17</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 71b" href="/Bava_Metzia.71b">Babli 71b</a>. As long as the money is given by the Gentile, it is permitted. R. Yose holds that the Gentile cannot be the Jew’s agent; therefore, the transaction is prohibited only if the Jew is an agent, not if the Jew agrees to the Gentile’s action.</i>“A Gentile had borrowed money from a Jew and wanted to return it when another Jew said to him, give it to me and I shall pay him the same rate as you are paying; this is permitted. But if he introduced him to the Gentile, it is forbidden.” Rebbi Yose said, only if he let him stand with the Gentile.
<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 5:21" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).5.21">Tosephta 5:21</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 72a" href="/Bava_Metzia.72a">Babli 72a</a>.</i>“A Jew had borrowed money from a Gentile or a Gentile from a Jew when he converted. Whether the loan was finalized before or after his conversion, he can collect the principal but not the interest.<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">E has a longer text:<br>יִשְׂרָאֵל שֶׁלָּװָה מִגּוֹי וְגוֹי מִיּשְׂרָאֵל וְנִתְגַּייֵר. בֵּין שֶׁזְּקָפָן עַד שֶׁלֹּא נִתְגַּייֵר בֵּין מִשֶׁנִּתְגַּייֵר גּוֹבֶה הַקֶּרֶן וְלֹא הָרִיבִּית [דִּבְרֵי רִבִּי מֵאִיר. רִבִּי יוֹסֵה אוֹמֵר. יִשְׂרָאֵל שֶׁלָּװָה מִגּוֹי וְנִתְגַּייֵר. בֵּין שֶׁזְּקָפָן עַד שֶׁלֹּא נִתְגַּייֵר בֵּין שֶׁזְּקָפָן עָלָיו מִשֶּׁנִּתְגַּייֵר גּוֹבֶה הַקֶּרֶן וְאֶת הָרִיבִּית.]<br>A Jew had borrowed money from a Gentile or a Gentile from a Jew when he converted. Whether the loan was finalized before or after his conversion, he can collect the principal but not the interest [the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Yose says, if the Jew had borrowed money from a Gentile, whether the loan was finalized before or after his conversion, he can collect both principal and interest.]<br>The text attributed to R. Meïr is the same in Tosephta and Babli. The text attributed to R. Yose is the same in the Babli; it is the same as attributed to Bar Qappara in the Halakhah. In the Tosephta, R. Yose (the Tanna) holds that the Gentile debtor cannot pay interest after conversion but a Jewish debtor of a loan executed before the Gentile’s conversion has to pay interest (since the obligation he entered into was legitimate at the time it was assumed.) The E text as it stands is either superfluous or defective; the L text certainly is defective since the third sentence of the paragraph contradicts the second. The two sentences most likely belong to two different authors, as attested by the Tosephta.</i> But if a Gentile had borrowed money from a Jew and converted, if the loan was finalized before he converted, he can collect principal and interest; after he converted, he can collect principal but not interest. Bar Qappara said, he can collect both principal and interest.” Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Bar Qappara’s reason is that if you exempt him from interest he is turned into an insincere proselyte<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Who therefore might have difficulty in finding a Jewish mate; his rule is to the ex-Gentile’s benefit.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 5:15" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).5.15">Tosephta 5:15</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 75a" href="/Bava_Metzia.75a">Babli 75a</a>. The Babli version, “to lend his children and family” is presupposed here; otherwise Rav could not point to deals between him and his cousin’s son Rabba bar bar Ḥana. The Babli prohibits this practice; it only permits deals between scholars who know that interest is forbidden and give the money as a gift.</i>“A person may borrow from his sons or daughters on interest, but he educates them for interest.” Rav said, e. g., I from Rabba bar bar Ḥana or Rabba bar bar Ḥana from me. “A person may not borrow for a <i>šeqel</i> and lend out for a tetradrachma; but a Gentile may borrow for a <i>šeqel</i> and lend out for a tetradrachma.<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">A Jew cannot be a banker, taking deposits from Jews at 2% interest (one <i>šeqel</i> per <i>mina</i>) in order to lend to Gentiles at 4%. But a Gentile banker can take deposits from Jews or Gentiles to lend to other Jews at a higher rate.</i>”
<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 5:20" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).5.20">Tosephta 5:20</a>; quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Bava Metzia 71b:8:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Bava_Metzia.71b.8.1">Tosaphot 71b, <i>s. v</i>. כגון</a>.</i> A Jew may borrow from a Gentile, and a Gentile from a Jew, and a Jew can become his guarantor without any worries about interest. If a Jew appointed a Gentile as guardian or steward<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">The word was identified by S. Lieberman as Latin <i>saltuarius,</i> administrator of an agricultural domain. Since the Gentile gives the loan, interest is permitted even if the capital is a Jew’s.</i>, it is permitted to borrow from him on interest. But if a Gentile appointed a Jew as guardian or steward, it is prohibited to borrow from him on interest. <sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 5:19" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).5.19">Tosephta 5:19</a>.</i> It is forbidden to borrow on interest a Gentile’s money deposited with a Jew; it is permitted to borrow on interest a Jew’s money deposited with a Gentile. This is the principle: Anything which the Jew has to warrant<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Since the banker is obligated to pay his depositors even if a borrower defaults, the deposits are treated as the banker’s property.</i> is forbidden, what the Gentile has to warrant is permitted.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not contract for produce until a rate was established<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">In the absence of a functioning commodities exchange, a trader may not conclude a contract for future delivery of a commodity for a fixed price if he receives the money immediately. For he might have to buy the commodity later at a higher price and then the material delivered would convey a “premium for waiting”. Once the rate is established and the seller can immediately hedge his trade by another contract, the transaction is legitimate even if the seller does not have any of the commodity which he sells and for which he is paid.</i>. If he was the first to harvest, he may contract<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Before a rate was established, the farmer can sell at any price level he sees fit if the basic produce is in his hand. He can sell grain on the basis of the harvested sheaves, wine on the basis of the harvested grapes, olive oil on the basis of the harvested olives. The potter can sell finished pottery once he has the raw material in storage.</i> for the stack of sheaves, or the vat of grapes, or the barrel of olives, or for potter’s clay and lime when it was loaded in his oven. One may contract for manure the entire year; Rebbi Yose says, one may not contract for manure unless he has manure on his dungheap, but the Sages permit it<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Since on a farm manure is continually produced.</i>. One contracts for wholesale price<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">It is possible to write a contract that the money be paid now for a later delivery at the then going wholesale rate.</i>; Rebbi Jehudah says, even if one did not contract for wholesale price he may say to him, give me some like that or return my money<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Since money does not acquire (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.4.1.1">Chapter 4, Note 2</a>), the buyer can require delivery at the going rate, if it is less than the one stipulated earlier, or annul the contract without being subject to judicial censure since he only paid for future delivery.</i>.<br>A person may lend to his sharecroppers wheat for wheat<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">He gives them a certain amount as seed grain and receives the same amount back at harvest time. As a commercial transaction this is forbidden since in case the rate at the new harvest was higher than at sowing time there would be an element of interest (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.7.1">Note 180</a>). But seed grain for the landlord’s own domain is an investment, not a loan.</i> as seed grain but not as food; but Rebban Gamliel lent his sharecroppers wheat grain; if at high price and the rate went down or at low price and the rate went up, he took from them at the low rate<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of the Mishnah would have been unnecessary since it already was stated that investments do not fall under the rules of loans, were it not that Rabban Gamliel (in the low-inflation environment of Nerva’s and Trajan’s times) used to provide his sharecroppers with seed grain for which he determined the price and then at the next harvest took it back at the lower of last year’s and this year’s prices, to avoid even the semblance of taking interest.</i>, not because this is practice but because he wanted to be strict with himself.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not contract for produce,” etc. May one contract based on Saracens’ rates<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">The rate quoted by Arab traders who in most cases are itinerant and might not be around at the time of delivery.</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan says, one may contract; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, one may not contract<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 72b" href="/Bava_Metzia.72b">Babli, 72b</a>, this is attributed to R. Joḥanan and accepted as practice.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: Would it not be reasonable that Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish agree with Rebbi Joḥanan about rates of Saracens of Sepphoris, since that is uninterrupted<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">While it is the going rate on a Saracens’ market, it is not called “Saracens’ rate”.</i>? Rebbi Abun bar Cahana said, I am wondering about what the rabbis disagree? Whether contracting for produce<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Contracting for a fixed amount of produce.</i>, or contracting for money<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Fixing the unit price.</i>, or a loan on produce<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Taking a loan of produce now to be repaid at the next harvest. If at that time the rate is expected to be higher, this certainly is forbidden (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.1.1">Note 6</a>). But if 6 months before the next harvest the prices probably are higher, this might be permitted.</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah said, they disagree about a loan, but about (a hostelry) [a contract]<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is L’s, a scribal error. The text in brackets is E’s.</i> everybody agrees that it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Since the seller cannot be sure to find the merchandise at a reasonable price if the exchange does not exist permanently.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, they disagree about a contract, but about a loan everybody agrees that it is permitted. In the House of Rebbi Yannai they said, one contracts for cattle feed<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Mostly taken year round from the spontaneous growth of fallow fields.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, also on honeycombs. Rebbi Joḥanan said, in all villages near Tiberias one contracts once the rate was established at Tiberias.
“If he was the first to harvest, he may contract for the stack of sheaves,” etc. Rav said, if one processing stage was missing, one may contract; if several processing stages were missing, one may not contract<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">The farmer may contract to sell his produce if no more than one operation is needed to prepare the produce as it is customarily delivered.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish both say, even if several processing stages were missing, one may contract. But the Mishnah disagrees with Rav: “If he was the first to harvest,<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Since the vintner may contract to sell wine once he harvested his grapes, which have to be transported to the wine press, be pressed, the juice fermented, filtered, and cured, it is obvious that Rav is in error. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 74a" href="/Bava_Metzia.74a">Babli 74a</a>, Rav is quoted as permitting two processing stages but not three; Samuel represents the opinion here attributed to the Galilean Sages.</i>” etc.
Abba bar Zamina gave a denar to the food merchant and took from him at the cheapest rate of the entire year. Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">A name must be missing here since the first generation Amora Rav could not express an opinion about dealings three generations later.</i> did not agree. Rebbi Ḥiyya the Elder had flax. The donkey drivers wanted to buy from him. He told them, it is not my intention to sell this before Purim<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">When one would expect the price to be the highest of the year.</i>. They said to him, sell it to us now at the price which you would sell it for at Purim<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">They intended to transport the flax to a region where flax was more expensive, and pay him on their return. The flax then is a loan and the higher payment later is a “premium for waiting.”</i>. He went to ask Rebbi, who told him, it is forbidden. He went out, formulated this in a <i>baraita,</i> and stated the following: <sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta 4:23; a different version in the Babli, 62b.</i>“If one was owing money and the other wanted to take from him on the threshing floor, when he told him, credit it to my account by the market rate<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">The wholesale rate at harvest time, which is the lowest of the entire year.</i> and I shall provide you all twelve months<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">Paying his debt by delivery of produce in installments at a reduced rate implies hidden interest payments.</i>; this is forbidden for it is not as if the <i>assarion</i> came to his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the Mishnah, where money changes hands at the time the contract is signed.</i>.” Rav said, my uncle Rebbi Ḥiyya will agree that if they paid him and he immediately acquired, then it is permitted. If he paid beforehand and acquired afterwards, it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">This is the case of the Mishnah.</i>; if he acquired earlier and paid afterwards, it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">They intended to transport the flax to a region where flax was more expensive, and pay him on their return. The flax then is a loan and the higher payment later is a “premium for waiting.”</i>.
Rebbi Eleazar invested a denar with a person and told him, what it earns between now and Ḥanukkah is mine [and yours]<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E, split 50–50. In the light of the following comments, this seems to be the correct reading.</i>, afterwards I do not have any part with you, whether they earn or lose it is yours<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">The trader did not have to return the investment immediately.</i>. He wanted to give to him after Ḥanukkah but he did not accept<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">Since the trader bore the entire risk, R. Eleazar did not accept riskless income which is interest.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar had misgivings because of “close to gain and far from loss,” as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 4:16" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).4.16">Tosephta 4:16</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 70a" href="/Bava_Metzia.70a">Babli 70a</a>.</i>: “Close to gain and far from loss is the way of the wicked<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">As the Tosephta states, the yield of an investment in which the investor is partially shielded from risk is partially interest and therefore forbidden.</i>. Close to loss and far from gain is the way of the just. Close and far equally to both is the way of everybody<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">As long as risk is evenly distributed among all investors, the question of interest does not arise.</i>.” Rebbi Isaac invested denars with a person. He wanted to stipulate as did Rebbi Eleazar but the other did not accept<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">He did not want to appear as wicked to let R. Isaac feel just.</i>. Cahana invested 40 denars with a person to buy flax for him. Flax went up in price<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">When they sold the flax and made money.</i>. He went to ask Rav, who told him to go and collect 40 large <i>kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">Money made on investments is not interest. (A gold denar is given as the normal price for a <i>kor</i> of grain, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.1.1">Note 3</a>. The excess of a large over a regular <i>kor</i> represents Cahana’s gain.) In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 103a" href="/Bava_Kamma.103a">Babli, <i>Bava qamma</i> 103a</a>, Rav similarly holds that if from the start the money was given as investment, Cahana was entitled to the gain, but not if it was given as a loan.</i>.
Rebbi wanted to permit the ocean’s absorption but Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose did not admit it; some say that Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose wanted to permit but Rebbi did not admit it. What is the ocean’s absorption? A person gave 100 denars to another. [He tells him, my yield is 20 denars. If it is less, it is on your account, if it is more, it is for your account. If it is lost, it is of both<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">Money made on investments is not interest. (A gold denar is given as the normal price for a <i>kor</i> of grain, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.1.1">Note 3</a>. The excess of a large over a regular <i>kor</i> represents Cahana’s gain.) In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 103a" href="/Bava_Kamma.103a">Babli, <i>Bava qamma</i> 103a</a>, Rav similarly holds that if from the start the money was given as investment, Cahana was entitled to the gain, but not if it was given as a loan.</i>. Rebbi Ḥuna said,]<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">Addition from E.</i> for instance those who give merchandise to those who depart overseas to the stadia<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">Probably Greece or Southern Italy. The text of E is לכיסין; Lieberman reads לְנִיסִין, Greek νῆσοι “islands”.</i> for two or three <i>xestes</i><sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">A ξέστης is the equivalent of a Roman <i>sextarius,</i> as a measure about .53 1. Maybe the idea is that the gain from a successful voyage overseas brings in enough money to be measured by volume (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.6.4.1"><i>Yebamot</i> 6:4, Note 75</a>).</i>. That is not interest but property.
Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose in the Mishnah denying an exception to the rules of interest for manure; R. Eleazar ben Azariah in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Sheviit 3:2" href="/Mishnah_Sheviit.3.2">Mishnah <i>Ševi‘it</i> 3:2 (Notes 18–22</a>) denying an exception to the rules of the Sabbatical year for accumulation of manure.</i> said the same thing, as we have stated there: “If he had little, he may continuously add to it, but Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah says only if he raises or lowers it by three [hand-breadths] or puts it on a rock.”

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person may not say to another, lend me a <i>kor</i> of wheat and I shall return it to you from the threshing-floor<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">This is the general prohibition of lending commodities against the same amount of commodities since lending and repayment might represent very different values on the spot market.</i>, but he may tell him, lend me until my son comes or until I find the key<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">Short term loans for which no big change in commodity prices is to be expected.</i>, but Hillel forbids. Similarly, Hillel<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">While the records of Hillel’s time, the reigns of Caesar and Augustus, do not indicate inflationary pressures, it is possible that in Egypt and the neighboring countries the price of wheat was very sensitive to the requisitions for the provision of Rome and therefore unpredictable.</i> said that a woman may not lend a loaf to another unless she fixed its price, for possibly wheat may rise in price and they would be entangled in problems of interest.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A person may not say to another,” etc. It was stated: “A person may not say to another, lend me a <i>kor</i> of wheat and I shall return it to you from the threshing-floor,” therefore for two or three weeks it is permitted; “but Hillel forbids.<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">E adds: “But he may tell him, lend me until my son comes or until I find the key, but Hillel forbids;” therefore two or three weeks is forbidden. The formulation of L shows that his short text is original; the implication of both texts is the same.</i>” Samuel said, practice follows Hillel<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 75a" href="/Bava_Metzia.75a">Babli 75a</a>; the editors of the Babli reject Samuel’s statement.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A man may say to another, weed with me and I shall be weeding with you, hoe with me and I shall be hoeing with you, but he shall not tell him, weed with me and I shall be hoeing with you, hoe with me and I shall be weeding with you. All the dry season is one, all the wet season is not; one may not say to him, plough with me in the dry season and I shall be ploughing with you in the wet season<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">One may not offer to exchange agricultural work against agricultural work if these would represent vastly different expenditures if they needed hired hands.</i>.<br>Rabban Gamliel says, there exists predated and postdated interest. How is this? If one indended to borrow from him and sent him [a gift] saying, so you should lend me; that is predated interest. If one had borrowed and returned the money when he sent him [a gift] saying, that is for your money which was not earning for you while it was with me; that is postdated interest. Rebbi Simeon says, there exists verbal interest. One should not say to him, you should know that X came from place Y<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">To let the debtor take care of the guest for the creditor.</i>.<br>The following transgress prohibitions: The creditor, and the debtor, and the guarantor, and the witnesses; the Sages say, also the scribe. They transgress “do not give,<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:37" href="/Leviticus.25.37"><i>Lev.</i> 25:37</a>.</i>” and “do not take from him,<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:36" href="/Leviticus.25.36"><i>Lev.</i> 25:36</a></i>” and “do not be a creditor,<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:24" href="/Exodus.22.24"><i>Ex.</i> 22:24</a>.</i>” and “do not burden him with interest,<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:24" href="/Exodus.22.24"><i>Ex.</i> 22:24</a>.</i>” and “before a blind man do not put an obstacle; and fear your God, I am the Eternal.<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:14" href="/Leviticus.19.14"><i>Lev.</i> 19:14</a>. While the preceding verses spell out the guilt of the parties, this verse explains the guilt of the scribe, who is supposedly learned in the law and has to know that he participates in an illegal activity. The extended interpretation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:14" href="/Leviticus.19.14"><i>Lev.</i> 19:14</a> is one of the signs of pharisaic doctrine; it is intimated that the Sadducees, the opponents of the Sages, would absolve the scribe from guilt.</i>”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The following transgress prohibitions,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 6:17-18" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).6.17-18">Tosephta 6:17–18</a>; a short quote also in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 75b" href="/Bava_Metzia.75b">Babli, 75b</a>.</i>“Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">One has to read R. Yose, the Tanna, instead of R. Yasa, the Amora.</i> said, come and see how blind are the eyes of people who lend on interest. If a person calls another one idolator, or adulterer, or murderer, the other will want to destroy him. But this one pays witnesses and a scribe and tells them, come and testify that I negated the roots [of Judaism]. Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar said, they negate much more than the roots, for they declare the Torah false<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πλαστός, ή, όν, “fabricated”.</i> and Moses an idiot and say, if Moses had known how much we are earning, he would not have written it. Rebbi Aqiba says, interest is difficult since even a favor can be interest. If one<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote">If the creditor uses the debtor to run errands for him without paying for the latter's time, it is interest.</i> told him to buy vegetables on the market for him, even though he gave him the money, this is interest. Rebbi Simeon said, interest is difficult since even greeting can be interest. If he never greeted him but because he took a loan from him he is quick to greet him, this is interest<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta, this is R. Aqiba’s statement.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 71a" href="/Bava_Metzia.71a">Babli 71a</a>.</i> In this sense, Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar used to say, about a person who has money and does not lend it on interest the verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ps.</i> 15:6. “One who does this will never totter.”</i>: ‘his money he did not lend on interest,’ etc. Rebbi Samuel bar Immi<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta, R. Simeon or R. Shemaia. R. Simeon bar Immi was a third generation Amora. In <i>Midrash Ps.</i> 15(6), R. Samuel (ed. S. Buber, Note 31).</i> said, we would not know what this tottering was if Salomon had not come and explained: ‘to save those taken to death.’<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 24:11" href="/Proverbs.24.11"><i>Prov.</i> 24:11</a>: “To save those taken to death, and tottering to be killed, if you refrain.” The root used in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 24:11" href="/Proverbs.24.11"><i>Prov.</i> 24:11</a> is the same as that in <i>Ps.</i>15:6; it is presumed to have the same meaning. As usual, the argument is about those parts of the verses which are not quoted.</i>”

Chapter 6



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody hired tradesmen<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Independent contractors.</i> and they tricked one another<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">This Chapter is the continuation of Chapter Four. While in trade there is a notion of fair price, and overcharge or underpayment are reasons to annul the contract, there is no mechanism to establish fair rates for services (or labor wages, Chapter 7); the notions of overcharge or underpayment do not apply. In addition, a contract for services becomes final only with the start of work; before that time each party is in principle free to annul the contract at any moment. While this right is unalienable for workers (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 6:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.6.2.2">Halakhah 2</a>), for contractors it is restricted to cases where no irreparable loss would be caused to the other party.</i>, they only have mutual complaints on one another<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The injured party in general has no recourse in court. But if the injured party tells others about the bad treatment he received, it is no slander.</i>. If somebody hired a donkey driver, or a teamster<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Reading קרר “teamster” instead of קדר “potter”.</i>, carriage carriers<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Reading with D. Hoffmann πορεῖο φόροι. [Cf. Greek πόρευμα “means of transportation or φέρετρον “bier, litter, frame” (E.G.)] {Musaphia reads περιφέραια “circumference, circle” and interprets as “curved trumpets”. <i>Arukh</i> explains “a kind of litter” without indicating an etymology Rashi: prepared wood to construct a litter, φορεῖον.}</i>, or flute players for a bride or a burial, workers to bring his flax from the soaking pond, or anything in danger of being lost, if they reneged at a place where nobody is available<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">If the contractor had agreed to a certain price but then, when the employer was in a situation in which it was impossible for him to quickly find a replacement that would save him from a big loss, reneged on the contract and demanded a much higher price, he can either try to get a replacement at a much higher rate and then sue the original contractor for the difference, or he can agree to the exorbitant amount and then pay only the original rate with the blessing of the court.</i>, he hires at their expense or tricks them.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody hired tradesmen,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli has a completely different interpretation.</i> What means, they tricked one another? Come, work for me for five large [coins]. It turned out it was work for ten large [coins]<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The employer tricked the contractor.</i>. You work for me a job which needs ten days. It turned out it was work for five days<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The contractor tricked the employer.</i>. What means, [they]<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E, supported by Ran (Commentary to the Mishnah). L reads: The employer tricks them. Both statements apply.</i> tricked the householder? Come, work for me with your colleagues {for ten large [coins]. It turned out it was work for five large [coins]<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The contractor tricked the employer.</i>. You work for me a job which needs five days.}<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E, supported by Ran (Commentary to the Mishnah). L reads: The employer tricks them. Both statements apply.</i> It turned out it was work for ten days<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The employer tricked the contractor.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 7:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).7.1">Tosephta 7:1</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 76b" href="/Bava_Metzia.76b">Babli 76b</a>.</i>“When was this said; if the donkey drivers did not start yet. But if they went and did not find grain, or workers to weed and found the field flooded, he has to pay them for going and coming<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli and in 2 out of 3 Tosephta sources: “their fee completely.”</i>. But one cannot compare coming loaded with coming empty, or working with being idle, sitting in the shadow with sitting in the sun.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">When it would spoil in transport.</i>” Rebbi Ḥiyya the Elder hired donkey drivers to bring his flax. They went and found it wet<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">When it would spoil in transport.</i>. (Rav)<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The Tosephta and the Babli allow the employer to pay only for the actual work done, not the contract sum.</i> said to him, go and pay them their full fee. He said to them, not that I would be obligated to pay you your fee but I, Ḥiyya, am handing it over to you<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">As a voluntary gift; by this declaration he barred himself from appealing to the court to get his money back and established practice to pay the full fee due.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 7:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).7.1">Tosephta 7:1</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 76b" href="/Bava_Metzia.76b">Babli 76b</a>.</i>“When was this said? Before they started. But if he accepted to harvest a field for two tetradrachmas, harvested half and left half uncut, a garment to weave for two tetradrachmas, wove half and left half unwoven, one estimates for him. How does one estimate what he did? If what he did was worth six denar, he gives him a <i>sela‘</i> or he has to finish his work<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rashi, the anonymous Tanna holds that a contractor, like a hired worker, has an unlimited right to stop his work. Therefore, the contractor is always paid what is due him; for half the contract work done he must be paid half the contract sum. Even if the replacement contractor will charge 6 denars for work specified in the original contract for a <i>sela‘</i> or 4 denars, the additional costs are to be borne by the employer.</i>. If it was worth a tetradrachma he gives him a tetradrachma; Rebbi Dosa says, one estimates what remains to be done<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 67a" href="/Bava_Metzia.67a">Babli, 67a</a>, explains that R. Dosa follows <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 6:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.6.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> and restricts the unlimited right to leave work without notice to workers only. The contractor can only claim the difference between the contract sum and what was paid to his replacement.</i>. If what remains to be done was worth three denar, he gives him a <i>šeqel</i> or he has to finish his work<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This is difficult to understand. Instead of “3 denars”, in the Tosephta, the reading is “5 denars”, in the Babli “6 denars”. The Babli is clear: The contract sum was 8 denars. If the substitute contractor gets 6, the original one gets 2 denars, or one <i>šeqel.</i> S. Lieberman conjectures that in L the numeral was spelled out, as it is in E, and that שלשה was a copyist’s error for ששה, the Babli’s reading.</i>. If it was a <i>šeqel,</i> he gives him a <i>šeqel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">This reading is confirmed by the Tosephta; the Babli reads as in the preceding sentence, “if a tetradrachma, he gives a tetradrachma.” M. Margalit in <i>Pene Moshe</i> (supported by D. Pardo, <i>Ḥasde David</i>) writes that in case the first contractor finished exactly half the job, he is paid the smaller of half the contract sum or the amount another contractor takes to finish the job. If that would be one <i>šeqel,</i> the original contractor also gets only one <i>šeqel</i>; since he broke the contract, the earlier obligation of his employer was abrogated.</i>.” Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rav Ḥuna, Rebbi Bina, Rebbi Jeremiah<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian. The statement is not mentioned in the Babli but is accepted by Maimonides.</i> in the name of Rav: practice follows Rebbi Dosa.
<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 7:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).7.1">Tosephta 7:1</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 76b" href="/Bava_Metzia.76b">Babli 76b</a>.</i>“When was this said? If nothing is in danger to be lost. But if it is in danger to be lost, he hires others on his account<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, the anonymous Tanna agrees with R. Dosa that the original contractor cannot claim more than the difference between the original contract sum and the amount paid to the second contractor.</i> or tricks him and tells him, I contracted with you for one tetradrachma, I shall give you two. He takes from one and gives to the other.” Rebbi Ila said, only up to his wages<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">If the substitute contractor charges more than the original amount, the defaulting contractor cannot be sued for the difference. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 78a" href="/Bava_Metzia.78a">Babli 78a</a>).</i>. So far, only up to his wages for that day, or only up to his wages for that hour? <sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 7:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).7.1">Tosephta 7:1</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 76b" href="/Bava_Metzia.76b">Babli 76b</a>.</i>“When was this said? At a place where no workers are ready to be hired. But if one saw that donkey drivers approached, or that he was unloading his ship in a port<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λιμήν.</i>, he<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The first contractor can get out of the contract without penalty. “He” is the contractor,” “him” the employer.</i> may tell him, go and hire yourself one of these; he only has a complaint on him.”

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody hired tradesmen<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Independent contractors.</i> and they backed out they are disadvantaged<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The contractor can only claim the difference between the contract sum and what was paid to his substitute..</i>; if the householder backs out, he is disadvantaged<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">He has to pay the contractor’s claims in full.</i>. Anyone changing the terms of a contract is disadvantaged<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 9:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.9.5.1">Mishnah <i>Bava Qamma</i> 9:5</a>.</i>; anyone backing out of a contract is disadvantaged.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody hired tradesmen,” etc. Rav said, “because the Children of Israel are My servants<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:55" href="/Leviticus.25.55"><i>Lev.</i> 25:55</a>.</i>,” Jews cannot buy one another. Rebbi Joḥanan said, this was stated about a Hebrew slave<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The verse is in the paragraph about a Hebrew slave of a Gentile, who has to be freed in the Jubilee year.</i>. In Rav’s opinion, both a worker and an employer can back out; in Rebbi Joḥanan’s opinion the worker can back out but not the employer<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the attributions have to be switched. Rav, who quotes the verse, holds that a contract which bars a worker from quitting his job is invalid as “stipulating against the words of the Torah”, must hold that any employment contract which specifies a fixed term of employment can be enforced only against the employer, not the employee. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 10a" href="/Bava_Metzia.10a">Babli 10a</a> this is universally accepted. This does not exclude that the worker who quits his job in the middle of work be financially disadvantaged (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 77a" href="/Bava_Metzia.77a">Babli 77a</a>). R. Joḥanan, who holds that the verse is irrelevant in the context, must read the Mishnaiot as applying both to contractors and workers; both can renege on their obligations if they accept the penalties spelled out there.</i>.

Halakhah 3
