<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Somebody who rented a donkey to lead it on the mountain but led it in the valley, in the valley but led it on the mountain, even if both ways were ten <i>mil,</i> if it died he has to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Even though a renter does not have to pay if the rented animal dies (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.7.6.1">Mishnah 7:9</a>), since he “changed the terms of a contract, he is disadvantaged.”</i>. If he rented a donkey and it developed eye trouble<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Arabic بَرِقَ “to have trouble seeing”.</i> or it was taken to government service<sup class="footnote-marker">32a</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἀγγαρεία.</i>, he tells him<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The owner tells the renter that this is a common risk which anybody working with animals has to live with; he does not have to provide him with another animal.</i>, here is your property before you. If it died or broke a limb, he has to provide him<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The owner has to provide the renter with a new animal.</i> with another donkey.<br>Somebody rented a donkey to lead it on the mountain but led it in the valley; if it slipped, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">A mountain donkey can be presumed to be sure-footed. If it slipped it is not the lessee’s fault; the lessor cannot sue the lessee even<br>though the lessee violated the conditions of the lease.</i>; if it suffered a heat stroke, he is liable. In the valley, but he led it on the mountain; if it slipped, he is liable; if it suffered a heat stroke, he is not liable but if it happened because of the ascent<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">If the donkey died of heat stroke while climbing in the mountains, the lessor can sue the lessee for the value of the donkey since the lessee violated the conditions of the lease.</i>, he is liable.<br>Somebody rented a cow to plough<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">He rented the plough with the cow.</i> on the mountain but ploughed in the valley, if the plough’s peg<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The central peg with which the plough is steered; the most delicate part of the plough; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:4:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.4.12"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:4, Note 206</a>.</i> broke, he is not liable. In the valley but he ploughed on the mountain, if the plough’s peg broke, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">For the uneven terrain puts more stress on the steering mechanism.</i>. To thresh legumes<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Beans or peas.</i> but he threshed grain, he is not liable; to thresh grain but he threshed legumes, he is liable, for legumes make [the floor] slippery.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Somebody who rented a donkey,” etc. One understands on the mountain, but he led it in the valley. In the valley, but he led it on the mountain? Rebbi Simeon ben Yaqim said, if it died because of the air<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Even though mountain air is healthier, some animals cannot stand the change in climate. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 78a" href="/Bava_Metzia.78a">Babli, 78a</a>, the argument is inverted.</i>. Rebbi Dositheos ben Yannai said, if it was bitten by a snake<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Which are infrequent in agricultural areas.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is Rebbi Meïr’s who said, anybody who deviates from the owner’s instructions is called a robber<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 2:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.2.1.10"><i>Qiddušin</i> 2:1, Note 70</a>.</i>.
“If he rented a donkey and it developed eye trouble,” it was stumbling<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">This is Rava’s explanation in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 78b" href="/Bava_Metzia.78b">Babli, 78b</a>.</i>. “Or it was taken to government service.” There are Tannaїm who state, government service is like death<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 7:7" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).7.7">Tosephta 7:7</a>; in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 78b" href="/Bava_Metzia.78b">Babli 78b</a> this is a statement of Rav.</i>; but there are Tannaїm who state, he tells him, here is your property before you. He who stated, government service is like death, if he<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The donkey’s owner with the government official who wants to take the donkey, to make sure the animal is returned to him after a short period.</i> can find an accomodation; he who says, he tells him, here is your property before you, if he cannot find an accomodation.
<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 7:7" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).7.7">Tosephta 7:7</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 78b" href="/Bava_Metzia.78b">Babli 78b</a>. For the entire paragraph cf. <i>Tosefta kiFshutah Bava Meṣia‘</i> pp. 251–252.</i>“It was stated, Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says, if it happened on the road, he does not have to provide him with another donkey; if it did not happen on the road, he has to provide him with another donkey.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">If the donkey was taken by the government while it was on the road, the owner may say to the lessee that it was his bad luck to be in the wrong place at the wrong time. But if the officials went to look in all the barns, it could not have been the lessee’s bad luck and the owner has to provide a replacement. (Explanation of R. Hananel, quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Bava Metzia 78b" href="/Tosafot_on_Bava_Metzia.78b">Tosaphot 78b</a>. <i>s. v.</i>אם.)</i>” Rebbi Abbahu said in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: At a short-cut<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>compendiarium</i>[<i>iter</i>]; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.13"><i>Berakhot</i> 1:1, Note 36</a>. Since the government officials found him alone on a narrow path, it is his fault that the animal was taken.</i> he does not have to provide him with another donkey; on the king’s highway<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Greek (ὁδός) βασιλική. Since everybody walks there, the lessee is not to blame. This argument is roughly equivalent to that of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 6:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.6.3.4">Note 42</a>.</i> he has to provide him with another donkey. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: If he hired it from here to Lydda and it was taken into government service from here to Lydda, he does not have to provide him with another donkey<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">This is a temporary inconvenience; the donkey will be available to the lessee after a short interval.</i>; from here to Lydda and it was taken into government service from here to Tyre<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">It is up to the owner, not the lessee, to go to Tyre and to reclaim his animal. The lessee cannot be left without a beast of burden for a very long time.</i>, he has to provide him with another donkey.
“If it died or broke a limb, he has to provide him with a donkey.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, if [the lessee] requested “a donkey” without specifications. But if he requested “this donkey”, [the lessor] may tell him, you wanted this one, bring it before us. Rav Ḥuna said, he<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The lessee, not the lessor is obligated to sell the carcass or the injured animal.</i> has to deal with it on place. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, if he can get enough from his effort to lease a small donkey, he does not need to provide him with another donkey. Otherwise, he has to provide him with another donkey. Rebbi Ḥuna<sup class="footnote-marker">47a</sup><i class="footnote">In E: Rebbi Ila.</i> said, if the cadaver is worth enough to buy a small donkey, he does not need to provide him with another donkey. Otherwise, he has to provide him with another donkey<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The dispute between R. Ze‘ira and R. Ḥuna is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 79a" href="/Bava_Metzia.79a">Babli, 79a</a>, as a dispute between Samuel and Rav. Samuel and R. Ze‘ira hold that the lessee is empowered to use the proceeds for his needs within the terms of the lease; only an eventual residual would go to the lessor. Rav and R. Ḥuna hold that the receipts for the carcass or the injured animal represent capital which, therefore, has to be delivered to the lessor who for his part is required to provide a substitute animal to the lessee.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Somebody who rented a donkey to use it to transport wheat but transported barley, grain but transported straw, is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah in the Babli and the independent Mishnah mss. add: Because volume is difficult to carry (Maimonides’s text: because volume is as difficult as weight.) The specific weight of barley is less than that of wheat; the same specified weight will mean a larger load for barley than for wheat. The Mishnah also adds that the standard volume to be carried by a donkey is a <i>letekh,</i> 15 <i>se’ah,</i> about 190 liter.</i>. How much did one add to the load that he became liable? Symmachos said in the name of Rebbi Meïr: one <i>se’ah</i> for a camel, three <i>qab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Half a <i>se’ah,</i> about 6.4 1.</i> for a donkey<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">A contract to carry a certain volume allows an excess volume of 1/30 of the stipulated amount. This makes the standard load carried by a camel 1 <i>kor,</i> 30 <i>se’ah</i>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Somebody who rented a donkey,” etc. “How much did one add to the load that he became liable? Symmachos said in the name of Rebbi Meïr: one <i>se’ah</i> for a camel, three <i>qab</i> for a donkey, one <i>qab</i> for a porter, and three <i>se‘ah</i> for a cart. But a ship according to its size”<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 7:10" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).7.10">Tosephta 7:10</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 80b" href="/Bava_Metzia.80b">Babli 80b</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 7:13" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).7.13">Tosephta 7:13</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 116b" href="/Bava_Kamma.116b">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i> 116b</a>.</i>“If a caravan was attacked by soldiers<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of גייס is as variable as the corresponding Arabic جيش “troup; army; soldiers”. In this context it is presumed that they came to plunder, not to kill. Therefore, what is left after they are gone is divided proportionally to the value of the original cargoes.</i> one computes according to value but not according to persons. If they sent a scout ahead of them, one computes<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Payment for the scout’s salary. In Tosephta and Babli: “One computes <i>also</i> according to the number of persons.”</i> according to persons. One does not deviate from the usage of caravans<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Local rules always supersede general rules.</i>.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 7:14" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).7.14">Tosephta 7:14</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 116b" href="/Bava_Kamma.116b">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i> 116b</a>.</i>“If a ship encounters a calamity<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear from the frequent mention of נַחְשׁוֹל שֶׁבַּיָּם whether the reference be to a storm or to a single giant wave. The translation compares the word to Arabic نحس “unlucky, ominous, calamitous, disastrous”.</i> and one lightens its load, one computes according to weight and value but not according to persons. But one who rents a carriage or a ship from another person computes according to load and persons but not according to value<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">For the transporter, the value of the merchandise carried should not make any difference.</i>.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 8:25" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).8.25">Tosephta 8:25</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 116b" href="/Bava_Kamma.116b">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i> 116b</a>.</i>“If a caravan was attacked by soldiers<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of גייס is as variable as the corresponding Arabic جيش “troup; army; soldiers”. In this context it is presumed that they came to plunder, not to kill. Therefore, what is left after they are gone is divided proportionally to the value of the original cargoes.</i> and one of its members managed to save something from their hand, he saved equally [for all]; but if they gave him permission<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of the Babli this refers to the case of a prior agreement that in case of an attack, everyone will keep what he can save. This agrees with the explicit rule for donkey caravans which prescribes a prior arrangement by a court document.</i>, he saved for himself. If donkey-drivers were attacked by robbers and one of theirs managed to save something from their hand, he saved equally [for all]; but if he stipulated with them in court, he saved for himself. If toll collectors forgave to partners, they forgave equally [for all]; but if they indicated, we forgave to Mr. X, what they forgave, they forgave him.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 8:25" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).8.25">Tosephta 8:25</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 94b" href="/Bava_Kamma.94b">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i> 94b</a>.</i>“The repentance of publicans and toll collectors is difficult<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Their problem is the generally accepted doctrine (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 8:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.8.7.1">Mishnah <i>Yoma</i> 8:9</a>) that sins between man and fellow man are not forgiven by Heaven unless the sinner asks forgiveness from his fellow man. Since under the antique system of tax farming, neither the tax- nor the toll-collector could make a living if they only exacted the legal amounts, they will have sinned against a great number of people whom they do not know by name.</i>. They should return to those whom they know; [moneys taken] from people they do not know they should use for the public good.”

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> All artisans are paid trustees<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">As such, he is liable if the article was stolen or lost, in contrast to the unpaid trustee who swears but does not pay (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.7.6.1">Mishnah 7:9</a>). Since the artisan earns his money by creating or repairing other people’s possessions, he is rewarded for having them.</i>, but in any case where he said, take what is yours and bring money, he becomes an unpaid trustee<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">From the moment the client is informed that his order is ready, the artisan no longer has a monetary interest in keeping the object.</i>. Watch over what is mine and I shall watch over what is yours, paid trustee<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">The mutual services are worth money.</i>. Watch over what is mine, and he said, put it down before you, an unpaid trustee<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">As the Halakhah points out, the Mishnah really should read: “put it down before <i>me</i>”, as in the Babli and the Mishnah manuscript corrected by Maimonides in his autograph Commentary.</i>. He who gives a loan on a pledge is a paid trustee<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The pledge will save the creditor an application to the court for forclosure in case the loan was not repaid. This convenience is worth money in the opinion of this Tanna.</i>; Rebbi Jehudah said, if he lent money, he is an unpaid trustee<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion, the possibility of a future advantage is not money’s worth today.</i>, if produce, a paid trustee<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Giving a loan in produce against a pledge transfers the risk of spoilage to the borrower. This is an immediate advantage to the creditor which makes him a paid trustee.</i>. Abba Shaul said, a person is authorized to rent out a poor person’s pledge to continuously slice off [from the loan] because he is like a person who returns lost property<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The creditor of a loan on a pledge by a poor person has automatic permission to transform the loan into an antichretic loan in which the yield of the pledge is used to amortize (“slice off”) the loan. Once the loan is repaid, the pledge is returned to the borrower who otherwise probably would have no possibility of redeeming his pledge. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 4:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.4.6.3"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 4:6, Note 168</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “All artisans are paid trustees,” etc. This ἀντίχρησις<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">In E correctly: אנטיכריסיס “Substitution of usufruct for interest.” The antichretic loan described by Abba Shaul is unquestionably permitted since it is easily verifiable that all earnings of the pledge be used to reduce the amount of the loan. What is questionable is a loan in which the creditor himself uses the pledge and credits the borrower’s account with a specified amount per accounting period. It is held that such a set-up automatically contains a certain amount of interest and is permissible only between Jews and Gentiles.</i> is interest.
Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E and <i>editio princeps.</i> L has ששאל “that he asked”, a scribal error. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 81b" href="/Bava_Metzia.81b">Babli, 81b</a>, the statement is attributed to Rav’s student Rav Huna.</i> said, if he said, put it down before me. But if he said, put it down before yourself, he is neither an unapaid nor a paid trustee<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The Yerushalmi version really is a rejection of any responsibility by the person asked to act as trustee.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, a person is ready to give something so that he can sell his produce based on a pledge<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">He gives a reason for R. Jehudah to declare the lender of produce to be a paid trustee. The creditor not only transfers the risk of spoilage to the borrower but also has his claim covered by a pledge.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, a person is ready to give something so that he can sell his produce to somebody who will appease him by a pledge<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">He differs with R. Joḥanan in extending the status of paid trustee not only to the person who lends against a pledge but also to the lender without pledge who only receives a pledge later against extension of the loan for an additional period.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who transports an amphora from place to place and it breaks shall swear<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">That it did not happen because of his negligence.</i>, whether unpaid or paid trustee. Rebbi Eliezer said, both shall swear<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">This is the rule he was taught by his teachers.</i>, but I am wondering whether any of them be able to swear<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Since the paid trustee is liable whether it was his fault or not, why and about what should he swear? The unpaid trustee is not liable if he swears, but how can he be sure that nothing he did contributed to the accident?</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who transports an amphora from place to place,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Yose ben Ḥanina was a second generation Amora, this expression is inappropriate.</i>: <sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 83a" href="/Bava_Metzia.83a">Babli, 83a</a>, the story is told about Rabba bar bar Ḥana and Rav. Its upshoot is that while the teamster who breaks something in transport is liable, if he is poor one should not insist on the letter of the law.</i> Rebbi Nehemiah the potter gave his pots to a person who broke them. He<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">R. Nehemiah the potter. The reading is confirmed by <i>Kaftor wa-Peraḥ</i> 44 (ed. Luncz, Jerusalem 1899, p. 615) against the opinion of S. Lieberman who here suspects a lacuna.</i> confiscated his<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The transporter.</i> coat. He<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The transporter.</i> went to Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina, who told him, go and tell him: <sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 2:20" href="/Proverbs.2.20"><i>Prov.</i> 2:20</a>.</i>“That you should go in the ways of good people.” He<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The transporter.</i> went and told him<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">R. Nehemiah the potter. The reading is confirmed by <i>Kaftor wa-Peraḥ</i> 44 (ed. Luncz, Jerusalem 1899, p. 615) against the opinion of S. Lieberman who here suspects a lacuna.</i> who then returned his coat. He<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose ben Ḥanina.</i> asked him, did he<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">R. Nehemiah the potter. The reading is confirmed by <i>Kaftor wa-Peraḥ</i> 44 (ed. Luncz, Jerusalem 1899, p. 615) against the opinion of S. Lieberman who here suspects a lacuna.</i> pay your wages? He<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The transporter.</i> said, no. He<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose ben Ḥanina.</i> told him, go and tell him: “And to the paths of the just you shall keep.” He<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The transporter.</i> went and told him<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">R. Nehemiah the potter. The reading is confirmed by <i>Kaftor wa-Peraḥ</i> 44 (ed. Luncz, Jerusalem 1899, p. 615) against the opinion of S. Lieberman who here suspects a lacuna.</i> who then paid his wages.

Chapter 7



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who hires workers<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">On daily wages.</i> and tells them to start early and work late<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">It is universally accepted that a working day is from sunrise to sundown. An early riser starts work at dawn and a late worker works until dusk.</i>, at a place where it is not customary to start early or work late cannot force them to do so. At a place where it is customary to feed them, he has to feed them; to supply a snack, he has to supply it, always following local custom. It happened that Rebbi Joḥanan ben Matthew said to his son, go and hire workers for us. He awarded them food, but when he returned to his father, the latter told him, even if you serve them Solomon’s meal in his time<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">30 <i>kor</i> fine flour, 60 <i>kor</i> coarse flour, 10 feed-lot cattle, 20 grass fed cattle, 100 sheep, also fallow-buck, gazelle, roe-buck, and fattened geese (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 5:3" href="/I_Kings.5.3"><i>1K.</i> 5:3</a>).</i> you did not fulfill your pledge since they are descendants of Abraham, Isaac, and Jacob. But before they start work go and tell them: On condition that your only claim be for bread and legumes. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, that was unnecessary; one always follows local custom.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who hires workers,” etc. Rav Hoshaia said, this means that usage supersedes practice<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">At least in civil law. As a statement of Rav this also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 12:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.12.1.9"><i>Yebamot</i>12:1, Note 21</a>. The rule is never stated explicitly in the Babli but has generated a very great number of responsa in all periods of rabbinic literature.</i>. Rebbi Immi said, everywhere the burden of proof is on the claimant except here<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In employment law, the party accused of not following local custom has to prove that in fact he is following it.</i>.
In Tiberias one does not work early or late<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">It is universally accepted that a working day is from sunrise to sundown. An early riser starts work at dawn and a late worker works until dusk.</i>. In Bet Ma‘on<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">A place on the slope of the mountain overlooking Tiberias (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.8.3"><i>Soṭah</i>1:8, Note 280</a>). According to Josephus, <i>Vita</i> 12, it is situated 4 stadia from Tiberias, less than 600 m.</i> one works early and late. People from Tiberias who climb up to be hired in Bet Ma‘on are hired on the terms of Bet Maon. People from Bet Ma‘on who descend to Tiberias are hired on the terms of Tiberias. But an employer from Tiberias who climbs up to hire workers in Bet Maon can tell them, you should not think that I could not find workers to hire in Tiberias but came up because I heard that you start early and work late<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The hiring contract always follows the local custom of the place of contracting, not the place of work.</i>. At a place where there is no common usage, Jehudah ben Buni, Rebbi Immi, Rav Jehudah: It is a stipulation of the court that the early part of morning belong to the workers and the late part of evening to the employer<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">A workday for a journeyman starts at sunrise and ends at nightfall.</i>. What is the reason? “You make darkness, there is night; the young lions roar for prey; You give to them, they are collecting; the sun shines, they return; man leaves for his work<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 104:19-23" href="/Psalms.104.19-23"><i>Ps.</i> 104:19–23</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 104:28" href="/Psalms.104.28">28</a>. The only relevant verses are <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 104:22" href="/Psalms.104.22">v. 22–23</a>: “The sun shines … man goes out to work, his labors until evening.” This implies that a workday does not start before sunrise but continues after sunset until nightfall. The same argument in the Babli, in the name of R. Simeon ben Laqish (83a/b).</i>.” (In the evening)<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of L, rejected by all commentators.</i> [Fridays]<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E.</i> both early rising and late going is at the employer’s expense<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">On Friday a full working day does not start before sunrise but only continues until the later afternoon, leaving enough time for the worker to return home and prepare for the Sabbath before sundown.</i>. How much? That he may fill an amphora of water, fry himself a fish<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The quickest way to prepare cooked food.</i>, and light the candle.
In the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan ben Matthew<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Who relies neither on local usage nor on the stipulations of the Mishnah.</i>, a man who goes to another place to preliminarily marry a woman has to stipulate with her and tell her, on condition that you work so and so much and eat for such and such costs.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following eat by Torah law<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The agricultural worker empowered by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:25-26" href="/Deuteronomy.23.25-26"><i>Deut.</i> 23:25–26</a> to eat from grain and grapes.</i>: He who works on produce connected to the ground at the completion of work<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The only time he may eat from standing produce is at harvest time if he is occupied in harvesting.</i>, or separated from the ground before the completion of work<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the produce is not subject to the laws of tithing, i. e., ready to be stored.</i>, only things grown from the earth<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The worker at a hydroponic farm has no right to eat from the produce, nor may the worker in a dairy drink from the milk.</i>.<br>But the following may not eat: He who works on produce connected to the ground before the completion of work<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The agricultural worker during the growing season.</i>, or separated from the ground after the completion of work<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The worker on food after it became subject to the laws of tithing.</i>, or things not grown from the earth<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The worker at a hydroponic farm has no right to eat from the produce, nor may the worker in a dairy drink from the milk.</i>.<br>If he was working with his hand but not with his feet, with his feet but not with his hands, even with his shoulder, he may eat. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says, only if he works with hands and feet<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Like the harvester of grain or grapes mentioned in the verse.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The following eat by Torah law,” etc. Rebbi La said, “if you come into your neighbor’s vineyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:25" href="/Deuteronomy.23.25"><i>Deut.</i> 23:25</a>: “If you come into your neighbor’s vineyard, you may eat grapes to satisfy yourself.” What else is there to eat in a vineyard?</i>” Could anybody think that he would eat wood or stones? Why does the verse say “grapes”? That he may not skin figs or suck grapes<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Different, tannaïtic, arguments are in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 87b" href="/Bava_Metzia.87b">Babli 87b</a>; <i>Ma‘śerot</i> 2:7 (Notes 142–149); <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 266.</i>.
“If he was working with his hand but not with his feet.” Rebbi La said, it is written: “If you come into your neighbor’s standing grain,” etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:26" href="/Deuteronomy.23.26"><i>Deut.</i> 23:26</a>. This quote is not followed up here; it refers to the discussion in <i>Ma‘serot</i> 2:6, Notes 122–123.</i> With his hands, binding. With his feet, he compresses. Even with his shoulder, he carries.
<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 8:7" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).8.7">Tosephta 8:7</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 91b" href="/Bava_Metzia.91b">Babli 91b</a>; <i>Ma‘serot</i> 2:6, Note 117.</i>“Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says, only if he works with hands and feet; this excludes him who works with his hands but not with his feet, with his feet but not with his hands.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 8:7" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).8.7">Tosephta 8:7</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 89a" href="/Bava_Metzia.89a">Babli 89a</a>; <i>Ma‘serot</i> 2:6, Notes 118–121. The argument refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:4" href="/Deuteronomy.25.4"><i>Deut.</i> 25:4</a>, the prohibition to muzzle the ox while he is threshing. It is declared that the rights of the laborer are no less than the rights of the ox. The other sources have an additional set of inferences which is quoted in E as:<br>דַּיַּיִשׁ מָה דַּיַּיִשׁ מְיוּחָד שֶׁהוּא תָּלוּשׁ מִן הַקַּרְקַע. יָצָא הַמְּעַדֵּר בַּגְּפָנִים הַמְּנַכֵּשׁ בְּשׁוּם וּבִבְצָלִים וְבָקַפְלוֹטִין דָּבָר שֶׁהוּא מְחוּבָּר לַקַּרְקַע.<br>Threshing, just as threshing is particular in that it applies to plucked [grain]. This excludes him who weeds among vines, weeds among garlic, and onions, and leeks, which are connected to the ground.<br>Since this text parallels a statement of the Tosephta and the Yerushalmi <i>Ma‘serot</i> but is formulated in the Yerushalmi <i>Neziqin</i> style, it has to be accepted as original; missing in L.</i>“Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah says, threshing. Since threshing is special in that it refers to something grown from the earth, this excludes the one who milks, makes cheese with rennet or with mold, which is not something grown from the earth. Since threshing is special in that it refers to something not completely processed; this excludes wine after it was fermented, [olive oil]<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">From E, missing in L.</i> after it descended into the vat, one who isolates dates or separates dried figs, done after complete processing. Since threshing comes before the obligation of tithes, this excludes him who kneads dough, separates into cakes, or bakes, which comes after it became obligated for tithes.”

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one was working on figs he may not eat grapes, on grapes he may not eat figs; but he may restrain himself until he reaches the place of the good ones and eat there<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The farmer cannot hinder his agricultural worker from eating of his best produce.</i>. In all cases they only referred to the time of finishing<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">This is also the reading of Alfasi and Maimonides; the latter infers from here (<i>Śekhirut</i> 12:2–3) that at harvest time the worker may eat only during breaks in harvesting, not while he is actually working; he may not harvest with his right hand and eat with his left. The word “finishing” is not in any Ashkenazic text, nor in the <i>editio princeps</i> of the Babli. The Provençal <i>Meïri</i> writes the word in his Mishnah text but not in the quotes from the Mishnah in his commentary.</i> work, but because of returning lost property to its owner<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">In order to minimize the time for which the employer pays but which the worker uses to eat during working hours, traditional rules encourage him to eat only while going from one job to another or waiting for others to finish a task from where he can start his work. A worker who does not follow this rule is within his rights but cannot be expected to be rehired.</i> they said that workers may eat when they go from row to row, and when they return from the wine press, and a donkey while offloading.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one was working on figs,” etc. Rebbi La said, it is written: “If you come into your neighbor’s vineyard, you may eat grapes to satisfy yourself.” What else is there in the vineyard to eat but grapes? To teach you that if he was working on grapes he may not eat figs<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:25" href="/Deuteronomy.23.25"><i>Deut.</i> 23:25</a>. E quotes the entire first part of the Mishnah which is from the text of <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 266 on this verse. It seems therefore that the text of L is original.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A worker may eat zucchini even for a denar’s worth, or dates even for a denar’s worth. Rebbi Eleazar Ḥasma said, a worker may not eat more than his wages<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">His wages for the day’s work. The Halakhah shows that this was less that a silver <i>denar.</i> In Mishnah <i>Ševi‘it</i>8:4 it is intimated that the daily wages might be as low as one <i>assarius</i> or a quarter denar. (In <i>Matth.</i> 20:2 one denar; but this is an aggadic text.)</i> and the Sages permit it<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 266, both parties learn from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:25" href="/Deuteronomy.23.25"><i>Deut.</i> 23:25</a>: “you may eat grapes to <i>satisfy yourself</i>.” For R. Eleazar Ḥasma the criterion is <i>yourself,</i> for the Sages <i>to satisfy.</i></i>, but one instructs a man not to be voracious lest he close the door before him<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody will hire him in the future if he is known to abuse his rights.</i>.<br>A person may contract for himself, for his adult son or daughter, for his adult male and female slaves, for his wife, because they have understanding. But he may not contract for his underage son or daughter, nor for his underage male and female slaves, nor for his animal, because they have no understanding<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">This Mishnah is explained only in a short section of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 92b-93a" href="/Bava_Metzia.92b-93a">Babli, 92b/93a</a>, which is summarized by Maimonides (<i>Śekhirut</i> 12:14): “A worker who worked together with his wife, children, and slaves for one employer may contract with the employer that none of them will eat from what they are working on. That is, if they are adults, because they have understanding and therefore can give up their rights. But for those underage he cannot contract, since they eat not from their father’s, nor from their employer’s, but from Heaven’s.” Cf. also <i>Ma‘serot</i> 2:6, Note 129.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A worker may eat zucchini even for a denar’s worth,” etc. Rebbi Eleazar ben Antigonos says in the name of Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yannai, this means that a worker may eat more than his [daily] wages<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">His wages for the day’s work. The Halakhah shows that this was less that a silver <i>denar.</i> In Mishnah <i>Ševi‘it</i>8:4 it is intimated that the daily wages might be as low as one <i>assarius</i> or a quarter denar. (In <i>Matth.</i> 20:2 one denar; but this is an aggadic text.)</i>. But some say, Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: This means that a worker may eat the first or the last bunch of grapes<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Both the first and the last bunches of grapes would fetch a premium on the market of fresh fruits; nevertheless the farmer may not prohibit his hired hand from eating them. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 93a" href="/Bava_Metzia.93a">Babli, 93a</a>, this is formulated as: Even if the entire yield is only one bunch, the worker may eat it.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody hires workers to work with him in his fourth-year plantation, they may not eat. If he did not inform them, he has to redeem it and feed them<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Newly planted fruit trees in their first three years have to be stripped of their fruits as forbidden <i>‘orlah.</i> In the fourth year the yield may be redeemed and then eaten in place, or brought to the Temple and eaten there in purity (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:23-24" href="/Leviticus.19.23-24"><i>Lev.</i> 19:23–24</a>; Tractate <i>‘Orlah</i>). Since eating from the fruit is a recognized privilege of agricultural workers, if they are not informed that they are to work on forbidden fruit they could not ask for higher wages to compensate for the fruit.</i>. If his fig cakes were dislodged or his amphoras opened, they may not eat. If he did not inform them, he has to tithe and feed them<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">It was stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.7.2.1">Mishnah 3</a> that workers on food processed to the stage of tithes have no privilege of eating from it. It is forbidden to eat titheable but untithed food If the workers were hired under the impression that they had the privilege, the employer has to provide them with permitted food.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody hires workers to work,” etc. Anything which is of a kind apt to be under the rules of tithing but another prohibition caused it [to be forbidden to eat], if one hired [a worker] without mentioning it, he has to redeem and feed, tithe and feed<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">This is the case of the Mishnah; the “other prohibition” is the fourth-year status of the orchard.</i>. If [the worker] contracted for it, he eats<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">This is obvious; any worker may contract to receive part or all of his wages as food. It is only needed here as a parallel to the next statement.</i>. But if it is of a kind exempt from the rules of tithing<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">For example, any unripe fruit as mentioned in <i>Ma‘serot</i> 1:2.</i> but another prohibition caused it [to be forbidden to eat], if one hired [a worker] without mentioning it, that one cannot eat<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">If this is from a fourth-year orchard, the worker has no claim to the fruit even if he was not informed beforehand.</i>; if he contracted for it, he eats.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Watchmen of produce<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">They do not actually work and are not covered by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.7.2.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i> eat because of local practice but not from the Torah. There are four kinds of keepers: The unpaid trustee and the borrower, the paid keeper and the renter. The unpaid trustee swears about everything<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">If anything happens to the goods entrusted to him he does not pay if he is able to swear that he did not take anything and that it was not his fault; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6-8" href="/Exodus.22.6-8"><i>Ex.</i> 22:6–8</a>.</i>, the borrower pays for everything. The paid keeper and the renter swear about the broken, the kidnapped, and the dead<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">They do not pay for what happened beyond their control; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:11-12" href="/Exodus.22.11-12"><i>Ex.</i>22:11–12</a>.</i> but pay for the lost and the stolen.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Watchmen of produce eat because of local practice,” etc. Rav Huna<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 93a" href="/Bava_Metzia.93a">Babli, 93a</a>, the opinion of Rav Huna is quoted as that of his teacher Rav; Samuel’s opinion is reproduced there.</i> said, the Mishnah refers to watchmen of produce<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">After the harvest.</i>. But watchmen of vegetable gardens and orchards eat by Torah law. Samuel said, the Mishnah refers to watchmen of vegetable gardens and orchards<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">They do not actually work and are not covered by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.7.2.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i>. But watchmen of produce eat neither by Torah law nor by local practice<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">If the produce is ready to be sold, it only has to be watched to prevent loss.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated in support of<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">This use of עַל when one would expect לְ may be the root of German-Jewish כָּשֵׁר עַל פֶּסַח “usable for Passover” instead of כָּשֵׁר לְפֶסַח used by all other Jewish groups.</i> Rav Huna: “The Cohen shall be impure until evening,” to include watchmen, that they make their garments impure<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the law of the Red Cow, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num.</i> 19</a>, in particular 19:7. The priest who supervises the burning of the cadaver of the red cow becomes impure simply by watching the procedure even if he touches nothing and does nothing himself. This proves that watching alone is counted as an activity in Torah law, as asserted by Rav Huna.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The next four Mishnaiot deal with the responsibilities of the paid keeper and the renter, who may free themselves from paying for an unavoidable accident by swearing but have to pay for avoidable accidents. The prime example of the paid keeper is the shepherd, for whom these rules are formulated.</i> One wolf is not an unavoidable accident, two wolves are. Rebbi Jehudah says, in times when wolves appear in packs, even a single wolf is an unavoidable accident.<br>Two dogs are not an unavoidable accident. Yaddua the Babylonian says in the name of Rebbi Meїr: From the same direction it is not an unavoidable accident; from two different directions it is an unavoidable accident<br>Robbers are an unavoidable accident. A lion, and a bear, and a leopard, and a panther<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πάρδαλις, Latin <i>pardalis</i>.</i>, and a snake are unavoidable accidents. When? If they came by themselves. But if he led [the flock] to a place of troops of wild animals or robbers, these are not unavoidable accidents.<br>If [an animal] dies a natural death it is an unavoidable accident. If he tortured it and it died, it is not an unavoidable accident. If it climbed to the top of a cliff and fell, it is an unavoidable accident. If he led it onto the top of a cliff and it fell, it is not an unavoidable accident. The unpaid keeper may stipulate that he be free from swearing, the borrower that he not be liable to pay, the paid keeper and the renter that they not be liable to swear or to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In money matters there is freedom in contracting. Even though the rules of responsibility of keepers are biblical (Notes 44,45), they are default rules to be used in the absence of contrary stipulations. It follows that Mishnah 14 deals only with stipulations other than money matters.</i>.<br>The stipulation of anybody who stipulates against what is written in the Torah is invalid. Any stipulation which follows a prior action is invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">An action cannot be invalidated retroactively. If a man preliminarily marries a woman and after that tries to impose conditions on the act, he remains unconditionally married.</i>. But any stipulation which it is possible to fulfill at the end and he stipulated it at the start is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">But any condition which was intended to be impossible to keep is void. If a man divorces his wife saying, this is your letter of divorce on condition that you cross the ocean on your feet, or that you fly in the air like a bird, she is unconditionally divorced (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 5:12" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).5.12">Tosephta <i>Giṭṭin</i> 5:12</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One wolf is not an unavoidable accident,” etc. “The stipulation of anybody who stipulates against what is written in the Torah,” etc. It was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 3:8" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).3.8">Tosephta <i>Qiddušin</i> 3:8</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:2:25" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.2.25"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:2, Notes 262–271</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 19b" href="/Kiddushin.19b">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 19b</a>).</i>“The stipulation of anybody who stipulates against what is written in the Torah, monetary stipulations are valid, nonmonetary stipulations are invalid.” How is this? If one said to a woman: be preliminarily married to me on condition that you have no claim on me for sustenance, clothing, and marital relations, she is preliminarily married [and his stipulation is valid. “On condition]<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Addition from E, missing in L. The addition is unquestionably correct even though it is pointed out in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:2:25" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.2.25"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:2 (Note 268</a>) that only the first two conditions are of monetary character. There, the problem is explained away by restricting the possibility of such a condition to preliminary marriage with a very young girl who is not available for sexual relations. But from the story of R. Tarphon who in times of famine preliminarily married 300 women to give them access to heave (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:12:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.12.5"><i>Yebamot</i>4:12 Note 197</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Ketubot (Lieberman) 5:1" href="/Tosefta_Ketubot_(Lieberman).5.1">Tosephta <i>Ketubot</i> 5:1</a>) and certainly did not offer them sustenance and clothing from his own means, it seems that if some of the conditions are of monetary character and the nonmonetary stipulation is not operative at the moment (since marital relations are obligatory and permitted only after definitive marriage), the stipulation is valid and if it is valid at the moment it is made it remains valid even after the nonmonetary stipulation becomes operative.</i> that it I die you shall not be obligated for levirate marriage, she is preliminarily married but his stipulation is invalid.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 5:12" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).5.12">Tosephta <i>Giṭṭin</i> 5:12</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 94a" href="/Bava_Metzia.94a">Babli 94a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 84a" href="/Gittin.84a"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 84a</a>; cf. <i>Tosefta kiFshutah Giṭṭin</i> pp. 886–887.</i>“This is the principle enunciated by Rebbi Jehudah ben Tema: Anything which is impossible to keep but he stipulated for her only to put her off<sup class="footnote-marker">55,</sup><i class="footnote">But any condition which was intended to be impossible to keep is void. If a man divorces his wife saying, this is your letter of divorce on condition that you cross the ocean on your feet, or that you fly in the air like a bird, she is unconditionally divorced (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 5:12" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).5.12">Tosephta <i>Giṭṭin</i> 5:12</a>).</i><sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The majority opinion in the Tosephta and the Babli holds that a bill of divorce given with an impossible stipulation is invalid.</i>. Whether we put it in writing or said it orally, everything valid orally is valid when written<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">In general, conditions written into a bill of divorce invalidate the entire bill. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.1.6"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 3:1, Notes 40–56</a>.</i>”. <sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Gittin (Lieberman) 5:7" href="/Tosefta_Gittin_(Lieberman).5.7">Tosephta <i>Giṭṭin</i> 5:7</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 76a" href="/Gittin.76a">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i>76a</a>. This refers to a man who before departing gave his wife a bill of divorce and stated that she should be divorced if he left Judea, or if he reached Galilee, etc. On the <i>via maris</i> Antipatris (near Petaḥ Tiqwah) was the border station in Judea and Kefar Othnay (Kafr Uthnay near Megiddo) the border station in Galilee; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.7.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 7:8</a>.</i>“And Kefar Othnay belongs to Galilee, Antipatris to Judea; the places in between are interpreted stringently<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Since the position of the stretch of the <i>via maris</i> passing through Samaria is indeterminate in <i>halakhah,</i> if the husband, e. g., left Judea but did not reach Galilee the wife is “divorced and not divorced.”</i>.”

Chapter 8



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody borrowed a cow and asked its owner [to work with him], or borrowed a cow and paid its owner [to work with him], or asked or paid its owner [to work with him] and then borrowed the cow: if it died he is not liable since it was said: “if its owner was with it he does not pay<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:14" href="/Exodus.22.14"><i>Ex.</i> 22:14</a>. The verse is read to mean that the borrower is not liable to pay for an animal which dies while working for him if its owner also was working for him all the time the animal was working. The owner need not be near the animal at the moment of its death.</i>.”<br>But if he borrowed the cow and afterwards asked or paid its owner [to work with him] and it died he is liable since it was said: “if its owner was not with it, certainly he shall pay.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:15" href="/Exodus.22.15"><i>Ex.</i> 22:15</a>.</i>”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody borrowed a cow,” etc. Rebbi Ila said, from the meaning of what is said, “certainly he shall pay”; would we not have known that if its owner was with it he does not pay? Why does the verse say, “if its owner was not with it, certainly he shall pay”? But since he borrowed the cow and did not ask its owner at the same time, even if the latter was ploughing with it when it died, he is liable.
“But if he borrowed the cow and afterwards asked or paid its owner [to work with him] and it died he is liable.” Rebbi La said, from the meaning of what is said, “if its owner was not with it, certainly he shall pay,” would I not have known that if its owner was not with it, certainly he shall pay? Why does the verse say, “if its owner was with it he does not pay”? But since he borrowed the cow and did ask its owner at the same time, even if the latter was ploughing at another place when it died, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A similar, tannaïtic, text is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 95b" href="/Bava_Metzia.95b">Babli, 95b</a>, and <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon bar Ioḥai</i> 22:14 (p. 206–207). In the language of <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael</i> (<i>Neziqin</i> 16, p. 306): The borrower has to pay if the cow was out of its owner’s control even for one moment.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Somebody borrowed a cow; he borrowed it for half a day and leased<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The lessor does not have to pay if the cow died; that risk was covered by the rental fee (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:14" href="/Exodus.22.14"><i>Ex.</i> 22:14</a>).</i> it for half a day, or borrowed it for one day and leased it for the next, or borrowed for one and leased for one. The lessee says, the borrowed one died, or it died on the day it was borrowed, or it died during the time it was borrowed, and the other one says “I do not know”, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since one party claims certainty and the other ignorance, judgment has to be given to the party claiming certainty.</i>. The lessor says, the leased one died, or it died on the day it was leased, or it died during the time it was leased, and the other one says “I do not know”, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since one party claims certainty and the other ignorance, judgment has to be given to the party claiming certainty.</i>. One says the borrowed and the other one says the leased; let the lessee swear that the leased one died. If both say “I do not know” they shall split<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The value of the cow is “money in doubt” which has to be split evenly (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.1.1.1">Chapter 1, Note 5</a>). The lessee/borrower has to pay half the value of the cow which died.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Somebody borrowed a cow,” etc. What is the status of the night in between<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">He borrowed for one day and leased for the next; the cow dies during the night.</i>? Some say, usually cows rest at their owners’, and this one, because it is leased to him, stays overnight and is as if loaned to him; he is liable. But some say, usually cows do not rest at their owners’, and this one is as if being leased to him; he is not liable.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody borrowed a cow and [the lender] sent it through his son, or his slave, or his agent, or through the borrower’s son, or slave, or agent, and it died, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">If everything is done on the lender’s initiative, the cow is not delivered until it reaches its destination; it still is under its owner’s control.</i>. If the borrower said, send it to me through my son, or slave, or agent, or through your son, or slave, or agent, or if the lender said, I am going to send it to you through my son, or slave, or agent, or through your son, or slave, or agent, and the borrower said: send! If it died, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">If the delivery is on the borrower’s initiative or explicit agreement, the cow is under the borrower’s control during delivery. If anything happened, he is liable.</i>. The same holds when he returns it<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The person on whose orders the delivery takes place carries the financial responsibility.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: ‘Lend me your cow for ten days and you shall be lent to me<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., work with me during five days.</i> the first five days.’ If it died, was it subject to the first days’ servitude? It died subject to the later days’ servitude<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The text of L contradicts the principle established in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 8:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.8.1.3">Note 3</a>. Therefore the text of E has to be accepted; the borrower has to pay for the dead cow.</i>. ‘Lend me your cow for ten days and you shall be lent to me the first of these.’ If it died during the later days, was it subject to the first days’ servitude? it died subject to the later days’ servitude<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This second paragraph is an almost verbatim copy of the preceding; it should be deleted.</i>. ‘Lend me your cow after ten days.’ The other one said, ‘take it now.’ If it died, was it subject to the first days’ servitude<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The rhetorical question should be read in all three paragraphs.</i>? It died subject to the later days’ servitude.
Lend me your cow and I shall respond to your request<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear what this means. In the other three cases the borrower asks the owner to be with the animal, tool, or vessel; the biblical rule of the borrower applies. Therefore, one has to assume that in the first case also the borrower asks for animal and owner and is ready to reciprocate with his animal and time.</i>; lend me your cow and come work with me; lend me your axe and come weed with me; lend me your plate and come eat with me. If he borrowed it from the superintendent of cisterns, the steward<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:5:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.5.9">Chapter 5, Note 138</a>. The steward is the organizer of the work on the estate; the administrator (οἰκονόμος) is the paymaster and the accountant. On large estates, both could be slaves, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 4:7:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.4.7.2"><i>Bava batra</i> 4:7</a>.</i>, or the administrator and it died, it is as if the owners were with it<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">On an estate of absentee owners, the employees authorized to dispose of certain items do represent the owners. If the employee worked with the borrower, it is as if the owner worked with him.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Somebody exchanged a cow for a donkey and it gave birth, or he sold his slave girl and she gave birth. If one said, [it happened] before I sold, the other said, after I bought, they shall split<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">If neither of them has proof.</i>. If he had two slaves, one adult and one young, or two fields, one large and one small. The buyer says, I bought the large one, and the seller says, I do not know: he acquired the large one<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Even if neither of them has proof, judgment has to be given to the one who claims certainty against one who asserts uncertainty.</i>. The seller says, I sold the small and the other party says, I do not know: he has only the small one<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Even if neither of them has proof, judgment has to be given to the one who claims certainty against one who asserts uncertainty.</i>. If one says the large one and the other says the small, let the seller swear that he sold the small one<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Both assert their claim with certainty and neither of them has proof. Since the buyer did not yet enter into possession, the seller may substantiate his claim by an oath.</i>. If both say, I do not know, they shall split<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Since neither of them asserts his claim as certain, the rule applies that “money in doubt has to be split evenly” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.1.1.1">Chapter 1, Note 5</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he said to him, lend me,” etc.<br>“Somebody exchanged a cow,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, this<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">If both parties submit their claim as certain as in the case of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 8:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.8.4.1">Note 22</a>.</i> contradicts Symmachos who says that in <i>all</i> cases of doubt one splits evenly<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">But in cases where both claims are tentative, the rule is universally accepted and we do not need Symmachos’s statement. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 100a" href="/Bava_Metzia.100a">Babli 100a</a>.</i>. Rebbi La said, explain it if both of them say, I am certain<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that Symmachos gave his rule only in cases where nobody submits a certain claim.</i>. Rebbi Yose explained, if both of them say, I am certain, that is what Rebbi Joḥanan refered to when he said, this contradicts Symmachos’s words.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Somebody sold his olive trees for their wood<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to cut down fruit bearing trees (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 4:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.4.8.1"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 4:9, Notes 109–113</a>). But one is permitted to cut down a fruit tree which does no longer yield a commercially useful crop, or if its wood is more valuable than its crop.</i>. If they produced less than a <i>quartarius</i> [of oil] per <i>se‘ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">If the yield in oil is &lt; 1/96 of the volume of olives harvested. This is such a minute quantity that nobody is supposed to quarrel about it.</i>, it belongs to the owner of the olive trees. A <i>quartarius</i> [of oil] per <i>se‘ah,</i> one says, my olive trees grew [the olives], the other says, my land grew them: they shall split evenly<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The buyer, instead of cutting down the tree for its wood, left it standing on the seller’s ground until the next harvest. Then the seller may claim part of the yield.</i>.<br>If a river flooded his olive trees and carried them to another’s field<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah presupposes not that the trees were torn away by the flood but that the flood eroded the earth around the trees which then were carried away with their roots still embedded in the original owner’s earth. Then the owner of the parcel on which they were deposited can dig a hole in which to set the tree with its original earth and hope for a normal harvest.<br>When the flood swept land away, it becomes ownerless since the owner is presumed to have abandoned hope of recovery (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 2:1:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.2.1.2-4">Halakhah 2:1</a>); it does not have to be returned (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).2.2">Tosephta 2:2</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 7:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.7.2.3"><i>Šeqalim</i> 7:3</a>).</i>; one says, my olive trees grew them, the other says, my land grew them: they shall split evenly.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Somebody sold his olive trees for their wood,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan asked, what is their status if they become moist<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">If the olives only contain 1% of recoverable oil, they probably are not edible. What is the status of the crop if somehow they become edible? The question is not answered.</i>?
<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 8:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.8.5.1">Mishnah 7</a>.</i> Rav Huna said, if it swept them away in their earth. Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, the years of <i>‘orlah</i> are their dispute<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 101a" href="/Bava_Metzia.101a">Babli, 101a</a>, the entire discussion is in the name of R. Simeon ben Laqish.<br>If the trees are swept away with their roots exposed, the farmer who replants them on his property must observe the first three years as <i>‘orlah</i>(Mishnah <i>‘Orlah</i> 1:3). There is no yield to quarrel about. Therefore, the Mishnah presupposes that the earth in which the tree was planted was eroded and the tree was swept away with it, as explained in the Mishnah. R. Yose b. Ḥanina notes that the original owner has a claim only for the three years in which the new owner would not have had any yield, were it not for the original owner’s earth in which the roots were embedded. After three years the entire harvest is the second owner’s.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who rents a dwelling to another person cannot remove him from there during the rainy season, from Tabernacles to Passover, but in the dry season on thirty days’ notice. But in walled cities,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 101a" href="/Bava_Metzia.101a">Babli, 101a</a>, the entire discussion is in the name of R. Simeon ben Laqish.<br>If the trees are swept away with their roots exposed, the farmer who replants them on his property must observe the first three years as <i>‘orlah</i>(Mishnah <i>‘Orlah</i> 1:3). There is no yield to quarrel about. Therefore, the Mishnah presupposes that the earth in which the tree was planted was eroded and the tree was swept away with it, as explained in the Mishnah. R. Yose b. Ḥanina notes that the original owner has a claim only for the three years in which the new owner would not have had any yield, were it not for the original owner’s earth in which the roots were embedded. After three years the entire harvest is the second owner’s.</i> whether in the dry or the rainy season, on twelve months’ notice. For a store<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The store owner frequently sells on credit and needs time to collect what is due him.</i>, whether in a walled city or in a village, twelve months. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, a baker’s or dyer’s store<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Not only do they sell on long term credit contracts, they also have installations which have to be dismantled and reconstructed.</i>, three years.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who leases a dwelling to another person,” etc. It was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 8:27" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).8.27">Tosephta 8:27</a>; the first sentence also in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 101b" href="/Bava_Metzia.101b">Babli 101b</a>.</i>“Anywhere they mention 30 days or twelve months it does not mean that one has to dwell there 30 days or twelve months but notice must be given before 30 days or twelve months<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta and Babli add: The tenant is held to the same deadlines if he wants to terminate the lease. But for the duration of a contract of lease there are no general rules.</i>. An oil press, the entire period it is in use. A wine press, the entire period it is in use. A potter’s atelier no less than twelve months. Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta: R. Nehemiah.</i> said, when has this been said? For those who work with black earth. But one who works with white earth collects his stock and leaves.”

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who leases a dwelling to another person: the lessee must provide the door, the key-bolt and the door handle and everything that requires expert installation. But anything that does not require experts, the lessor does it. Manure<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Which accumulates in the courtyard and is sold as fertilizer.</i> belongs to the lessee; the lessor only has what comes from the oven and the cooking stove<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Even if they are in the landlord’s courtyard; the tenant may sell the ashes as fertilizer.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who leases a dwelling to another person,” etc. Rebbi Isaac ben Ḥaqula instructed that a <i>mezuzah</i> requires expert installation<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">If a Jewish landlord leases a dwelling to a Jew, it must come with the required box of scriptural verses on the doorpost (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:9" href="/Deuteronomy.6.9"><i>Deut.</i> 6:9</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:20" href="/Deuteronomy.11.20">11:20</a>). The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 102a" href="/Bava_Metzia.102a">Babli disagrees, 102a</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who leases a dwelling to another person by the year, if the year was intercalated<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Before the publication of calendar computations in the Fourth Century, the uninitiated could not know which lunar year would be decreed to have a thirteenth month added to it.</i>, it was intercalated for the lessor. If he leased by the month, if the year was intercalated it was intercalated for the lessee. It happened in Sepphoris that a person leased a bathhouse from another for twelve gold denars per year, one gold denar per month<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The contract was written so that the lease was both per year and per month. The rent for the thirteenth month was “money in doubt.”</i>. The case came before Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel and Rebbi Yose who said, they should split the intercalated month.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who leases a dwelling to another person,” etc. Why? If he came to claim at the end of the month, can he collect everything<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since the tenant is already living in the house, the landlord is the claimant and on him is the burden of proof that the contract was written as a monthly lease.</i>? And if he came to him at the beginning of the month, he can tell him, get out<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The tenant claims that he has the right to live there rent free; the burden of proof is on him. The restrictions on evictions spelled out in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 8:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.8.6.1">Mishnah 8</a> only apply to tenants who are not in arrears with their payments.</i>. Samuel says, the Mishnah is so: If he came to claim in the middle of the month<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">This is a case of “money in doubt”; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 102b" href="/Bava_Metzia.102b">Babli 102b</a>.</i>. Rav said, a rule to collect<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees with the Mishnah and would have ruled for the landlord; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 102b" href="/Bava_Metzia.102b">Babli 102b</a>. דָגַר is short for דְאָגַר; E reads here: דִּינָא דְגָרְדֵיי דָנוּ “they gave a judgment of weavers”. The text of L seems preferable.</i>. Rebbi La said, this clearly does not follow the rule of Ben Nannas who said, the later language supersedes the earlier one<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.7.3.1">Mishnah <i>Bava batra</i> 7:4</a>. Since months are mentioned last, the lease is per month only and every month is payable. This is Rav’s reason in the Babli.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who leases a dwelling to another person and it collapsed has to provide him with a dwelling. If it was small he may not make it large, large he may not make it small. One room he shall not make into two, two he may not make into one. He may not reduce the number of windows nor add to them except with the agreement of both parties.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who leases a dwelling to another person,” etc. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, if he puts him into it<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The strict requirements of the Mishnah only apply if the lessor makes a sales pitch about the qualities of the particular dwelling.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he told him, a dwelling like this I shall lease to you<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Even if only the kind of dwelling is mentioned.</i>. But if a person leasing a house wants to sell it? Rebbi Immi said, nobody thinks that this one should die of hunger<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">For him, a sale breaks a lease.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira and Rebbi Hila both say, while certainly it is acquired by him, he has to tell him, let him dwell there until the time of his recall<sup class="footnote-marker">53,</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἀνάκλησις “recall, restoration”. <i>‘Or zarua‘</i> reads עַד מִישׁלַם זִימְנֵיהּ “until his time is up.”</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">While a sale during a lease is certainly valid, there is an obligation of the seller to include a paragraph in the sales document which protects the tenant for the remainder of his lease.</i>. There came a case before Rebbi Nisi<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">This probably should read: Assi, with <i>‘Or zarua‘, Rosh,</i> and the later text.</i> and he did not accept this. Does he disagree? They said, his daughter was given as a pledge to a Roman [and he did not have the wherewhithal to redeem her and Rebbi Assi instructed to sell;] therefore he instructed following Rebbi Immi<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In an emergency one follows R. Immi. The rule of RR. Ze‘ira and Ila is a recommendation, not the law. This is implied in R. Immi’s formulation.</i>.

Chapter 9



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who contracts with another for a field<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Either as a sharecropper or as a tenant for a fixed amount per year. The percentage given to the owner of the land has to be specified in each case; all other conditions follow the rules of the Mishnah unless they are contracted otherwise.</i>, at a place where one usually cuts, he has to cut<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">At harvest time, one cuts the grain with sickle or scythe.</i>, to uproot, he has to uproot<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">One tears out the entire plant with its roots.</i>, to plough after harvest, he has to plough, everything according to local usage. Just as they divide the grain, so they divide straw and chaff. Just as they divide the wine, so they divide the vine tendrils<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Cut during the pruning of the vines.</i> and the props<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">To which the vines are tied.
