Since they are bought in common, they are disposed of in common.</i>; they both provide the props together.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who contracts with another for a field” etc. If he uprooted at a place where one usually cuts, he<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This gives the reasons why the landlord can force the contractor to follow customary rules. The same arguments are in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 103b" href="/Bava_Metzia.103b">Babli, 103b</a>.</i> may tell him: “you make it sterile.” Where one uproots and he cuts, one tells him, go out and make it clean.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 9:14" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).9.14">Tosephta 9:14</a>.</i>: “One who contracts with another for a field has to harvest, bind into sheaves, thresh, winnow, and grade<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Separate the grain into the different commercially recognized grades. All this work done far away from the field is included in the lease of the field.</i>. The digger<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">He digs cisterns and is responsible for the cleaning and upkeep of the water system. This is a permanent job.</i>, the measurer<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">He measures the volumes of grain (cf. Arabic كيال) and other produce. The correct determination by <i>Arukh</i> (6 כל) was misunderstood by later commentators and lexicographers as “geometer”, whose services outside of Egypt were almost never needed. This led S. Lieberman to note (<i>Tosefta kiFshutah Bava meṣia‘</i> p. 285) that he does not know the definition of כייר and that the explanations of commentaries and dictionaries are unconvincing. The seal of the grain measurer was needed for bulk sale of produce.</i>, the steward<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 8:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.8.3.3">8:3, Note 15</a>.</i>, the town watchmen, and the administrator<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 8:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.8.3.3">8:3, Note 15</a>.</i> take their wages from the community<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">They are paid from town taxes. This presupposes that the entire town is the private property of a large landowner.</i>. The builder of furnaces<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">כּוּר is an industrial furnace. But since building furnaces is not continuous work and כּוֹר is a measure of volume (30 <i>se’ah</i>), perhaps the כייר is identical with the כייל (E. G.).</i>, the maker of seals, and the scribe<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Usually one reads הַבַּלָּן וְהַסַּפָּר “the bath attendant and the barber” but these providers of personal services cannot be understood here. A בּוּל “seal” in Hebrew and Syriac, Latin <i>bulla,</i> is needed for commercial and financial transactions.</i>, if hired by the tenant they take<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Their fees or wages.</i> from the tenant; if hired by the landlord they take from the landlord. <sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">E adds:<br>אָמַר ר׳ לָא. הַפּוֹעֲלִים בִּזְמַן שֶׁבָּאִין מִכֹּחַ הָאָרִיס נוֹטְלִין מִכֹּחַ הָאָרִיס. מִכֹּחַ בַּעַל הַבַּיִת נוֹטְלִין מִכֹּחַ בַּעַל הַבַּיִת.<br>R. La said: Workers if hired by the tenant receive from the tenant; if hired by the landlord they receive from the landlord.</i> One does not change local usage.”

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who contracts with another for a field<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The nature of the field was not specified in the lease.</i>, if it is an irrigated field or an orchard and the source dried up or the orchard was cut down, he<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The tenant. This Mishnah does not apply to sharecroppers.</i> may not deduct from his leasing fee. If he said, this irrigated field or this orchard shall be leased to me<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The nature of the field was specified in the lease.</i> and the source dried up or the orchard was cut down, he may deduct from his leasing fee.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who contracts with another for a field,” etc. Rebbi Isaac said, if the well dried up completely<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the second case of the Mishnah, where the nature of the area to be leased was specified in the contract. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 103b" href="/Bava_Metzia.103b">Babli (103b</a>) points out that the drying up of the water source must be a local phenomenon; for if it were due to a countrywide drought the tenant could reduce his rent payments according to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 9:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.9.5.1">Mishnah 9:6</a>.</i>. But if it was two storeys deep and now it is three storeys deep, one may tell him: exert yourself and it will be sufficient.
“The orchard was cut down.” Rebbi Isaac said, if the entire orchard was cut down. But if there remained ten trees standing per <i>bet se’ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Ten fruit trees per <i>bet se’ah</i> constitute a minimal orchard (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 1:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.1.2.2"><i>Ševi‘it</i>1:2, Note 18</a>), one tree per 250 square cubits. If the trees are more widely spaced, the area legally is no longer an orchard. The statement that “the entire orchard was cut down” has to be qualified to mean that “the trees were cut down to the extent that the orchard no longer exists.”</i> he may tell him: At the beginning they were sickly and were not bearing much. But now they are sparse and will be productive.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One contracts with another for a field; if after he received<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The share cropper.</i> it he lets it lie fallow<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Without the landlord’s agreement or against the local rules of crop rotation.</i> one estimates how much it could yield and gives to him, for so he writes to him: If I let it lie fallow or do not work it, I shall pay as if well-worked<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Since the sharecropper is presumed to be uneducated, any condition binding on him has to be formulated in Aramaic.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who contracts with another for a field,” etc. Rebbi Isaac said, this<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Not this Mishnah, but <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 4:22" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).4.22">Tosephta 4:22</a> quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 5:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.5.3.3">Halakhah 5:5, Note 56</a>. For the definition of “complaint”, see there, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 9:7:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.9.7.2">Note 58</a>.</i> implies that if one lets another’s money lie idle, the other only has a complaint against him. If one lets another’s field lie idly, he has to compensate him adequately<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This is the Mishnah here.</i>. If one lets another’s ship or store lie idly, what is the rule?

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The tenant farmer, not the share cropper.</i> who contracts with another for a field, did not want to weed<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Or flagrantly neglected any other common agricultural practice.</i>, and told him, what do you care since I am paying you rent? One does not listen to him<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The court.</i> since he<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The landlord.</i> may tell him, tomorrow you leave it and I’ll have it overgrown with grasses.<br>One who contracts with another for a field and it did not produce<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">And the share cropper does not work for a minimal yield.</i>. If it is enough to make a pile, he has to work on it. Rebbi Jehudah said, what is the definition of a pile? But if it is enough for spreading.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who contracts with another for a field,” etc.<br>“One who contracts with another for a field,” etc. How much is “enough to make a pile”? Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: that the winnowing shovel can stand upright in it<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">It is enough if the shovel can be held standing by the grain around it (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 105a" href="/Bava_Metzia.105a">Babli 105a</a>).</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: only that its handle be on top<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The shovel’s blade must be completely covered by grain, only the handle be visible on top (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 105a" href="/Bava_Metzia.105a">Babli 105a</a>).</i>. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said: in addition to his expenses<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">There must be enough grain so it may be sold to cover the share cropper’s expenses and the remainder form a pile in which a shovel can be kept standing. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 105a" href="/Bava_Metzia.105a">Babli (105a</a>), R. Simeon ben Laqish says, the expenses and two <i>se‘ah</i> of grain (about 25 liter).</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said: in addition to the expenses of both of them. Rebbi Abbahu said: “for spreading”, to recover the seed grain which was spread on it<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This is explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 5:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.5.1.7"><i>Peah</i> 5:1, Notes 28–30</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The tenant farmer, not the share cropper.</i> who contracts with another for a field, but it was eaten by locusts or scorched. If it was a countrywide calamity, he reduces his leasing fee; if it was not a countrywide calamity<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">But only individual fields were damaged.</i>, he does not reduce his leasing fee. Rebbi Jehudah says, if he contracted for a sum of money<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The lease has to be paid in coin, not in produce. Yerushalmi <i>Demay</i> 6:1 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 6:2" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).6.2">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 6:2</a>) notes that the tenant paying in kind is called חוֹכֵר “lessee”, the one paying in money is called שׂוֹכֵר “he who rents”.</i> in no case does he deduct from the leasing fee.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The tenant farmer, not the share cropper.</i> who contracts with another for a field,” etc. Rav Huna said, if the entire area in that direction was scorched<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 105b" href="/Bava_Metzia.105b">Babli, 105b</a>, all opinions agree that there is a countrywide calamity only if fields in all directions from the village were scorched.</i>. Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan. But only if it was sown. [But if he did not sow]<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Reading E, missing in L and <i>editio princeps,</i> but implied by the text.</i>, the other can tell him, if you had sown it would have produced a lot<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">There might have been so great a yield that even after the field was scorched, a reasonable harvest would have been left.</i>. Think of it, if there were other fields [which were not hit]<sup class="footnote-marker">41,</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E and R. Eliahu Fulda; missing in L. R. Eliahu usually is careful to note his emendations. The absence of a note indicates that he had some basis for his reading. The <i>editio princeps</i> moves the period: הַגַּע עַצְמָךְ שֶׁהָיוּ שָׂדוֹת אֲחֵרוֹת. וְלֹא יְכִיל מֵימַר לֵיהּ. “Think of it, if there were other fields. May he not tell him …?” The meaning is unchanged.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">If only most but not all fields were scorched. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 105b-106a" href="/Bava_Metzia.105b-106a">Babli, 105b/106a</a> from the start only speaks of “most of the <i>pagus</i> (the agricultural area)”.</i>? He may tell him, the Holy One, praise to Him, is forbearing with the evildoers. If there were other fields which were damaged<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">This is the opposite case, if not all but only most of the fields were damaged; a situation which the Babli still would classify as a countrywide calamity but the Yerushalmi considers as the farmer’s personal bad luck.</i>? He<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The landlord may refuse to reduce the rental fee, saying that he would be forced to agree only if all fields had been damaged.</i> may tell him, so far I was liable in the case; from now on I am no longer liable.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The tenant farmer, not the share cropper.</i> who contracts with another for a field, for ten <i>kor</i> of wheat per year. If it was damaged, he delivers from it<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">If the rental fee is payable in kind, one may assume that the yield of the field given in lease is meant. If the grain produced was of inferior quality, the landlord is obliged to accept this low quality grain as payment.</i>. If the wheat turned out to be of high quality, he may not tell him: I am buying wheat in the market, but he delivers from the yield<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The tenant cannot sell the high quality grain on the market, buy medium quality for his rental fee, and pocket the difference.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who contracts with another for a field,” etc. It was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Demai (Lieberman) 6:4" href="/Tosefta_Demai_(Lieberman).6.4">Tosephta <i>Demay</i> 6:4</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 6:1:5-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.6.1.5-7"><i>Demay</i>6:1 Notes 12–25</a>.</i>“One who contracts<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">As a lessee (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 9:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.9.5.1">Note 37</a>).</i> with another for a field tithes and delivers to him, the words of Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Since the produce is delivered to the landlord as payment, in his opinion it must be fully profane in that first heave (for the Cohen) and tithe (for the Levite) be taken and charged to the lessee’s account. This means that the cost to the lessee in the Land of Israel really is 11.8% higher than the amount stipulated in the lease.</i>. But the Sages say, both for the same kind and for the same field<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">In case the lessee pays with the produce of the field, he may tell the landlord to give tithes himself. This applies also if the contract calls for delivery of certain produce but the tenant farmer grows other kinds besides the specified produce.</i> he lifts heave<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">In earlier times, when Cohanim were able to be ritually pure and eat heave in purity, recommended 2% of the produce. In times when ashes of the red cow are no longer available, a nominal amount.</i> and delivers to him. But from another field or another kind he tithes and delivers to him<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">This is the procedure frowned upon in the Mishnah: The lessee sells his crop and pays his rent with cheaper produce bought on the market. Since then he incurs a 10% surcharge for the tithe, he will not pay with bought produce unless he is able to get an extraordinarily high price for his produce.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The Tosephta shows that this sentence should be attributed to R. Jehudah, not R. Meїr who always requires delivery of tithed produce. He permits delivery after lifting token heave only for agricultural property whose rent comprises all kinds of produce grown there.</i> Rebbi Meïr says, only for the same kind and for the same field he lifts heave and delivers to him. But from another field or another kind he does (not)<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">“Not” seems to be a scribal error.</i> tithe and deliver to him.”

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who contracts with another for a field to sow barley shall not sow wheat; for wheat he may sow barley. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel prohibits<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the tenant is always required to obey exactly the terms of his lease. The anonymous Tanna holds that barley may be substituted for wheat in crop rotation but not vice-versa.</i>. For legumes<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Beans and similar vegetables whose roots enrich the soil with nitrogen. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 107a" href="/Bava_Metzia.107a">Babli contends (107a</a>) that in Iraq grain is more beneficial to the soil than legumes.</i> he may not sow grain; for grain he may sow legumes. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel prohibits.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who contracts with another for a field,” etc. One understands that for barley he may not sow wheat<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Since wheat takes more out of the ground than does barley.</i>; for wheat, may he not sow barley? It follows Rebbi La<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">In the next Halakhah, following the reading of E.</i> or is the opinion of everybody at a place where the yield of barley is not twice that of wheat. But at a place where barley produces twice as much as wheat he may sow barley<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Even if this interferes with crop rotation.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who contracts with another for a field for a few years may not sow flax<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Which exhausts the soil which then takes a number of years to recover.</i> and does not have sycamore logs<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Sycamores are planted for their wood, not their fruit. If cut down, the stump (סַדַּן הַשִּׁקְמָה “anvil of the sycamore”) grows a new tree which will need at least seven years to grow to usable size. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.8.1"><i>Kilaim</i> 1:8 Note 150</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 4:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.4.4.6"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 4:5 Note 71</a>.</i>. If he contracted for seven years<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">For a minimum of 7 years.</i>, he may sow flax and does have sycamore logs.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who contracts with another for a field,” etc. One understands that for wheat he may not sow flax. For flax may he not sow wheat? Flax damages the soil for three years and you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">That one may not substitute wheat for flax.</i>? Rebbi Menaḥem, the brother of Rebbi Gorion, explained before Rebbi La: It is better to sow flax after flax but not barley after barley<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">E reads: “wheat after wheat”. It seems that this reading is required by the preceding Halakhah, Note 58. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 9:32" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).9.32">Tosephta, 9:32</a>, allows any sequence of crops on condition that no crop be planted for two consecutive years.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one who contracts with another for a field for a Sabbatical cycle at 700 denars, the Sabbatical year is included<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">He has the use of the field for 7 consecutive years, which gives him the possibility of 6 harvests.</i>. If he contracted for seven years at 700 denars, the Sabbatical is not included<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">He contracted for 7 agricultural years or 7 harvests. He has the use of the field for 7 non-consecutive years because in the Sabbatical year the field reverts to the disposition of its owner.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one contracts with another for a field,” etc. It was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 9:16" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).9.16">Tosephta 9:16</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 106b" href="/Bava_Metzia.106b">Babli 106b</a>.</i>“One contracts with another for a field; he sowed it a first year and it did not grow; one forces him to sow a second year. If the second time it did not grow, one cannot force him to sow a third year.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, that is, a field which had not been checked out<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear what a “checked-out field” would be. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 106b" href="/Bava_Metzia.106b">Babli, 106b</a>, R. Simeon ben Laqish holds that even the second time the contractor cannot be forced to sow if the first time the plants sprouted but the crop was then destroyed by a natural disaster.</i>. But if it had been checked out, one can force him to sow a third year.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person hired for the day collects the entire night<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Since he works until sundown, he cannot be paid during the day. If he was not paid by daybreak the next morning, his employer has sinned against the rule that “one may not withhold a hireling’s wages until the morning” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:13" href="/Leviticus.19.13"><i>Lev.</i> 19:13</a>).</i>; a person hired for the night collects the entire day<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">If he is not paid promptly, the employer sins against “you must pay his wages on his day” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:15" href="/Deuteronomy.24.15"><i>Deut.</i> 24:15</a>).</i>. A person hired for hours collects the entire day and the entire night<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">This is explained in the Halakhah.</i>.<br>A person hired for a week, a month, a year, or a Sabbatical period, if he finishes during daytime, he collects the entire day; if he finishes at nighttime, he collects the entire night and the following day.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A person hired for the day collects the entire night,” etc. Samuel said, the next morning he is in violation on his behalf because of “ “you must pay his wages on his day”<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">If he is not paid promptly, the employer sins against “you must pay his wages on his day” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:15" href="/Deuteronomy.24.15"><i>Deut.</i> 24:15</a>).</i>. Rebbi Dosa said, is there one day when you can say that he transgresses on all these counts<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">He refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 10:3" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).10.3">Tosephta 10:3</a> or <i>Sifry Deut</i> 278: One who retains a hireling’s wages transgresses five prohibitions: “Do not oppress” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:13" href="/Leviticus.19.13"><i>Lev.</i>19:13</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:14" href="/Deuteronomy.24.14"><i>Deut.</i> 24:14</a>), “do not rob” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:13" href="/Leviticus.19.13"><i>Lev.</i>19:13</a>), “do not withhold overnight” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:13" href="/Leviticus.19.13"><i>Lev.</i> 19:13</a>), “the sun shall not set for him” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:15" href="/Deuteronomy.24.15"><i>Deut.</i> 24:15</a>). Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:13" href="/Leviticus.19.13"><i>Lev.</i> 19:13</a> refers to paying in the night and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:14" href="/Deuteronomy.24.14"><i>Deut.</i>24:14</a> to paying by day, how could one person transgress all five commandments simultaneously? The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 111a" href="/Bava_Metzia.111a">Babli (111a</a>) even has a list of 6 verses; they also combine <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:13" href="/Leviticus.19.13"><i>Lev.</i> 19:13</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:15" href="/Deuteronomy.24.15"><i>Deut.</i>24:15</a>.</i>? If it is to refer to the verses, explain it about a person hired by the hour during day and night who can collect the entire day and the entire night. And it was stated thus: <sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 10:2" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).10.2">Tosephta 10:2</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 111a" href="/Bava_Metzia.111a">Babli 111a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim, Section 2 12" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim,_Section_2.12"><i>Sifra Qedošim Parašah</i> 2(12</a>).</i>“A person hired by the hour during the day collects the entire day; in the night he collects the entire night. A person hired by the hour during day and night collects the entire night and the entire day.”

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Wages of a human, as well as wages for an animal, as well as wages for implements are subsumed under “you must pay his wages on his day”<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">If he is not paid promptly, the employer sins against “you must pay his wages on his day” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:15" href="/Deuteronomy.24.15"><i>Deut.</i> 24:15</a>).</i> and “one may not withhold a hireling’s wages until the morning”<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Since he works until sundown, he cannot be paid during the day. If he was not paid by daybreak the next morning, his employer has sinned against the rule that “one may not withhold a hireling’s wages until the morning” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:13" href="/Leviticus.19.13"><i>Lev.</i> 19:13</a>).</i>. When? If he claimed it. If he did not claim it, one does not transgress. If one gave him a draft on a storekeeper or a banker, he does not transgress. In his time<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The time when he has to be paid according to Mishnah 11.</i> the hireling swears and takes<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The hireling claims that he was not paid; the employer claims that he did pay; neither of them has witnesses. Then the hireling is privileged; the court will allow him to swear that he was not paid and then order his employer to pay.</i>; after his time he does not swear and take<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The ordinary rules of procedure apply: in the absence of proof the defendant may swear to absolve himself.</i>. But if there are witnesses that he did claim, he swears and takes<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Even after the 12 hour period when he should have been paid. The unpaid hireling has to protect his claim by asking for his money in front of witnesses.</i>.<br>The resident proselyte<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">He is a full Jew in contrast to the גֵּר תּוֹשָׁב “resident proselyte” who only keeps the Noaḥide commandments.</i> is protected by “you must pay his wages on his day” but not by “one may not withhold a hireling’s wages until the morning”<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:14" href="/Deuteronomy.24.14"><i>Deut.</i> 24:14</a> speaks of “your proselyte … in your gates.” The same expression is used in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:20" href="/Deuteronomy.14.20"><i>Deut.</i> 14:20</a> to permit the sale of carcass meat to the resident proselyte. By the doctrine of invariable lexemes in the Torah, the expression in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:14" href="/Deuteronomy.24.14"><i>Deut.</i> 24:14</a> refers to the resident proselyte. But <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:13" href="/Leviticus.19.13"><i>Lev.</i> 19:13</a> speaks of “your neighbor”; only the just proselyte is covered by that expression. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 9:11:5-12:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.9.11.5-12.4">Halakhah 14</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 111b" href="/Bava_Metzia.111b">Babli 111b</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Wages of a human,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifry Deut.</i> 279.</i> It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:14" href="/Deuteronomy.24.14"><i>Deut.</i> 24:14</a>. The verse reads: Do not oppress the poor and needy <i>hireling</i> …</i>: “Do not oppress the poor and needy of your brothers,” this refers to Israel. “Your proselytes,” this refers to the just proselytes<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">He is a full Jew in contrast to the גֵּר תּוֹשָׁב “resident proselyte” who only keeps the Noaḥide commandments.</i>. “In your land,” to add animals and slaves. “In your gates,” to add movables<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">A different argument for the same implication, based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:13" href="/Leviticus.19.13"><i>Lev.</i> 19:13</a>, is in <i>Sifra Qedoŝim Paraŝah</i> 2(9).</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 10:5" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).10.5">Tosephta 10:5</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim, Section 2 11" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim,_Section_2.11"><i>Sifra Qedošim Parašah</i> 2(11</a>).</i>: I could think that if one gave him a draft on a storekeeper or a banker, he would transgress about him. He does not transgress about him but they transgress about him. When? If he claimed it. If he did not claim it, one does not transgress.
“In his time the hireling swears and takes.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, if he said: I paid<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the case that the hireling did not claim his wages immediately, but comes later to claim his wages. The employer may swear that he paid only if he asserts that he paid immediately. But if he admits that he transgressed the commandment to pay immediately, the hireling retains his right to swear in order to get a court order forcing the employer to pay.</i>. But if he said, I shall pay tomorrow: if then he said, I paid, he cannot be believed. Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, even the next day, if he said, I paid, he is believed<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">In any case where the hireling did not claim his wages immediately before witnesses and the claim is presented after the time due, the employer retains the right to swear that he paid.</i>.
“The resident proselyte is protected by ‘you must pay his wages on his day’ ”, etc. It is written: “Do not oppress your neighbor,” excluding the resident proselyte<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:14" href="/Deuteronomy.24.14"><i>Deut.</i> 24:14</a> speaks of “your proselyte … in your gates.” The same expression is used in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:20" href="/Deuteronomy.14.20"><i>Deut.</i> 14:20</a> to permit the sale of carcass meat to the resident proselyte. By the doctrine of invariable lexemes in the Torah, the expression in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:14" href="/Deuteronomy.24.14"><i>Deut.</i> 24:14</a> refers to the resident proselyte. But <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:13" href="/Leviticus.19.13"><i>Lev.</i> 19:13</a> speaks of “your neighbor”; only the just proselyte is covered by that expression. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 9:11:5-12:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.9.11.5-12.4">Halakhah 14</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 111b" href="/Bava_Metzia.111b">Babli 111b</a>).</i>.

Halakhah 12

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person who lends to another may take a pledge from him only in court<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Under the supervision of the court.</i>. He may not enter his house to take his pledge, as it is said: “You have to stand outside.<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:11" href="/Deuteronomy.24.11"><i>Deut.</i> 24:11</a>.</i>” If he had two vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">This expression, כֵּלִים, covers both implements and textiles.</i>, he takes one and returns one. He returns the pillow for the night<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:13" href="/Deuteronomy.24.13"><i>Deut.</i> 24:13</a>.</i> and the plough for the day. If he<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The debtor.</i> dies, he<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The creditor.</i> does not return it to his<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The debtor.</i> heirs. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, even to himself<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The debtor.</i> he<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The creditor.</i> returns it only up to 30 days; after thirty days<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The religious obligation to return the pledge is formulated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:13" href="/Deuteronomy.24.13"><i>Deut.</i>24:13</a> as an obligation to return it to the debtor in person. If the debtor died, the pledge may be sold and the proceeds deducted from the amount of the debt.</i> he<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The creditor.</i> sells it in court<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Under the supervision of the court.</i>
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It is written about torts that the collection be from the best quality, as it is said: “his best field, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:4" href="/Exodus.22.4"><i>Ex.</i> 22:4</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 48b" href="/Gittin.48b">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 48b</a>.</i>” And it is written about a loan that the collection be from average quality, as it is said: “and the man to whom you are creditor, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">87,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:11" href="/Deuteronomy.24.11"><i>Deut.</i> 24:11</a>.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The inference is explained later by R. Simai.</i>” They inferred real estate from pledges<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:14" href="/Deuteronomy.24.14"><i>Deut.</i> 24:14</a> refers to a pledge of movables, it is not obvious that the same rule should be applied to the foreclosure of a mortgage.</i>. Similarly, should not pledges be inferred from real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">If payment for torts is made by cash or movables, not in real estate, that only best quality would be acceptable. But any debt can be liquidated by money or money’s worth to avoid foreclosure.</i>? <sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel to the remainder of the paragraph is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.1.8"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 5:1, Notes 30–32</a>.</i> Rebbi Simai explained: It is a word of the Torah that the court’s bailiff enter and collect from average quality. For if the creditor enter, he would bring out the best. And if the debtor enter, he would bring out the worst. But the court’s bailiff enters and collects from average quality. Rebbi Ismael stated: It is a word of the Torah that the debtor enter, as it is said: “and the man to whom you are creditor etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:10-11" href="/Deuteronomy.24.10-11"><i>Deut.</i> 24:10–11</a>: “If you are a creditor to your neighbor for anything, do not enter his house to take his pledge. Stand outside, and the man to whom you are creditor shall bring the pledge outside to you.” This clearly indicates that it is up to the debtor to determine what to give as pledge; the requirement that it be of medium quality is purely rabbinical for R. Ismael; in the words of the Babli “not to lock the door before borrowers” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 113b" href="/Bava_Metzia.113b">Babli 113b</a>). The obligation to stand outside extends to the bailiff (<i>Sifry Deut.</i> 276).<br>But R. Simai, and the Masoretes who follow him in their punctuation, read: “Stand outside, and the man, acting on behalf of the one to whom you are creditor, shall bring …” <i>The man</i> is the court’s employee.</i>” Rebbi La said, it was stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia.</i>: “to seize as pledge”, by the court. Outside the court, from where<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The court has to give authorization; it does not have to oversee the execution.</i>? The verse<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:25" href="/Exodus.22.25"><i>Ex.</i> 22:25</a>.</i> says, “if to seize as pledge you seize as pledge.” If he took the pledge without authorization, he transgresses all these verses<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 10:8" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).10.8">Tosephta 10:8</a>: <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:25" href="/Exodus.22.25"><i>Ex.</i> 22:25</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:10-13" href="/Deuteronomy.24.10-13"><i>Deut.</i>24:10–13</a>, for a total of five sins committed by one action.</i>. Rebbi La said, he caused himself to transgress all these verses<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">If he received the pledge through the court’s bailiff and did not return it, he still would transgress <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:25" href="/Exodus.22.25"><i>Ex.</i>22:25</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:13" href="/Deuteronomy.24.13"><i>Deut.</i> 24:13</a>, but not the other commandments.</i>.
If he took a pledge and then returned it, by law he can collect from the property mortgaged to him<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Usually, נְכָסִין מְשׁוּעֲבָדִין denotes otherwise mortgaged real estate. But in this case it denotes the pledge, which in case of non-payment the creditor can satisfy himself from. Since by biblical law the pledge has to be returned periodically, why should the Jewish creditor of a Jew bother to take a pledge?</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 10:9" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).10.9">Tosephta 10:9</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 114b-115a" href="/Bava_Metzia.114b-115a">Babli, 114b–115a</a>, in the name of R. Meïr.</i>“But if he returns it, why does he take a pledge? Rebbi explained, for maybe there will be a Sabbatical year and annul [the debt]<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Secured loans are not remitted in the Sabbatical year; Mishnah <i>Ševi‘it</i>10:2.</i>. Or maybe that one<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">The borrower. If the borrower’s estate contains no real estate, the creditor might be unable to force repayment of the debt.</i> will die and leave the movables in the heir’s hand.”
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Mišpaṭim</i> 19 (p. 316–317); <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon b. Ioḥai</i> p. 212; <i>Tanḥuma Mišpaṭim</i> 10; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shemot Rabbah 30:7" href="/Shemot_Rabbah.30.7"><i>Ex. rabba</i> 30:7</a>; <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 277.</i>: He takes a day garment as pledge during the night and a night garment during the day. He returns a day garment during the day and a night garment during the night. Pillow and bed sheet<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Corrected version of the scribe. First he wrote סָגוֹס σάγος, "coarse coat" (expression used in <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon b. Ioḥai, Sifry Deut.</i>).</i> which are used as cover during the night he may take as pledge during the day and return for the night. An axe and a plough which usually are used for work during the day he may take as pledge during the night and return for the day. Rebbi La said: Sometimes one explains the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:15" href="/Deuteronomy.24.15"><i>Deut.</i> 24:15</a> (speaking of worker’s wages, not pledges).</i> “the sun shall not come onto it,” as “the sun shall not rise upon it”, but sometimes one explains as “the sun shall not set upon it” since it is written “returning you shall return the pledge to him when the sun comes,<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:13" href="/Deuteronomy.24.13"><i>Deut.</i> 24:13</a>.</i>” at sunrise, “until the going of the sun return it to him,<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:25" href="/Exodus.22.25"><i>Ex.</i> 22:25</a>.</i>” until sundown.

Halakhah 13

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One may not take a pledge from a widow, whether poor or rich, since it was said<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:17" href="/Deuteronomy.24.17"><i>Deut.</i> 24:17</a>.</i>: “And do not take a widow’s garment for a pledge.” One who takes a flour mill as pledge transgresses a prohibition and is guilty for two vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the moving millstone is useless without its stone base and vice-versa.</i>, as it was said: “Do not take upper and lower millstones as pledge<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:6" href="/Deuteronomy.24.6"><i>Deut</i>. 24:6</a>.</i>.” They said, not only upper and lower millstones but everything used to make food, as it was said: “For he takes a living person for a pledge<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:6" href="/Deuteronomy.24.6"><i>Deut</i>. 24:6</a>.</i>.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One may not take a pledge from a widow, whether poor or rich,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian tradition, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 115a" href="/Bava_Metzia.115a">Babli 115a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 10:10" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).10.10">Tosephta 10:10</a>, R. Meїr’s opinion is attributed to R. Jehudah and R. Jehudah’s to R. Simeon. In <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 281, the first statement is anonymous as in the Mishnah; R. Simeon is quoted to the effect that no woman’s pledge can be returned since any woman’s reputation would be ruined by frequent visits by a man not married to her. This argument is incorporated into Pseudo-Jonathan’s Targum of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:17" href="/Deuteronomy.24.17"><i>Deut.</i> 24:17</a>.</i>: “One may not take a pledge from a widow, whether poor or rich, since it was said<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:17" href="/Deuteronomy.24.17"><i>Deut.</i> 24:17</a>.</i>: ‘And do not take a widow’s garment for a pledge’, whether poor or rich, the words of Rebbi Meїr. Rebbi Jehudah says, from a poor one he cannot take any pledge at all, from a rich one he may take a pledge but does not return it since if he came to her he would ruin her reputation.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">For the Babylonian version, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 116a" href="/Bava_Metzia.116a">Babli 116a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 10:11" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).10.11">Tosephta 10:11</a>.</i> One who takes a pair of barber’s scissors<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Since the scissors are indispensable to the barber’s livelihood, for him they are as important as is food and cannot be impounded. Even though the scissors only work as a pair (ζεῦγος), each blade separately is counted as a tool. The rules of items which cannot be taken are spelled out in Mishnah <i>‘Arakhin</i> 6:3.</i> transgresses for the two blades separately. If he took only one component, he transgresses for this one [only]<sup class="footnote-marker">118,</sup><i class="footnote">From E, missing in L, but implied by the last sentence of the paragraph.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the part remaining in the barber’s hand is almost useless, the creditor may be punished only for one misdemeanor.</i>. One who takes a yoke of cows<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">From a farmer whose plough is drawn by a yoke of cows.</i> transgresses for each one separately. If he took only one, he transgresses for this one only.

Chapter 10



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the ground floor<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">בַּיִת in rabbinic Hebrew means either a one-storey (usually, one room) house or the ground floor of a multi-storey house. A building with more than two floors is called פָּלָטִין “a palace”.</i> and the upper floor belonging to two different persons collapse, they divide wood, stones, and dust between themselves<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 11:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).11.1">Tosephta 11:1</a> prescribes that in case the two apartments were of unequal size, the building material is divided proportional to the size of the apartments.</i>. One takes into consideration which stones were at risk to break<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This is an instruction to the court if the case needs judicial intervention.</i>. If one of them recognized his stones, he takes them, and they are debited to his account.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If the ground floor<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">בַּיִת in rabbinic Hebrew means either a one-storey (usually, one room) house or the ground floor of a multi-storey house. A building with more than two floors is called פָּלָטִין “a palace”.</i> and the upper floor belonging to two different persons collapse,” etc. It was stated: If they were blackened like an oven, the upper ones were apt to break. If they fell to the outside, the lower ones were apt to break<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">There are no parallels to the <i>baraita,</i> but it seems that one has to switch the statements: If the broken stones were black like the inside of an oven, they probably were foundation stones. If stones fell away from the house and are broken, they were upper floor stones (<i>Responsa Radbaz</i> vol. 5, #2112; cf. <i>Tosephta kiFshutah Bava meṣia‘</i> p. 304).</i>.
“If one of them recognized his stones, he takes them, and they are debited to his account.” Rebbi Hoshaia said, this means that the hand rules on one side<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear what the sentence means. It seems that R. Hoshaia’s problem is that both rules given in the Mishnah, i. e., that all broken stones probably from a certain place are debited to the owner of that place, and that the person who recognizes his stones takes them for his account, disregarding a certain margin of error. If property rights (“hand”) are given to one side, it rules over the entire lot in dispute. This seems to contradict common sense.</i>. There<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Demay</i> 7:9, Notes 128–129.</i>, we have stated: “A hundred <i>ṭevel</i> in a hundred profane, he removes 101. A hundred <i>ṭevel</i> in a hundred tithe, he removes 101.”<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">As explained there, the <i>ṭevel</i> in question is tithe of which heave of the tithe was not taken and which therefore is forbidden for consumption to anybody not a Cohen. The question is why 101 has to be taken as <i>ṭevel</i> rather than 100. The question is answered by R. Yose.</i> Rebbi Yose said, if you say that he takes 100, I would say that only profane came to his hand and the other remains <i>ṭevel</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">While this is extremely unlikely, it is possible and the legal rule is based on this possibility. The same situation applies here. While it is unlikely that all broken stones come from one party’s floor, it is possible and that is enough as a legal principle.</i> So he takes 101 in order to separate heave and heave of the tithe from the <i>ṭevel.</i>
Those bearing columns<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">If a condominium is rebuilt, who has to pay for those elements of the wall whose only function is to carry the weight of the upper storey and the roof?</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira: He<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the rights to the upper floor.</i> tells the bottom dweller, you are obliged to support me. Rebbi La: He<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the rights to the lower floor.</i> tells the dweller in the upper floor, the builders say that both have to build the foundations. The following supports the builders: <sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 11:4" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).11.4">Tosephta 11:4</a>.</i>“If his ruined building was close to the wall of his neighbor’s courtyard, he should not tell him: I am contributing to the foundations of your wall where it comes up, but he has to contribute to the foundations from the bottom and up<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">His plot is situated higher than his neighbor’s courtyard. Then he has to pay not only for the part of the wall which he will use but also for the foundations on which his part of the wall will be built. Therefore, the owner of the second floor also has to pay for the additional cost incurred by the owner of the ground floor for foundations and walls built for a heavier load.</i>. If it was built on top of ditches or caves, he is not obligated for anything.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since the ground floor could not be built without covering what is beneath, the costs cannot be claimed from the owner of the second floor.</i>” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, explain it, for example those inhabitants of Bet Shean where the bottom dweller cannot build unless the builder of the upper floor builds<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">If the house is built on a steep hillside, the excavation must be done simultaneously for both floors; otherwise debris from above would fall into the building site of the lower floor. Therefore, each one of the parties has to assume part of the cost; satisfying both Rabbis Ze‘ira and La.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The ground floor and the upper floor belong to two different persons<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Following the Babli, Maimonides and Rashi explain that the house is the property of the person dwelling in the lower apartment; the dweller in the first floor rented his apartment. The landlord is required to keep the rented apartment in good repair. According to Tosaphot, the Mishnah either refers to a rental situation or to two brothers who inherited the house together. Then the expenses for necessary repairs have to be borne equally by both of them. If one of them refuses to pay his part, the other brother may use his apartment as a temporary dwelling.</i>; if the upper floor sprang a leak and the owner of the ground floor does not want to fix it, the owner of the upper floor descends and dwells below until he fixes the upper floor for him. Rebbi Yose says, the lower one gives the roofing<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Wooden logs or planks to make a roof.</i> and the upper one the roof covering<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Mortar and cement to make a smooth, watertight surface.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The ground floor and the upper floor belong to two different persons,” etc. It was stated: “If it sprang a leak above the oven, he descends. If it sprang a leak above the fireplace, he descends<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Not to dwell there but to bake and cook in the courtyard in front of the bottom dweller’s door. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 116b" href="/Bava_Metzia.116b">Babli, 116b</a>, this is Rav’s opinion. Samuel holds that if the top dweller has to descend for any household chores, he comes to live in the ground floor apartment.</i>.” But he may tell him, make yourself a ladder so you do not have to ascend and descend through my dwelling<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In any case the second floor dweller is supposed to have his own stairs outside the first floor dweller’s apartment.</i>.
“Rebbi Yose says, the lower one contributes the roofing and the upper one the roof covering,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 117a" href="/Bava_Metzia.117a">Babli 117a</a>. In most mss. of the Babli the name is Justinus.</i> Rebbi Justus in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish:: The bottom dweller contributes the logs and the planks<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The logs to make a roof and the planks to create a platform on top of the roof.</i>; the dweller on the upper floor contributes the roof covering<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Mortar and cement to make a smooth, watertight surface.</i>. Which kind of roof covering? Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: heavy roof covering. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: medium roof covering, since they only spoke about rinsing cups. But if he wanted to wash clothing, also Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Eleazar will agree that the roof covering has to be heavy<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">If the flat roof is used for any activity, the roof covering must withstand prolonged use without becoming leaky.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina instructed those of Sepphoris that both of them should contribute the logs and the plates since they expect to bring up there their wine and their produce<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">If both parties intend to use the roof, both have to equally share the expenses.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the ground floor and the upper floor belonging to two different persons collapse, and the owner of the upper floor suggests to the owner of the ground floor to rebuild but himself refuses to build, then the owner of the upper floor rebuilds the ground floor and dwells in it until he<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the rights to the lower floor.</i> pays his<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the rights to the upper floor.</i> expenses. Rebbi Jehudah says, but he who dwells in another person’s [place] would have to pay him rent<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">His problem is that if the owner of the ground floor pays his share, the owner of the upper floor will have dwelt in the apartment rent free, which amounts to receiving hidden interest.</i>; so the owner of the (ground)<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Clearly one has to read הָעֲלִייָה “the upper floor” with the Babli and all Mishnah mss.</i> floor builds ground and upper floors and makes the roof over the upper floor<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">To make the upper floor livable, even if he does not dwell there.</i> but dwells on the ground floor until he<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the rights to the lower floor.</i> pays his<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the rights to the upper floor.</i> expenses.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If the ground floor and the upper floor,” etc. But if the owner of the ground floor wants to build and the owner of the upper floor does not, may he tell him: seal it?<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Can the ground floor dweller ask the owner of the upper floor to provide a roof? If both floors were rebuilt, the roof would be a charge on the second floor’s owner.</i> Let us hear from the following: <sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">A related <i>baraita</i> is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 11:4" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).11.4">Tosephta 11:4</a>.</i>“If his collapsed building was next to another’s collapsed building<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">And the two buildings shared a wall.</i>, and he rebuilt without permission, he computes his actual expenses and he has to pay him all his expenses<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">If the second owner later decides to rebuild his house, he cannot use the other building’s wall without paying the first builder’s cost in full. But at the moment of building, he is not required to contribute.</i>.” This implies that he cannot tell him, seal it on the side. Just as he cannot tell him, seal it on the side, so he cannot tell him, seal it on top<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the ground floor has no regress on the owner of the upper floor at the moment when he is building; he has to finish his own roof. He will be able to recoup part of his expenses only when the owner of the upper floor decides to rebuild.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> And so an olive press built into a rock with a vegetable garden on its top: If it caved in, the garden’s owner may sow on the bottom until he build cupolas on his olive press<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Olive presses (as well as wine presses) usually were built with cupolas. If the natural roof of the press hewn into the rock collapsed, the owner of the vegetable plot may use the bottom for his vegetables until the owner of the olive press rebuilds it. The owner of the vegetable garden may fill the top with soil to create a level surface.</i>. If a wall or a tree fell into the public domain and caused damage, he is not liable to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was an unforseeable accident.</i>. If he was given an order<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">By the court, on application of the building police.</i> to tear down the wall or cut down the tree; within the term he is not liable, after the term he is liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “And so an olive press,” until “if a wall or a tree,” etc. Rebbi Eleazar said: It happened that a vine was growing on another person’s peach tree<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>prunus persica</i> “Persian plum tree”</i>, and the peach tree withered<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Killed by the vine which overgrew it.</i>. The case came before the great Rebbi Ḥiyya who told him, go and provide him with a peach tree. Rebbi Joḥanan told him: Is this not a Mishnah? “And so an olive press built into a rock and another’s vegetable garden is on its top”? Do we not understand that one does not force<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah does not require the owner of the oil press to rebuild but gives the gardener the right to use the area on top of the oil press for his purposes. The court is not empowered to force the owner of the oil press to reconstitute the vegetable garden.</i>? But Rebbi Ḥiyya stated that one forces<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">To provide a replacement tree.</i>! We may say that they did not disagree. He who says that one forces, if he is there. But he who says that one does not force, if he is absent<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah speaks of an absentee landlord. Until the absentee be notified it may take a long time; in the meantime the gardener is given the use of the area occupied by the oil press.</i>.
“If he was given an order to cut down.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">This quote shows that the Mishnah underlying the Halakhah is not the version presented in the Mishnah (and Maimonides’s autograph) but the text given in the Babli and many Mishnah mss. which mention cutting down the tree before tearing down the wall.</i>” What is the time limit? Rebbi Hoshaia said, 30 days<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 118a" href="/Bava_Metzia.118a">Babli, 118a</a>, this is attributed to R. Joḥanan (R. Hoshaia’s student.) In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 11:7" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).11.7">Tosephta, 11:7</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 2:5:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.2.5.2-3"><i>Bava qamma</i> 2:5</a>, the wording is: at least 30 days. The enforcement of a strict limit of 30 days for obeying court orders is Amoraic.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Somebody’s wall bordering on another’s vegetable garden collapsed. That one told him, remove your stones; if he said, they are yours<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">If the neighbor pays for the removal of the building material, it is his.</i>, one<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The court will not accept the proposition; the opposing party can force a judgment for their claim.</i> does not listen to him. If after the other had accepted, he told him: here are your expenses and I shall take what is mine, one does not listen to him.<br>Somebody hired a worker to work on straw and chaff. When he said to him, pay me my wages, he answered, take what you worked on as your wages, one does not listen to him. If after the other<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The worker.</i> had accepted, he told him: here are your wages and I shall take what is mine, one does not listen to him<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The court will not accept the proposition; the opposing party can force a judgment for their claim.</i>.<br>If somebody brought his manure into the public domain, he who brings, brings, and he who uses it to manure, manures<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Temporary use of the public domain for agricultural purposes is permitted but care must be taken that the time in which the public domain is used to deposit potentially dangerous material is reduced to the absolute minimum.</i>. One does not soak loam in the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Which takes a long time.</i> nor does one form bricks; one kneads loam in the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">If a wall is built bordering on the public domain it is permissible to knead mortar for immediate use on the wall.</i> but not bricks. If somebody builds in the public domain and somebody brings stones; he who brings, brings, and he who builds, builds<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Again, the time in which the public domain is used to deposit potentially dangerous material must be reduced to the absolute minimum.</i>; but if he caused damage, he pays for what he damaged. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, also he has to prepare his work 30 days in advance.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">No building permit should be given without a plan to hold the use of public domain for private purposes to a minimum.</i>
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Somebody who brought his manure,” etc. It was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 11:8" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).11.8">Tosephta 11:8</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 118b" href="/Bava_Metzia.118b">Babli 118b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 81b" href="/Bava_Kamma.81b"><i>Bava qamma</i> 81b</a>..</i>”A person may unload his manure at his courtyard door in the public domain in order immediately to remove it. But it is forbidden to leave it there. If another person was injured by it, he is liable. Rebbi Jehudah says, at the time of carrying out manure<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">A month before ordinarily the fields have to be manured.</i> a person may unload his manure at his courtyard door in the public domain for it to be broken down by the feet of people and animals for 30 days, for on this condition did Joshua distribute the Land to Israel.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">For other authorized private uses of public or other people’s properties, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 5:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.5.1.3"><i>Bava batra</i> 5:1 Notes 16</a> ff.</i>” <sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 11:6" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).11.6">Tosephta 11:6</a>.</i>“A person may unload his dust at his courtyard door in the public domain in order immediately to mix it with water<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">To make mortar.</i> and to bring it to the row of stones; it is forbidden to leave it there. If another person was injured by it, he is liable. He should not knead on one side [and build on the other]<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Missing in L, added from E, confirmed by the Tosephta.</i> but on the side where he is building.” <sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 11:5" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).11.5">Tosephta 11:5</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 118b" href="/Bava_Metzia.118b">Babli 118b</a>.</i>“A person may unload his stones at his courtyard door in the public domain in order immediately to remove them; it is forbidden to leave them there. If another person was injured by them, he is liable. If the quarryman delivered to the camel driver, the camel driver is liable. The camel driver to the stone-mason, the stone-mason is liable. The stone-mason to the porter, the porter is liable. It it was brought up to scaffolding<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Greek βωμός.</i>, it was wobbly there and fell, the master-mason is liable. If the (stone-mason)<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of L, erroneously copied from the next sentence.</i> [quarryman]<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E, confirmed by the Tosephta.</i> handed it to the (porter)<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of L, erroneously copied from the next sentence.</i> [stone mason]<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E, confirmed by the Tosephta.</i> and somebody was injured either by splinters or the stone, the stone-mason is liable. The stone-mason to the porter and he<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">A third party.</i> was injured by a splinter, the stone-mason is liable. By the stone, the porter is liable. If it was delivered, settled in the row<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Greek δόμος “building; rows of stones or bricks in a building”; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.5.1"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:5, Note 226</a>.</i>, and fell, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was an unforseeable accident.</i>.”

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Two vegetable gardens, one on top of the other<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">One on the slope of a hill, the other in the valley.</i> and vegetables growing between them. Rebbi Meїr says, it is the upper’s<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The owner’s of the upper plot.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, it is the lower’s<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The owner’s of the lower plot.</i>. Said Rebbi Meїr: If the upper’s owner wanted to remove his earth, there would be no vegetables. Said Rebbi Jehudah: If the lower’s wanted to fill up his vegetable garden with earth, there would be no vegetables. Said Rebbi Meїr: Since both of them may object to one another one looks from where these vegetables grow<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The wild growing vegetable in between grows from the water with which the upper plot is irrigated.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, any which the upper’s may take by stretching out his hand are his; the remainder is the lower’s.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Two vegetable gardens,” etc. What shall one do? Ephraim<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 119a" href="/Bava_Metzia.119a">Babli, 119a</a>, he is called “Ephraim the scribe, R. Simeon ben Laqish’s student.” But there, he is quoted as deciding following R. Simeon (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 10:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.10.6.2">Note 69</a>).</i> in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, they shall split<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was not decided whom to follow in practice, it is “money in doubt” and must be split evenly (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.1.1.1">Chapter 1, Note 5</a>).</i>. In the House of Rebbi Yannai they say, up to ten handbreadths<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">They decide following R. Jehudah who has precedence over R. Meïr and R. Simeon (<i>Terumot</i> 3:1, Notes 25,26). The owner of private property owns the rights to its airspace without limitations (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 7" href="/Shabbat.7">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 7a/b</a>), but for any domain not purely private property, the domain only extends ten handbreadths from the ground (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 6:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.6.2.4"><i>Kilaim</i>6:2, Note 31</a>, <i>Šabbat</i> 11:2 13a l. 32; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 7b" href="/Shabbat.7b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 7b</a>). Since the area between the two vegetable gardens is not the private domain of either of them, the domain of the owner of the lower plot cannot extend to more than 10 handbreadths above his ground.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said in the name of Rebbi Yannai: Only he shall not force himself<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">He decides following R. Simeon, following the rule that if a dispute between two authorities in the Mishnah is not resolved, practice follows the third opinion which arbitrates between the two (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 39b" href="/Shabbat.39b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 39b</a>, R. Joḥanan in the name of R. Yannai). He qualifies the right of the owner of the upper plot in that he has a right only to those vegetables which he can reach without undue exertion. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 119a" href="/Bava_Metzia.119a">Babli, 119a</a>, this opinion is quoted in the name of the House of R. Yannai.</i>.
A wall between two partitions<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe first wrote שתי חצירות "two courtyards" and then corrected it to שתי מחיצות "two partitions". The problem to be corrected was that a courtyard usually is a condominium of many home owners dwelling in the same compound, whereas clearly, we are dealing here with only two owners. The corrected text is confirmed by E; the scribe's original text is quoted by <i>Sefer ha'Iṭṭur</i> (Warsaw 1883, vol. 1, part 2, p. 24b Note 14).</i> which was breached. Rav and Samuel, one said, half belongs to one party, the other half to the other party. The other said, both own it entirely. What is the difference between them? If one found a find. For him who said that half belongs to one party, the other half to the other party, if it was on one side it belongs to this party, one the other side it belongs to the other party. For him who said that both own it entirely, the finder acquired it<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Since abandoned property on private grounds automatically belongs to the owner.</i>.
